Your word is a lamp to my feet and a light to my path — Psalm 119:105

Acts — Study Notes

These study notes are opinion, not Gospel. Feel free to disagree.

📖 ▶ means go to the study note for this passage.

◀ 📖 means return to the associated passage in the text.

Our goal is to serve the reader who is coming to this text for the first time with no church background. When in doubt we added a note. It is easy to skip a note you don't need. It is hard to understand a passage without one.

Acts Chapter 1

The Promise of the Holy Spirit

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 1
What Is Happening Here

Luke opens his second book by addressing the same person he addressed in his Gospel — Theophilus. He summarizes what the Gospel covered — everything Jesus began to do and teach until the day he was taken up. The word began is important. The Gospel was what Jesus began. Acts is what Jesus continues to do through the Spirit and the apostles. After the resurrection Jesus appeared to the apostles over forty days giving convincing proofs that he was alive and teaching them about the Kingdom of God. Then he commanded them to stay in Jerusalem and wait for the gift the Father promised — the baptism of the Holy Spirit.

What You Might Not Know

Theophilus — the name means lover of God or friend of God. Luke addressed both his Gospel and Acts to this person. Some scholars think Theophilus was an actual Roman official perhaps a patron who sponsored Luke's writing. Others think the name is symbolic — a stand-in for any reader who loves God. Either reading works. Luke was writing for a specific person and for everyone like that person who wanted to understand what had happened in Jesus and what was continuing to happen through the community he left behind.

Everything Jesus began to do and teach — the word began is the key. Luke is not saying his first book covered everything Jesus did and now he is writing a sequel about something new. He is saying the first book was what Jesus began. The second book is what Jesus continues. The risen Jesus is still the main actor in Acts even when the visible actors are Peter Paul Philip and the others. The Kingdom of God that Jesus preached continues to advance through the Spirit working in the apostles.

Forty days — the number forty carries deep biblical weight. Moses was on Sinai forty days. Israel wandered forty years. Elijah journeyed forty days to Horeb. Jesus fasted in the wilderness forty days before his ministry began. Now the risen Jesus spends forty days with his disciples before his ascension — a final season of teaching and preparation before the Church is launched at Pentecost. The pattern is consistent — forty days of formation before the next major movement of God's work.

Wait for the gift my Father promised — the command is striking. The apostles have just spent forty days with the risen Jesus. They are presumably ready to go and tell everyone what they have seen. Jesus tells them to wait. The mission cannot begin on human enthusiasm or even on eyewitness testimony alone. It requires the Spirit. Power for the mission is not generated by the apostles' readiness. It comes from above and must be waited for.

Why It Still Matters

John baptized with water but in a few days you'll be baptized with the Holy Spirit. The contrast Jesus draws is not between something bad and something good. John's baptism with water was the genuine preparation Israel needed. But it was preparation for something larger. The Spirit's baptism would not replace water baptism — in Acts they will consistently go together — but it would add a new dimension that only the risen and ascended Jesus could give. The waiting the apostles are commanded to do is not passive inaction. It is the active expectation of receiving something that was promised and is about to arrive.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 1

Jesus Taken Up Into Heaven

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 1
What Is Happening Here

The apostles ask Jesus if now is the time he will restore the kingdom to Israel. Jesus tells them it is not for them to know the times the Father has set. But they will receive power when the Holy Spirit comes and they will be his witnesses in Jerusalem in Judea and Samaria and to the ends of the earth. After saying this he is taken up into the sky and a cloud hides him from their sight. Two men in white appear beside them and ask why they are standing looking into the sky. This same Jesus will come back in the same way they saw him go.

What You Might Not Know

Are you going to restore the kingdom to Israel now — the apostles' question reveals they still expected a political restoration. After forty days of teaching about the Kingdom of God they were still asking whether Jesus would now throw off Roman rule and establish Israel as the dominant nation. Jesus does not rebuke them for the question. He redirects it. The timing of what God will do at the end is not their concern. What is their concern is the mission he is giving them right now. The right question is not when will you restore everything but what do you want us to do in the meantime.

You will be my witnesses in Jerusalem in all Judea and Samaria and to the ends of the earth — this sentence is often called the outline of Acts. The book follows exactly this geographic progression. Chapters 1 through 7 — Jerusalem. Chapters 8 through 12 — Judea and Samaria. Chapters 13 through 28 — the ends of the earth culminating in Rome. Jesus did not just give them a mission. He gave them a map. The disciples did not know they were receiving the outline of the book Luke would later write. They were simply receiving their marching orders. Luke looking back saw how perfectly the history matched the commission.

A cloud hid him from their sight — clouds in the Bible are consistently associated with the presence and glory of God. The cloud at Sinai. The cloud filling the Tabernacle. The cloud at the Transfiguration. The cloud that received Jesus at the ascension is not a meteorological detail. It is the glory of God receiving the Son back into the divine presence from which he came. The ascension is not just a departure. It is an enthronement.

This same Jesus who has been taken up from you will come back in the same way — the angels' statement ties together the first coming and the second coming of Jesus. The one who ascended is the one who will return. The manner of his going — visible, physical, witnessed — is the manner of his coming. Not a ghost. Not a metaphor. The same Jesus. That continuity between the Jesus the apostles knew and the Jesus who will return is one of the most consistent claims of the New Testament.

Why It Still Matters

Why do you stand here looking into the sky — the angels' question is gently pointed. The apostles have been given a mission — to be witnesses to the ends of the earth. Standing frozen staring at where Jesus used to be is not that mission. The time for watching has ended. The time for going is about to begin. That temptation — to keep looking back at where we last saw God clearly rather than going forward to where he is sending us — is a temptation the Church has faced in every generation. The angels' correction is the correction every generation needs to hear.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 1

Matthias Chosen to Replace Judas

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 1
What Is Happening Here

The apostles return to Jerusalem and gather in an upstairs room. Luke lists the eleven remaining apostles by name. They devote themselves to prayer along with the women Mary the mother of Jesus and his brothers. Peter stands up before about a hundred twenty believers and addresses the situation of Judas. Scripture had to be fulfilled — the Spirit spoke through David about Judas who had been one of their number. Judas died violently after buying a field with the money he received for betraying Jesus. Peter quotes two psalms that point to replacing Judas's place of leadership. They need someone who has been with them from John's baptism through the ascension who can join them as a witness of the resurrection. They nominate two men — Joseph Barsabbas and Matthias. They pray for the Lord who knows every heart to show them his choice and cast lots. The lot falls to Matthias. He is added to the eleven.

What You Might Not Know

The women and Mary the mother of Jesus and his brothers — this single verse contains several remarkable details. The women who had followed Jesus from Galilee — including Mary Magdalene Mary the mother of James and Joanna — are present in the upper room praying with the apostles. Mary the mother of Jesus is there — the last time she appears in the New Testament. And his brothers are there — the same brothers who did not believe in him during his ministry according to John 7. The resurrection changed them. James the brother of Jesus will become a leader of the Jerusalem church and author of the letter that bears his name. The family of Jesus who once thought he was out of his mind is now gathered in prayer with his disciples.

About a hundred twenty believers — the number is specific. Jewish tradition held that a hundred twenty Jewish men were required to form a community with its own council. Luke may be noting that the followers of Jesus had now reached that threshold — they could be recognized as an organized community in their own right. The church is not yet born at Pentecost but the community that will become the church is already at the legal minimum for formal organization.

The death of Judas — Luke's description of Judas falling headlong his body bursting open and his intestines spilling out reads differently from Matthew 27 which says Judas hanged himself. The two accounts are usually reconciled by suggesting Judas hanged himself and then the body fell — either when the rope broke or when he was eventually taken down — and burst open as a body left dead for some time would. Both accounts agree that Judas died violently and that his money was connected to a field called the Field of Blood. Neither account presents his death as anything other than tragic.

Casting lots — this was the standard Old Testament method for discerning God's will in matters that could not be decided by wisdom alone. The high priest used lots to identify the scapegoat on the Day of Atonement. Joshua used lots to divide the land. Saul was identified as king by lot. The apostles use lots one last time in Acts 1 to choose Matthias. After Pentecost in Chapter 2 the Spirit will lead directly and the practice of casting lots disappears from the narrative. The old method is used one last time before the new way arrives.

Must become a witness with us of his resurrection — Peter's criteria for Judas's replacement are precise. The new apostle must have been with them from John's baptism through the ascension and must be able to testify to the resurrection. Apostleship is not a role anyone can fill. It requires specific qualifying experience. The twelve are eyewitnesses to the whole arc of Jesus's ministry. That is what makes their testimony foundational. Later apostles like Paul will have a different kind of commissioning but the twelve are the specific witnesses of the full earthly ministry.

Why It Still Matters

Lord you know everyone's heart — show us which of these two you've chosen. The apostles do not just cast lots and see what happens. They pray first and they ground the prayer in a theological conviction — the Lord knows every heart. The decision they are about to make is a decision only someone who sees inside people can make well. They are acknowledging their own limitation and inviting God to do what they cannot. That posture — making decisions prayerfully while admitting we cannot see what God sees — is the posture the early church models in this first leadership decision after the resurrection. It is a posture worth keeping.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 1

Acts Chapter 2

The Holy Spirit Comes

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2
What Is Happening Here

The disciples are gathered in one place on the day of Pentecost. Suddenly a sound like a rushing wind fills the house. What looks like tongues of fire spread out and touch each person. Everyone is filled with the Holy Spirit and begins to speak in other languages as the Spirit gives them the ability. This is the moment Jesus told them to wait for — the baptism with the Holy Spirit promised by the Father.

What You Might Not Know

Pentecost — the name comes from the Greek word for fiftieth. The festival fell fifty days after Passover. In Hebrew it was called Shavuot — the Feast of Weeks — one of the three great pilgrimage festivals when Jewish men were required to come to Jerusalem. Pentecost celebrated the wheat harvest and by the first century had also become associated with the giving of the Law to Moses at Mount Sinai. The Spirit coming on Pentecost is not incidental timing. The giving of the Law on Sinai produced a covenant people with God's word written on stone. The giving of the Spirit on Pentecost produces a covenant people with God's word written on hearts — exactly as Jeremiah and Ezekiel had promised.

A sound like the blowing of a violent wind — the Greek word for spirit and the Hebrew word for spirit both also mean breath or wind. The Spirit arrives with the sound that matches his name. The same wind that moved over the waters at creation in Genesis 1. The same breath God breathed into the first man to make him a living being. The same wind Ezekiel called on in the valley of dry bones. The Spirit of God arriving in wind is a pattern older than the Church.

Tongues of fire — fire in the Old Testament consistently signals the presence of God. The burning bush. The pillar of fire in the wilderness. The fire that fell on Elijah's altar. The fire in the Temple at its dedication. The tongues of fire at Pentecost place the presence of God on each individual disciple. The fire that used to settle on the Tabernacle and the Temple now settles on people. The shift is enormous. The sacred space of God's presence has been relocated from buildings to human beings.

They began to speak in other languages — the word is glossa meaning tongues or languages. This is not ecstatic unintelligible speech. The crowd will immediately say they hear their own native languages. This is real human languages given miraculously to people who had not learned them. The miracle at Pentecost is the reverse of Babel in Genesis 11. At Babel human pride led to the confusion of languages and the scattering of people. At Pentecost the Spirit gathers people from every language through the gift of languages. What pride scattered the Spirit reunites.

Why It Still Matters

They were all together in one place. The waiting was communal. The receiving was communal. The mission that launches from Pentecost is communal. The Holy Spirit does not come to individuals in isolation — he comes to a gathered people. Everything that will happen in Acts flows out of this moment of gathered expectation. The Church is not invented by the disciples. It is born by the Spirit descending on a community that was together praying and waiting for what had been promised.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2

The Crowd Is Confused

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2
What Is Happening Here

Devout Jewish men from every nation under heaven are living in Jerusalem. When they hear the sound a crowd gathers. They are confused because each person hears the believers speaking in their own native language. They list the regions they come from — Parthia Media Elam Mesopotamia Judea Cappadocia Pontus Asia Phrygia Pamphylia Egypt Libya near Cyrene Rome Crete and Arabia. They all hear about the great things God has done in their own languages. Some are amazed and ask what it means. Others mock the disciples and say they have had too much wine.

What You Might Not Know

Devout Jewish men from every nation under heaven — the Jewish diaspora had spread across the ancient world since the Babylonian exile centuries earlier. Jews lived in every major city of the Roman Empire and beyond. Many came back to Jerusalem for the great festivals. Some retired and moved back permanently. The list Luke provides covers an enormous geographic range — from Parthia east of the Roman Empire to the western reaches of North Africa and the Italian peninsula. The crowd at Pentecost is a cross-section of the known world gathered at the moment the Spirit arrives.

The list of nations — Parthians Medes and Elamites lived east of the Roman Empire. Mesopotamia Cappadocia Pontus Asia Phrygia and Pamphylia cover the eastern Mediterranean and modern-day Turkey. Egypt and the regions of Libya near Cyrene cover North Africa. Rome represents the west. Crete and Arabia fill out the remaining regions. Luke is making a theological point through geography — the Gospel that begins in Jerusalem will reach all of these places because representatives of all these places heard it first on Pentecost. The outline Jesus gave in Chapter 1 begins its fulfillment here.

They've had too much sweet wine — some of the crowd mocked what they could not explain. Ridicule is often the easier response to something beyond ordinary experience. The mockers were not engaging with what was happening. They were dismissing it. Peter's response will point out the obvious — it is nine o'clock in the morning — but the mockery reveals the pattern that will follow the Spirit's work throughout Acts. Some hear and are moved toward God. Others hear the same thing and laugh.

Why It Still Matters

We all hear them telling about the great things God has done in our own languages. The Spirit does not require people to learn a sacred language to hear the Gospel. He meets every person in the language of their heart. This principle — that the Gospel belongs in every language — has driven Bible translation missionary work and scripture access for two thousand years. The decision at Pentecost was that there is no inner circle of approved speakers. Every language is worthy. Every mother tongue can carry the good news. That decision was made by the Holy Spirit in Jerusalem on the first day of the Church.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2

Peter Speaks to the Crowd

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2
What Is Happening Here

Peter stands up with the eleven and addresses the crowd. He explains that the disciples are not drunk — it is only nine o'clock in the morning. What the crowd is witnessing is what the prophet Joel spoke about. God would pour out his Spirit on all people in the last days. Sons and daughters would prophesy. Young men would see visions. Old men would dream dreams. Even servants — men and women — would receive the Spirit. Wonders in the sky and signs on the earth would announce the great day of the Lord. Everyone who calls on the name of the Lord will be saved.

What You Might Not Know

Peter stood up with the eleven — this detail is important. The same Peter who denied Jesus three times in the courtyard of the high priest a few weeks earlier now stands up publicly and preaches to thousands. The restoration on the beach in John 21 has already begun to produce its fruit. Fear has been replaced by boldness. The transformation is not of Peter's own making. The Spirit that just arrived has taken a man who could not stand up to a servant girl and made him a public witness to the resurrection in front of Jerusalem.

In the last days — Peter applies Joel's phrase to the present moment. The last days have begun. This does not mean the end of the world is immediate. It means that a new era in God's dealing with the world has been inaugurated — the era that stretches from the Spirit's outpouring to the return of Jesus. The entire Church age is the last days. We are still in them. The signs Peter describes — wonders in the sky, signs on the earth — find partial fulfillment at Pentecost and ultimate fulfillment at Jesus's return. The prophecy spans the whole era.

Your sons and daughters will prophesy — the gift of the Spirit in Joel's prophecy is explicitly not limited by gender or age or social class. Sons and daughters. Young men and old men. Male and female servants. The democratization of the Spirit is built into the original prophecy. What used to be reserved for specific prophets and kings is now available to everyone God calls. Peter's decision to quote the full passage including sons and daughters and male and female servants is not incidental. The Church born at Pentecost includes all of them from the first day.

Everyone who calls on the name of the Lord will be saved — this line from Joel will become the foundation for one of the most important statements in the New Testament. Paul quotes it in Romans 10 to argue that the Gospel is for Jew and Gentile alike — everyone who calls on the name of the Lord. The word everyone does not have qualifications. It does not specify ethnic background or religious pedigree or moral record. Calling on the name of the Lord is the one requirement. Peter places this line at the center of his first sermon. It is the anchor everything else hangs from.

Why It Still Matters

These people aren't drunk as you think — it's only nine o'clock in the morning. Peter's first public sentence in the Christian era is half-apologetic and half-humorous. He is not pretending the scene does not look strange. He is acknowledging the strangeness and offering a different explanation. The early Church never tried to make itself look respectable to the world. It acknowledged the oddness of what was happening and pointed to the Scripture that explained it. That combination — honest about the appearance, grounded in Scripture for the meaning — is the template Peter establishes for Christian witness on the first day.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2

Peter Proclaims Jesus as Lord and Messiah

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2
What Is Happening Here

Peter declares that Jesus of Nazareth was approved by God through miracles wonders and signs. This Jesus was handed over according to God's set plan and foreknowledge and killed by being nailed to a cross. God raised him up because death could not hold him. Peter quotes Psalm 16 where David spoke of not being abandoned to the grave. David himself died and is buried — his tomb is still in Jerusalem. But David was a prophet and was speaking about the resurrection of the Messiah. God raised Jesus and the apostles are witnesses. Jesus has been lifted up to the right hand of God and has poured out the Spirit. Peter quotes Psalm 110 — the Lord said to my Lord sit at my right hand. He concludes — God has made this Jesus whom you crucified both Lord and Messiah.

What You Might Not Know

Handed over by God's set plan and foreknowledge with the help of lawless men — Peter holds two things together that seem in tension. The crucifixion happened by God's plan. It also happened through human sin and responsibility. Peter does not choose between these. He states both as true. The sovereignty of God over the crucifixion does not eliminate human responsibility for it. The human responsibility does not make God's plan any less sovereign. Both are real. Both are affirmed. The early church does not resolve this tension and does not need to. It preaches both.

David's tomb is still with us today — Peter's argument is a piece of careful exegesis. Psalm 16 spoke of not being abandoned to the grave and not seeing decay. But David did die. His tomb is a known location in Jerusalem that the crowd could visit. Therefore the psalm cannot ultimately be about David. It must be about someone else — someone who really did not see decay because he was raised from the dead. That person is Jesus. Peter is teaching his audience how to read their own Scriptures in light of the resurrection. This method will be used throughout Acts.

Sit at my right hand until I make your enemies a footstool for your feet — Psalm 110 was recognized in first century Judaism as a messianic psalm. The question was always who David's Lord was. David was king — who could David call Lord? Peter's answer is that David was speaking prophetically about the Messiah who is Lord over David himself. Jesus has been raised to the right hand of the Father — the position of highest honor and authority. The psalm the crowd had sung for centuries finds its true subject in Jesus.

Lord and Messiah — Peter's conclusion uses both titles. Messiah is the Jewish category — the anointed one promised throughout the Hebrew Scriptures. Lord is the broader category — the title used for God himself in the Greek translation of the Old Testament. Peter is making a claim about Jesus's identity that has both a Jewish frame and a universal frame. He is the fulfillment of specifically Jewish expectations and he is Lord over everything. Both are true simultaneously.

Whom you crucified — Peter does not soften the charge. The audience that includes many of the same people who were in Jerusalem fifty days earlier when Jesus was killed hears this directly. You crucified him. God raised him. The one you killed is the Lord. Peter does not blame Rome or the religious authorities in general. He addresses the responsibility of the crowd in front of him. This directness is part of what produces the response in the next section.

Why It Still Matters

God has made this Jesus whom you crucified both Lord and Messiah. The sentence structure matters. God made him — the action is God's. Lord and Messiah — the identities are permanent. Whom you crucified — the human act of killing him did not unmake what God had made. The crucifixion looked like the world's verdict on Jesus. The resurrection revealed God's verdict. God's verdict is the one that stands. Jesus is Lord. Jesus is Messiah. The crucifixion did not end that identity — it was the means by which it was fully revealed.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2

Three Thousand Are Baptized

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2
What Is Happening Here

The crowd is deeply troubled when they hear Peter's message. They ask what they should do. Peter tells them to turn away from their sins and be baptized in the name of Jesus the Messiah so their sins can be forgiven. They will then receive the gift of the Holy Spirit. The promise is for them their children and all who are far away — everyone the Lord calls. Peter continues pleading with them to save themselves from this crooked generation. Those who accept the message are baptized. About three thousand people are added to their number that day.

What You Might Not Know

They were deeply troubled in their hearts — the Greek phrase is katenugesan ten kardian — literally pierced through in the heart. The word for pierced is related to the word used for puncturing or stabbing. Peter's preaching did not produce polite interest. It produced a wound. The audience felt their hearts had been cut open. That reaction is the Spirit's work through the word — conviction that does not let the hearer walk away unchanged. The question they ask — what should we do — is the question of people who have just been shown the truth about themselves and cannot unsee it.

Turn away from your sins — the Greek word is metanoeo meaning to change one's mind or turn around. It involves more than emotional regret. It is a fundamental reorientation of direction. The crowd had just been told they crucified the Messiah. Turning involved admitting that judgment was correct and turning toward the one they had rejected. Peter does not ask for religious enthusiasm or theological agreement. He asks for a change of direction.

Baptized in the name of Jesus the Messiah — baptism in the name of Jesus was a public declaration of allegiance. For Jewish people already familiar with ritual washings this specific baptism — in the name of the one the religious authorities had just executed — was a costly public identification with a condemned man. It could cost social standing employment family relationships. The three thousand who were baptized that day counted the cost and chose to go under the water publicly associated with Jesus.

The promise is for you for your children and for all who are far away — Peter extends the scope of the promise in three concentric circles. Those present. Their descendants. Those far away. The phrase all who are far away echoes Isaiah 57 where God promises peace to those near and those far away. Peter is signaling that the Gospel is not limited to those who heard it in Jerusalem or to Jewish people only. The Gentile mission is already implicit in his sermon even before he fully understands what that will mean.

About three thousand people were added to their number that day — the number is striking. On Pentecost the Church went from roughly a hundred twenty believers to over three thousand in a single day. This would not have been a quiet baptism service. Jerusalem's public water sources — the Pool of Siloam and various mikvot around the Temple — would have been needed to accommodate the numbers. The scene must have been visible to much of the city. The Church did not emerge privately. It emerged publicly on a festival day in the capital in front of thousands of witnesses.

Why It Still Matters

Save yourselves from this crooked generation. Peter's final plea frames the decision as an urgent rescue. The generation the crowd is part of — a generation that crucified the Messiah — is heading somewhere, and Peter is asking them to step off the path. The call is not to join a nicer version of the existing religious establishment. It is to separate from a generation heading toward judgment and join a new community being formed by the Spirit. Salvation in Peter's sermon is not just personal. It involves choosing a different people to belong to.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2

The Believers Share Life Together

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2
What Is Happening Here

The believers spend their time learning from the apostles and sharing life together. They eat meals together and pray. A sense of awe comes over everyone. Many wonders and signs are done through the apostles. The believers stay together and share everything they have. They sell property and possessions to give to anyone in need. Every day they meet in the Temple courts and eat together in their homes with happy and sincere hearts. They praise God and are respected by all the people. Every day the Lord adds to their number those who are being saved.

What You Might Not Know

They devoted themselves to the apostles' teaching — the first activity Luke lists for the newborn church is learning. Three thousand new believers did not go immediately into mission or organizing or activism. They devoted themselves to being taught by people who had been with Jesus. The foundation of the church from day one was the accurate transmission of what Jesus said and did from eyewitnesses who had heard and seen it. Everything else the church would do depended on this.

Shared life together — the Greek word is koinonia meaning fellowship or participation or partnership. It describes not just friendly association but genuine sharing of life. The koinonia of the early church was not a program. It was a quality of mutual involvement that affected every area of their lives — meals possessions time worship. They were not just members of the same organization. They were participants in each other's lives.

They sold their property and possessions to give to anyone who was in need — this description has prompted debate throughout Christian history. Was this a mandatory practice or a voluntary response to an unusual situation? Acts 5 will show Ananias and Sapphira being rebuked not for failing to sell their property but for lying about what they sold. Peter explicitly says the money was theirs to do with as they chose. The sharing in Acts 2 was voluntary but it was real. People with property did sell it to meet needs. The community made sure no one was in need. How that shaped Christian practice across the centuries is a long conversation. That it happened is beyond dispute.

Every day in the Temple courts and in their homes — the early church worshiped in two locations simultaneously. The Temple courts were the large public space where thousands could gather for teaching and prayer. The homes were the small intimate settings where meals were shared and community was formed. Both were necessary. Neither was sufficient alone. The rhythm of large gathering and small gathering has been the rhythm of the church ever since.

Every day the Lord added those who were being saved to their group — the growth of the church is attributed to the Lord not to the apostles' strategies. Luke does not say the apostles grew the church. He says the Lord added to the church. The apostles taught. The community shared. The signs happened. But the growth itself was the Lord's work. That theological attribution runs throughout Acts. The Church is not a human enterprise that God blesses. It is God's work in which humans participate.

Why It Still Matters

They praised God and were respected by all the people. The earliest description of the Jerusalem church's public reputation is not that they were feared or hated or isolated. They were respected. A community that worshiped God devoted itself to teaching shared its resources with those in need and welcomed new members every day had something the broader population recognized as good. The opposition from the religious authorities will come in the next chapter. But in these earliest days the ordinary people of Jerusalem looked at the church and saw something worth respecting. That public witness — a community whose life itself commends the Gospel — is what Acts shows at its beginning and keeps pointing back to throughout.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 2

Acts Chapter 3

A Lame Man Is Healed

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3
What Is Happening Here

Peter and John are going up to the Temple for the three o'clock prayer. A man unable to walk since birth is being carried to the Temple gate called Beautiful where he is placed every day to beg from those entering the Temple courts. He asks Peter and John for money. Peter looks directly at him and tells him to look at them. The man gives them his attention expecting money. Peter says he has no silver or gold but will give what he has — in the name of Jesus the Messiah of Nazareth get up and walk. Peter takes him by the right hand and helps him up. Instantly his feet and ankles become strong. He jumps up and walks into the Temple with them walking jumping and praising God.

What You Might Not Know

The three o'clock afternoon prayer — devout Jews observed three set times for prayer each day. Morning at nine. Midday at noon. Afternoon at three. The three o'clock hour was associated with the evening sacrifice in the Temple — the moment when the lamb was offered for the sins of the people. Peter and John are going to pray at the very hour Jesus died on the cross. The new covenant community continues to honor the rhythms of Jewish worship while now understanding them as fulfilled in Jesus.

The Beautiful Gate — historical sources suggest this was likely the Nicanor Gate, an enormous bronze gate on the eastern side of the Court of Women — the most heavily trafficked entrance for ordinary worshipers. It was made of Corinthian bronze and was reputed to be so beautiful and heavy it took twenty men to open. A man begging at this gate was positioned to receive maximum exposure to worshipers entering the Temple. He was a familiar figure — everyone knew him — which is why his healing immediately produces such a strong public response.

I don't have any silver or gold but I'll give you what I do have — Peter's response is one of the most quoted lines in Acts. He acknowledges what he lacks before declaring what he has. The early Church was not financially wealthy. The apostles did not draw salaries or carry treasuries. What they had was access to the name of Jesus and the power of the Spirit. Peter does not pretend to have what he does not have. He offers what he does have honestly. That distinction — being clear about your resources and not pretending — is part of why the Church in Acts has integrity.

In the name of Jesus the Messiah of Nazareth — the name of Jesus is invoked deliberately. Peter does not say in my name or by my power. He attributes the healing to the name of Jesus from the start. The healing that follows is not Peter's miracle. It is Jesus's miracle performed through Peter. The point will be made even more explicitly in Peter's sermon that follows.

Walking jumping and praising God — the description has the ring of eyewitness memory. The man does not just walk politely into the Temple. He jumps. He cannot contain what has just happened to him. The court of the Temple that he had been carried past every day for years he now enters on his own legs leaping. The praise is the first response. The questions and the explanations come later. The praise comes immediately.

Why It Still Matters

Look at us — Peter said. The man gave them his full attention expecting to receive something. The healing started with attention. Peter did not throw a coin and keep walking. He stopped and required the man to look at him. The man's expectation was wrong — he was looking for money — but the act of attention itself opened the moment. Sometimes the first thing the Lord asks of us is just to stop and look at what is in front of us with full attention. Whatever happens after that begins with that turning of the eyes.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3

The Crowd Gathers in Amazement

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3
What Is Happening Here

The people see the man walking and praising God and recognize him as the same man who used to sit and beg at the Beautiful Gate. They are filled with wonder and completely amazed at what has happened. The man holds onto Peter and John as the people come running to them at Solomon's Porch.

What You Might Not Know

They recognized him as the same man — this detail matters. The crowd knew this beggar. He had been a fixture at the Temple gate for years — Acts 4 will note he was over forty years old. There was no question about his identity or his condition. Everyone in Jerusalem who attended the Temple regularly knew who he was. His healing could not be dismissed as mistaken identity or a coincidental recovery. The visibility of the before-state made the after-state undeniable.

Solomon's Porch — also called Solomon's Colonnade — was a large covered walkway on the eastern side of the Temple complex. It was named after Solomon though the actual structure was Herod's. It was a popular gathering place for teaching and discussion — Jesus had walked there himself in John 10. Peter chose this location because it could accommodate a large crowd and because its association with Solomon's wisdom made it a natural place for a teacher to address an audience. The location is not incidental — it is deliberate.

The man held onto Peter and John — the detail is touching. The newly healed man does not run off to celebrate alone. He clings to the men through whom God just touched his life. That instinct — to stay close to those who have been the means of God's grace to us — is a healthy instinct. The man's gratitude expressed itself as physical attachment to those who had stopped for him at the gate.

Why It Still Matters

The healing produced a crowd. The crowd produced a sermon. The sermon will produce thousands more believers. The chain of events shows the pattern of how God's work multiplies. A single act of compassion at a Temple gate becomes the occasion for thousands to hear the Gospel. The man Peter healed could never have known how far the ripples of his healing would reach. The work God does in one person is rarely just for that one person. It is meant to spread.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3

Peter Speaks to the Crowd

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3
What Is Happening Here

Peter asks the crowd why they are surprised and why they stare at him and John as if they made the man walk by their own power. The God of Abraham Isaac and Jacob has glorified his servant Jesus. The crowd handed Jesus over to be killed. They rejected him before Pilate even though Pilate had decided to let him go. They rejected the Holy and Righteous One and asked for a murderer to be released instead. They killed the author of life but God raised him from the dead. The apostles are witnesses. It is faith in the name of Jesus that has given this man complete healing in front of them all. Peter acknowledges they and their leaders acted in ignorance. But this is how God fulfilled what he had predicted through all the prophets — that his Messiah would suffer.

What You Might Not Know

Why does this surprise you — Peter's first move is to redirect attention away from himself and John. He refuses to let the crowd treat the apostles as miracle workers with personal power. The healing was not produced by their holiness or their technique. The crowd's instinct to credit the apostles is the instinct Peter immediately corrects. Throughout Acts the apostles consistently refuse credit for what God does through them. That refusal is part of what keeps the early Church healthy.

His servant Jesus — the word for servant is pais which can mean servant or child. It deliberately echoes the suffering servant passages in Isaiah 42 49 50 and 53. Peter is identifying Jesus with the figure Isaiah described — the servant of the Lord who would suffer for the sins of the people and be vindicated by God. This identification of Jesus with the Isaiah servant becomes a foundational interpretive key in early Christian preaching.

You killed the author of life — the Greek word archegos can mean author originator or pioneer. It carries the sense of the one who initiates and leads. Peter's rhetorical move is devastating in its compression. The author of life — the one through whom all life comes — was killed by the very people he gives life to. The contradiction is the point. The crowd handed over the source of life and the universe did not collapse only because God raised him up. The crime was unspeakable. The grace that responded to it was greater than the crime.

It's faith that comes through Jesus that has given this man complete healing — Peter is careful to credit not just the name of Jesus but the faith that comes through Jesus. The healing is mediated through faith — though notably the lame man had not asked for healing. He had asked for money. The faith involved is Peter's faith in the name of Jesus. The early Church understood that faith does not always have to originate with the one being healed. Sometimes it is exercised on behalf of someone else.

You and your leaders acted in ignorance — Peter's tone here is striking. After accusing the crowd of killing the author of life he immediately offers them ignorance as mitigating context. They did not fully know what they were doing. This is the same theological framework Jesus himself articulated from the cross — Father forgive them for they do not know what they are doing. Peter extends the same framework. The crime was real. The ignorance was also real. Both are acknowledged. Repentance is still possible.

Why It Still Matters

This is how God fulfilled what he had predicted through all the prophets — that his Messiah would suffer. Peter is teaching the crowd a new way to read their own Scriptures. The Messiah they had expected was a victorious king. The prophets — Peter argues — had actually predicted a suffering Messiah all along. The crowd had not seen it because they were looking for the wrong thing. The crucifixion was not the failure of God's plan. It was the fulfillment of it. That radical reinterpretation of the Hebrew Scriptures in light of Jesus is one of the early Church's most important theological accomplishments.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3

A Call to Turn Back to God

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3
What Is Happening Here

Peter calls the crowd to turn away from their sins and turn to God so their sins can be wiped away. Then times of rest will come from the Lord. He will send the Messiah Jesus who has been appointed for them. Jesus must remain in heaven until the time comes for God to restore everything as he promised through the prophets. Moses said the Lord will raise up a prophet like Moses from among the people — anyone who does not listen to that prophet will be cut off. All the prophets from Samuel onward have spoken about these days. The crowd are the heirs of the prophets and of the covenant God made with their ancestors. God promised Abraham that through his offspring all the families of the earth would be blessed. God raised up his servant and sent him first to them — to bless them by turning each of them from their sinful ways.

What You Might Not Know

So your sins may be wiped away — the Greek word for wiped away is exaleipho which literally means to smear out or erase. It was used for wiping a written record clean — removing the ink from a document so that no trace remained. Peter is not promising that sins will be partially forgiven or covered over. He is promising that the record will be erased — wiped clean as if it had never been written. That is a more thorough forgiveness than partial pardon. The slate becomes blank.

Times of rest will come from the Lord — the Greek phrase kairoi anapsuxeos can be translated times of refreshing or seasons of rest. The image is of relief from heat or exhaustion — the cool breath that follows a long hot day. Peter is promising that turning to God produces refreshment. The exhaustion of carrying sin and trying to manage it ourselves is replaced by the rest that comes from being forgiven. This is not just future eschatological rest. It is rest that begins now in the experience of the wiped-clean conscience.

He must remain in heaven until the time comes for God to restore everything — Peter introduces a tension the rest of the New Testament will explore. Jesus has come and accomplished salvation but has now ascended. He will return to restore everything. In the meantime his people live in the gap — between the first coming and the second. The kingdom has begun but is not complete. The salvation is real but the restoration is still ahead. Christians have lived in this in-between time for two thousand years.

A prophet like me from among your own people — Peter quotes Deuteronomy 18 where Moses promised God would raise up a prophet like himself. First-century Judaism had developed a specific expectation around this promise — there would be a Prophet like Moses in the messianic age. Peter applies this directly to Jesus. Jesus is the Prophet Moses promised. The warning attached — anyone who does not listen will be cut off — is given its full weight. Refusing to listen to the Prophet God sent is not a neutral position. It has consequences.

Through your offspring all the families of the earth will be blessed — Peter quotes the promise to Abraham from Genesis 12 and 22. The promise was always universal. Abraham was chosen so that all the families of the earth could be blessed. The Jewish people were not the destination of God's plan — they were the channel through which the blessing would reach everyone. Peter is reminding the crowd that the universal scope of the Gospel is not a departure from their tradition. It is the fulfillment of the original promise that defined their tradition.

He sent him first to you — Peter notes that Jesus was sent first to Israel. The order matters. The Gospel goes to Israel first and then through Israel to the nations. This pattern will be evident throughout Acts — Paul consistently goes to the synagogue first when he enters a new city before turning to the Gentiles. The first does not mean the only. It means the starting point. The blessing was meant to flow outward from Israel to all the families of the earth.

Why It Still Matters

He did this to bless you by turning each of you away from your sinful ways. Peter ends the sermon with a definition of what blessing actually means. The blessing God sent through Jesus is not primarily prosperity protection or pleasant circumstances. It is being turned away from sin. The greatest gift God can give a person is the power to stop walking in directions that lead away from him. Everything else flows from that. Peter does not promise the crowd that turning to Jesus will make their lives easier in the worldly sense. He promises something better — they will be turned away from the sin that was destroying them.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 3

Acts Chapter 4

Peter and John Are Arrested

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4
What Is Happening Here

While Peter and John are still speaking the priests the captain of the Temple guard and the Sadducees come up to them. They are upset because the apostles are teaching the people and announcing that because of Jesus there is a resurrection from the dead. They arrest Peter and John and put them in jail until the next morning since it is already evening. But many who heard the message believe. The number of men who believe grows to about five thousand.

What You Might Not Know

The Sadducees — this religious party controlled the priesthood and the Temple. Unlike the Pharisees they did not believe in the resurrection of the dead. They accepted only the first five books of Moses as fully authoritative and rejected the developing tradition of resurrection theology that the prophets and the later writings supported. Peter and John preaching the resurrection of Jesus directly attacked the central theological position of the Sadducees. The arrest was not just political — it was theological. The apostles were claiming that the doctrine the Sadducees had been denying for generations had just been demonstrated in history.

The captain of the Temple guard — this was a high-ranking position second only to the high priest in Temple administration. The captain commanded the Levite guards who maintained order in the Temple complex. The arrest of Peter and John was conducted with the full weight of Temple authority. This was not a minor disciplinary matter. The religious establishment was treating the apostles as a serious public order problem.

The number of men who believed grew to about five thousand — Luke specifies men in the count which probably means adult Jewish men. The total community including women and children would have been substantially larger. From a hundred twenty believers before Pentecost the community has grown to over five thousand men plus families in just weeks. The opposition was not slowing the growth — it was happening alongside explosive growth. The arrest of the apostles did not stop the work. It seemed only to accelerate it.

Put them in jail until the next morning since it was already evening — Jewish law generally prohibited trying capital cases at night. The apostles were held overnight not to extend their suffering but because the Sanhedrin could not legally convene until morning. The procedural formality was being followed. The legal machinery that had condemned Jesus was now being applied to his apostles within months of his execution. Peter and John would have understood exactly what kind of trial they were heading toward.

Why It Still Matters

But many of those who heard the message believed it. The arrest happened mid-sermon. Peter never finished. He was hauled off to jail before he could deliver his closing remarks. And yet the message had already done its work — many believed before the arrest stopped the preaching. The growth of the early Church did not depend on perfect circumstances or completed presentations. The word planted began to grow even when the planter was being silenced. The arrest of the messengers could not stop the message that had already been heard.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4

Peter Speaks Before the Council

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4
What Is Happening Here

The next day the rulers elders and teachers of the law meet in Jerusalem. Annas the high priest is there along with Caiaphas John Alexander and others from the high priest's family. They bring Peter and John before them and ask by what power or in what name they did this. Peter filled with the Holy Spirit answers. If they are being questioned about a kind act done to a man who could not walk and how he was healed then the rulers and all Israel should know — it is by the name of Jesus the Messiah of Nazareth that this man stands before them completely healed. This is the same Jesus they crucified and whom God raised from the dead. Jesus is the stone the builders rejected which has become the most important cornerstone. There is no salvation in anyone else — no other name under heaven given to people by which they must be saved.

What You Might Not Know

Annas Caiaphas John and Alexander — this is the same religious establishment that had condemned Jesus a few months earlier. Annas the former high priest still wielded enormous influence and was the patriarch of the family that controlled the priesthood. Caiaphas his son-in-law was the current high priest. Peter and John were standing before the same court that had condemned their Lord — and the irony was not lost on anyone. The very men who had said it was better that one man should die for the people were now facing two of that man's followers who would not stop talking about him.

By what power or in what name did you do this — the Sanhedrin's question revealed their assumption. They believed in supernatural power and they recognized that something beyond the natural had happened. Their question was not whether the healing was real. It was whose authority had produced it. In the ancient world miracles could be attributed to legitimate divine power or to demonic power. The Sanhedrin was asking Peter and John to identify which kind of power they had used.

Peter filled with the Holy Spirit — Jesus had promised the disciples in Luke 12 that when they were brought before authorities the Holy Spirit would teach them what to say. Peter is now experiencing the fulfillment of that promise. The fisherman who had denied Jesus three times in the courtyard of the high priest is now standing before the high priest himself preaching boldly. The Spirit that filled him is the source of the boldness. The change in Peter between Luke 22 and Acts 4 is the change the Spirit produces.

The stone the builders rejected has become the cornerstone — Peter quotes Psalm 118. The builders are the religious establishment in front of him. The rejected stone is Jesus. The cornerstone is the most important stone in any structure — the one that determines the alignment of everything else. Peter is telling the men who rejected Jesus that they were the builders who threw away the stone everything depends on. The audacity of saying this to the men who could order his execution is breathtaking. Peter does not soften it. He delivers it plainly.

There is no other name under heaven given to people by which we must be saved — this is one of the most exclusive claims in the New Testament and one that has shaped Christian theology and missions for two thousand years. Peter is not arguing that Jesus is one path among many to God. He is claiming that there is no other path. The healing of the lame man is presented as a small visible sign of the larger reality — the only name with the power to heal completely is the only name with the power to save completely. The exclusivity is not invented. It is built into the foundation of apostolic preaching from the beginning.

Why It Still Matters

It is by the name of Jesus the Messiah of Nazareth that this man stands before you completely healed. Peter does not let the question stay vague. He names the name. The same name they wanted suppressed is the name he announces in their faces. The early Church refused to make Jesus generic. They named him specifically — Jesus the Messiah of Nazareth — even when naming him publicly was the very thing that produced the danger. The boldness was not abstract. It was the boldness to say the actual name out loud in front of the people who hated it.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4

The Council Is Stumped

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4
What Is Happening Here

The council is amazed at the boldness of Peter and John. They realize these are ordinary men with no special training and recognize them as men who had been with Jesus. The healed man is standing right there with them so the council has nothing to say. They order Peter and John out of the room and discuss what to do. Everyone in Jerusalem knows about the remarkable miracle and they cannot deny it. To keep it from spreading they decide to warn the apostles never to speak in this name again. They call them back and command them not to speak or teach at all in the name of Jesus. Peter and John answer — which is right in God's eyes, to listen to you or to listen to God? They cannot stop speaking about what they have seen and heard. The council threatens them further and lets them go because all the people are praising God for what happened. The man who was healed was over forty years old.

What You Might Not Know

Ordinary men with no special training — the Greek phrase agrammatoi kai idiotai literally means unlettered and unprofessional. It does not mean Peter and John were illiterate or stupid. It means they had not been formally trained in the rabbinic schools. They were not credentialed scholars. The Sanhedrin recognized that the boldness and theological depth of Peter's response was not what they expected from a fisherman from Galilee. The implication was clear — these men had a source of teaching that was not the rabbinic system. They had been with Jesus.

They recognized them as men who had been with Jesus — the Greek word for recognized is epeginoskon meaning to know thoroughly or recognize as familiar. Some council members had probably seen Peter and John during Jesus's earlier visits to Jerusalem. The recognition is not just intellectual identification. The council saw something of Jesus in his disciples. The time spent with Jesus had marked them. That mark was visible to people who had known Jesus themselves even when they were enemies.

Which is right in God's eyes to listen to you or to listen to God — Peter's response establishes one of the foundational principles of Christian conscience. When the command of human authority directly contradicts the command of God the believer must obey God. This is not a recipe for general lawlessness. The early Church was generally law-abiding and respectful of authority. But when authority demanded silence about Jesus the line was drawn. The line has been drawn at the same place by Christians under hostile authority for two thousand years.

We can't stop speaking about what we've seen and heard — Peter does not claim a right to speak. He claims an inability to stop. The witnesses cannot unsee what they saw. The Gospel is not just a teaching they were assigned to share. It is a reality they experienced personally — they saw Jesus alive after his death. Telling other people about it is not optional. It is the natural overflow of having seen what they saw.

The man who was miraculously healed was over forty years old — this detail at the end of the section adds weight to everything that came before. The healing was not of a recent injury or a temporary condition. The man had been unable to walk for over forty years. His healing was complete and instantaneous after a lifetime of disability. The council could not deny it because the evidence was over forty years deep. The undeniability of the miracle is what tied the council's hands.

Why It Still Matters

They couldn't find a way to punish them because all the people were praising God for what had happened. The council's hands were tied not by the apostles' theological arguments but by the public response. The people of Jerusalem were on the side of the apostles because they had seen the miracle and heard the message and recognized the work of God. The protection of the early Church in this chapter came not from political power but from the genuine faith and praise of ordinary people. When the people of God praise God for what God has done the powers that oppose his work find themselves with fewer options. That is a kind of protection worth noticing.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4

The Believers Pray Together

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4
What Is Happening Here

Peter and John return to their own people and report what the leading priests and elders said. The believers all pray together to God. They address God as the Master who made the sky earth sea and everything in them. He spoke through David — why do the nations rage and the people plot in vain. The kings and rulers gather against the Lord and his Messiah. Herod and Pilate met together with the Gentile nations and the people of Israel to plot against Jesus whom God anointed. They did what God's power and will had already decided should happen. The believers ask God to listen to the threats and help them speak the word with great boldness — to reach out his hand to heal and perform signs and wonders through the name of Jesus. After they pray the place is shaken. They are all filled with the Holy Spirit and keep on speaking the word of God boldly.

What You Might Not Know

They all prayed together to God — the Greek word is homothumadon meaning with one mind or with one accord. Luke uses this word repeatedly in Acts to describe the unified prayer and action of the early Church. The prayer that follows was not led by one person while others listened. It was prayed together — perhaps with one person voicing words the whole community affirmed. The unity of the community at prayer is the precondition for the power that follows.

Sovereign Lord — the Greek word despotes is stronger than the usual word for Lord. It carries the sense of absolute master with complete authority. The believers begin their prayer by addressing God as the absolute Sovereign over everything. This framing matters because they are about to ask God to act in a situation where powerful human authorities have just opposed them. They start by establishing in prayer the truth that the human authorities are not actually in control. The Sovereign is.

Why do the nations rage — the believers quote Psalm 2 which describes the kings and rulers of the earth gathering against the Lord and his Messiah. They apply this directly to their current situation. Herod and Pilate the Gentiles and the Jewish leaders all gathered against Jesus exactly as the psalm predicted. The believers are reading their present circumstance through the lens of Scripture. The opposition they face is not unprecedented or surprising. It was foretold. That recognition strengthens rather than weakens their faith.

They did what your power and your will had already decided should happen — the believers hold the same theological tension Peter held in Chapter 2. The crucifixion happened by God's plan and through human responsibility. Both are affirmed. The opposition the believers now face is also held within God's sovereignty. They do not pray for the opposition to disappear. They pray for boldness to keep speaking in the middle of it.

Help us your servants to speak your word with great boldness — note what they ask for. Not protection from the council. Not the silencing of the opposition. Not even the conversion of the rulers. They ask for boldness — the courage to keep doing the very thing that got them arrested. The prayer is not for circumstances to change. It is for the believers to be strengthened to face the circumstances faithfully.

The place where they were meeting was shaken — the physical shaking of the meeting place is a sign of God's powerful answer. The same kind of physical phenomenon happened at Sinai and at Pentecost. God answers the prayer for boldness with a tangible demonstration of his presence. They are filled with the Spirit again — a second filling for a new moment of need. The first filling at Pentecost was not a one-time event. The Spirit fills again as new boldness is needed for new situations.

Why It Still Matters

They were all filled with the Holy Spirit and kept on speaking the word of God boldly. The prayer was answered specifically as it was prayed. They asked for boldness. They received boldness. They asked to keep speaking. They kept speaking. The pattern of prayer and answered prayer in Acts is direct and verifiable. The early Church prayed for what they actually needed and God gave them what they asked for. That kind of focused prayer — asking for the specific thing the situation requires — is something the Church learned in its earliest crisis and never lost.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4

The Believers Share Everything

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4
What Is Happening Here

All the believers are one in heart and mind. No one claims that any of their possessions are their own. They share everything. With great power the apostles continue to give witness to the resurrection of the Lord Jesus. God's great grace is on all of them. There are no needy people among them. Those who own land or houses sell them from time to time bring the money and give it to the apostles. The money is then given to anyone who has a need.

What You Might Not Know

One in heart and mind — the Greek phrase is mia kardia kai psyche — one heart and one soul. It echoes the great commandment from Deuteronomy 6 — love the Lord your God with all your heart and all your soul. The community that loves God with all its heart and soul also loves one another with the same heart and soul. The unity is not organizational uniformity. It is a depth of shared life that touches the inner reality of each person.

No one claimed that any of their possessions were their own — this is a statement about attitude not about legal ownership. Acts 5 will make clear that property still legally belonged to its owners. What changed was the attitude — the conviction that what I have is not really mine alone, it is for the community when there is need. The shift was internal first. The external sharing followed from the internal reorientation.

There were no needy people among them — Luke states this as accomplished fact. The Jerusalem church had eliminated need within its own community. This is a remarkable claim. The early Church did what no government and few communities have ever done — they made sure no one in their fellowship was in need. The mechanism was simple. Those with resources sold them when others needed help. The redistribution was voluntary, ongoing, and effective.

From time to time those who owned land or houses sold them — the Greek tense suggests this was a continuing practice not a one-time event. As needs arose those with assets liquidated them to meet the needs. The community had a culture in which holding onto wealth while others lacked basics was not acceptable. The selling was not a religious requirement. It was the natural overflow of being one in heart and mind with people who had nothing.

And gave it to the apostles — the money was given to the apostles who then distributed it. This created a clear point of accountability. The apostles managed the distribution and ensured it reached those who needed it. As Acts 6 will show this responsibility eventually grew too large for the apostles alone and additional leaders were appointed. But the initial pattern of trust — bringing resources to the apostles for distribution — established a model of community-managed care that did not depend on each individual giver finding the right recipient.

Why It Still Matters

God's great grace was on all of them. The grace is described as great and as resting on the whole community. The grace was not only the forgiveness each person received individually. It was the grace that produced the kind of community in which no one was in need. The grace shaped the economics. It shaped the relationships. It produced a community that was not just spiritually transformed but materially organized differently. That is what grace looks like when it actually arrives in a community — it changes how money and possessions are held and distributed. The Church has never fully recovered the Acts 4 pattern. But the pattern stands as a witness to what grace can do when it is allowed to do its full work.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4

Barnabas Sets an Example

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4
What Is Happening Here

Joseph a Levite from Cyprus is given an example. The apostles call him Barnabas — Son of Encouragement. He sells a field he owns brings the money and gives it to the apostles.

What You Might Not Know

Joseph a Levite from Cyprus — Joseph was a Levite which meant his tribe was traditionally not supposed to own land in Israel. The Levites were given cities and pasturelands but were dependent on the offerings of the other tribes for their livelihood. By the first century however many Levites had acquired property especially in the diaspora. Joseph being from Cyprus suggests he had inherited or acquired land outside Israel. His sale of the field is an act of voluntary generosity from a man whose tribe was historically the recipient of others' generosity.

The apostles called him Barnabas which means Son of Encouragement — the apostles gave him a new name based on his character. Encouragement — the Greek word paraklesis is the same word used for the work of the Holy Spirit as the Paraclete. Joseph's encouragement of the church reflected something of the Spirit's own work of comfort and strengthening. He earned the name through what he was like. Throughout the rest of Acts he will live up to it — encouraging Paul, defending John Mark, holding communities together. He becomes one of the most important figures in the early mission.

He sold a field he owned — the specific mention of one field he owned is contrasted in the next chapter with Ananias and Sapphira who sold property and lied about the price. Barnabas sold and gave honestly. Ananias sold and pretended. Luke deliberately introduces Barnabas's example just before the negative example of Ananias to set up the contrast. The same generosity could be done in two completely different spirits. Barnabas's spirit is the one to follow.

Why It Still Matters

Barnabas appears in this chapter only briefly but he becomes one of the most important figures in the rest of Acts. He is the one who will vouch for Saul when no one else trusts him. He is the one who will travel with Paul on the first missionary journey. He is the one who will advocate for John Mark when Paul refuses to take him. The man whose first appearance in Acts is selling a field and giving the money turns out to be the man who keeps the early mission together at multiple critical points. The character that produced the generosity in Acts 4 is the same character that produces the encouragement that holds the church together in the chapters ahead. Generosity and encouragement come from the same root.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 4

Acts Chapter 5

Ananias and Sapphira

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5
What Is Happening Here

A man named Ananias with his wife Sapphira sells some property. With his wife's full knowledge he keeps back part of the money for himself and brings the rest to the apostles. Peter confronts him — why has Satan filled his heart to lie to the Holy Spirit. The land was his before he sold it. The money was his to use as he wished. He has not lied to men but to God. Ananias falls down and dies. Young men wrap him and bury him. About three hours later his wife comes in not knowing what happened. Peter asks her if the price she names is the actual price. She says yes. Peter asks how they could agree to test the Spirit of the Lord. The men who buried her husband are at the door and will carry her out. She falls at his feet and dies. The young men carry her out and bury her beside her husband. Great fear sweeps over the whole assembly and everyone who hears about these things.

What You Might Not Know

This story is one of the most disturbing in Acts and one of the most important for understanding the early Church. Luke places it immediately after Barnabas's honest generosity in Chapter 4. The contrast is deliberate. Barnabas sold a field and gave the money openly. Ananias and Sapphira sold property and kept some of the money while pretending they had given all of it. The sin was not keeping some of the money — Peter explicitly says the money was theirs to use. The sin was the lie about what they were giving.

Why has Satan filled your heart — Peter attributes the deception to Satan entering Ananias's heart. This echoes the description of Judas in Luke 22 and John 13 where Satan entered him to produce the betrayal. The early Church took spiritual reality seriously. Sin was not just a human choice. It could be the expression of deeper spiritual forces at work. This does not remove Ananias's responsibility — Peter asks him why he decided to do it — but it names what was happening in spiritual terms.

The land was yours before you sold it. After it was sold the money was yours to use as you wished — this is one of the most important verses in Acts for understanding the sharing described in Chapter 4. The sharing was not compulsory. Private property still existed. Giving was voluntary. What Ananias and Sapphira lied about was not how much they owed — they owed nothing. They lied about how generous they were choosing to be. They wanted the reputation of total generosity without the cost of total generosity.

You haven't lied to men — you've lied to God — Peter identifies the Holy Spirit with God directly. Lying to the Spirit is lying to God. This is one of many passages in the New Testament that treats the Holy Spirit as fully divine. The Spirit is not a lesser force or a divine attribute. The Spirit is God. Ananias and Sapphira did not realize who they were actually deceiving. Peter tells them.

Why were these two struck dead — readers for two thousand years have wondered. The severity seems disproportionate to the sin. Several observations matter. The early Church was at a formative moment. The pattern of integrity between inner spirit and outward action was being established. A community in which people could lie to the Holy Spirit and face no consequences would have been corrupted from the inside. God acted to preserve the integrity of the newborn community. The judgment also demonstrates that the same God who poured out grace at Pentecost is not indifferent to hypocrisy within the community. Grace does not mean the absence of holiness. The early Church learned both at once.

Great fear swept over the whole assembly — the word translated fear is phobos. It can mean terror or reverent awe depending on context. Luke uses it twice in this passage — once for the believers and once for everyone who heard. The fear is not paranoia. It is the recognition that the Holy Spirit is really present and that this community is not a club to be manipulated. The seriousness of what the apostles were leading is understood in a way it had not been before.

Why It Still Matters

The story of Ananias and Sapphira is not comfortable and should not be softened. It reveals something important about the community God was building. The early Church was not a place where image could be managed independently of reality. What you said you were giving had to be what you were actually giving. What you claimed to be had to be what you actually were. The community could absorb sin that was confessed. It could not absorb deception that was performed. That standard has been the witness of the Church at its best ever since — a place where honesty about what you actually are matters more than the appearance of what you want to be seen as.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5

Signs and Wonders Among the People

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5
What Is Happening Here

The apostles perform many signs and wonders among the people. The believers meet together at Solomon's Porch. No one else dares to join them even though the people speak highly of them. More and more people — crowds of men and women — believe in the Lord and are added. People bring the sick into the streets and lay them on beds and mats hoping that as Peter walks by at least his shadow might fall on some of them. Crowds also gather from the towns around Jerusalem bringing those who are sick or troubled by evil spirits — and every one of them is healed.

What You Might Not Know

No one else dared to join them — the phrase has puzzled readers because the next sentence says more and more people believed. The two statements are not contradictory. The immediate aftermath of the Ananias and Sapphira incident produced a kind of reverent distance. Casual hangers-on kept their distance. People who wanted the benefits of being associated with the movement without genuine commitment backed off. What remained and grew was genuine faith. The filtering produced a community that was smaller in its casual edges but larger in its committed center.

The people spoke highly of them — even while some were too intimidated to join the believers had a strong positive public reputation. The Ananias incident did not make the Church feared in a negative sense. It made the community respected. A group that takes its own integrity seriously earns respect even from those who keep their distance. That public reputation will be important when opposition comes — the council in the next section cannot simply eliminate the apostles because the people are on their side.

Hoping that as Peter walked by at least his shadow might fall on some of them — this detail is striking. People were positioning sick relatives in the street where Peter would pass hoping for healing by proximity to his shadow. Whether the shadow itself carried power or whether the people's faith expressed in this way became the occasion for healing Luke does not specify. He simply reports what happened. The reference to Peter's shadow is echoed later in Acts when handkerchiefs and aprons that had touched Paul's skin are reported to heal the sick. God sometimes worked through unusual means as an accommodation to people's faith. Neither Peter nor Paul made shadows or handkerchiefs into a system. But the stories are preserved to show the extravagance of God's response to expectant faith.

Crowds also gathered from the towns around Jerusalem — the ministry was no longer confined to the city. People from the surrounding region were bringing the sick to Jerusalem for healing. The reach of the apostolic ministry was expanding geographically even before any organized mission began. The Jerusalem church was becoming a magnet for people across Judea who needed what only God could give.

Every one of them was healed — the statement is universal. Everyone brought was healed. No one went home still sick. This level of consistent healing is unusual even in Acts. The season Luke describes in this passage was an extraordinary moment of concentrated divine confirmation of the apostolic message. The healings were not random. They were signs pointing to the truth of what was being preached. The same God who healed through Jesus was now healing through his apostles and everyone was noticing.

Why It Still Matters

More and more people — crowds of both men and women — believed in the Lord and were added to their number. Luke specifies men and women. The growth of the Church was not confined to one gender or one social category. Women believed and were counted in the numbers. This reflects the transforming social reality of the early Church. In a culture where women were often not counted at all Luke counts them. Both men and women were being added. That pattern continues wherever the Gospel is preached faithfully.
