The Gospel does not stay in one demographic. It draws from all of them.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5

The Apostles Are Arrested Again

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5
What Is Happening Here

The high priest and his associates the Sadducees are filled with jealousy. They arrest the apostles and put them in the public jail. During the night an angel of the Lord opens the jail doors and leads them out. The angel tells them to go stand in the Temple courts and tell the people the full message of this new life. Early the next morning they enter the Temple courts and begin to teach. The high priest and his associates arrive and call together the full council sending word to the jail to bring the apostles. The officers arrive and find no one inside even though the jail is securely locked and the guards are standing at the doors. When the captain of the Temple guard and the leading priests hear this they are baffled. Someone reports — the men you put in jail are standing in the Temple courts teaching the people. The captain goes with his officers and brings the apostles back. They do not use force because they are afraid the people would stone them.

What You Might Not Know

Filled with jealousy — the Greek word is zelos which can mean zeal or jealousy. The religious authorities were not primarily motivated by theological concern. They were jealous. The apostles were drawing the crowds they believed should be coming to them. The Temple was losing its monopoly on religious authority in Jerusalem to a group of unordained former fishermen. The wound was to their position not to their theology. Luke's diagnosis of their motive is unsparing.

An angel of the Lord opened the doors of the jail — this is the first of three jailbreak stories in Acts. Peter will be released again in Chapter 12 after Herod arrests him. Paul and Silas will experience a jailbreak in Chapter 16 after being imprisoned in Philippi. God demonstrates repeatedly in Acts that human authorities cannot hold his messengers when he wants them released. This does not mean every imprisoned Christian will be miraculously freed — many weren't. Stephen will be killed in Chapter 7 and James in Chapter 12. But when God's purpose required it prison walls proved no obstacle.

Go stand in the Temple courts and tell the people the full message of this new life — the angel's instructions are striking. Released from prison the apostles are sent back to the very place where they had been preaching when they were arrested. The release is not an escape — it is a re-commissioning. Keep doing what got you imprisoned. That command reframes the jailbreak. God did not free them for their safety. He freed them for their mission.

We found the jail securely locked with the guards standing at the doors but when we opened them we found no one inside — the officers' report is almost comic in its precision. Every external indicator suggested the prisoners should still be there. Only when they opened the doors did they discover what the reader already knows. The scene captures the impotence of human authority when God has decided to move.

They didn't use force because they were afraid the people would stone them — the captain and officers had to handle the apostles gently. The public was on the apostles' side. The captain had the legal authority to arrest. He did not have the popular authority to manhandle. The gap between legal power and actual power is one of the recurring dynamics in Acts — the authorities can arrest the apostles but they cannot silence them and they cannot mistreat them without risking a public backlash.

Why It Still Matters

The full message of this new life — the angel describes the Gospel this way. Not a religion. Not a doctrine. A new life. What the apostles were offering was not a modified version of Judaism or a set of beliefs to subscribe to. It was entrance into a new kind of life — life beyond death, life filled with the Spirit, life in a community that shared everything, life connected to the risen Jesus. The angel's phrase captures something the early Church understood — they were not recruiting members. They were inviting people into a different way of being alive altogether.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5

Peter Stands Firm Before the Council

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5
What Is Happening Here

The apostles are brought in and made to stand before the council. The high priest questions them. He had given strict orders not to teach in this name. Yet they have filled Jerusalem with their teaching and are determined to make the council responsible for this man's death. Peter and the other apostles reply — they must obey God rather than human beings. The God of their ancestors raised Jesus from the dead — the one the council killed by hanging him on a cross. God lifted him up to his own right hand as Prince and Savior so that Israel could turn from sin and have their sins forgiven. They are witnesses of these things — and so is the Holy Spirit whom God has given to those who obey him.

What You Might Not Know

Are determined to make us responsible for this man's death — the high priest's complaint is revealing. The council was not concerned that Jesus had been wrongly executed. They were concerned that the apostles kept saying he was. The issue for the council was reputation management. They had handed Jesus over to be crucified and they wanted that story to die. The apostles kept telling it. The charge against the apostles is not primarily theological. It is that they are damaging the council's public standing.

We must obey God rather than human beings — Peter gives the same answer he gave in Chapter 4 only more concise and stronger. This single sentence has become one of the foundational principles of Christian conscience. When civil or religious authority commands what God forbids or forbids what God commands the Christian must obey God. The principle has been invoked by martyrs from Stephen onward, by reformers, by abolitionists, by Christians under totalitarian regimes. It is not a license for general defiance of authority. It is a line drawn at the point where authority commands disobedience to God.

The one you killed by hanging him on a cross — Peter uses the phrase hanging him on a cross which echoes Deuteronomy 21 where anyone hung on a tree was described as cursed by God. Peter is not softening the horror of the crucifixion by calling it a hanging. He is invoking the Scripture that described such a death as accursed — and then declaring that God raised that accursed one up to his own right hand. The reversal is complete. The one the Law would have called cursed is the one God has exalted.

Prince and Savior — the Greek word for Prince is archegos — the same word used in Acts 3 for the author of life. It means originator, founder, or pioneer. Savior is soter — deliverer or rescuer. Jesus is both the pioneer who opens the way and the rescuer who brings people through it. The combination captures both his leadership and his saving action. He is not just one or the other. He leads by rescuing and rescues by leading.

So that Israel could turn from sin and have their sins forgiven — Peter states the purpose of Jesus's exaltation directly to the council. God raised and exalted Jesus to make repentance and forgiveness available to Israel. The very council that had handed Jesus over to death is being told that his death and resurrection make forgiveness possible for them. The offer is extended even to them. The apostles are not just defending themselves. They are preaching the Gospel to their judges.

We are witnesses of these things and so is the Holy Spirit — Peter pairs the apostles' human testimony with the Spirit's divine testimony. The apostles witness what they have seen. The Spirit witnesses through the signs and wonders and transformed lives that accompany the message. The two testimonies together are meant to be compelling. To reject them is to reject both human eyewitness and divine confirmation.

Why It Still Matters

The Holy Spirit whom God has given to those who obey him. Peter's phrase connects obedience and the Spirit. God gives the Spirit to those who obey him. This is not a transactional statement — obedience earns the Spirit. It is a description of reality — the Spirit is given to those who have turned toward God in obedience rather than those who have turned away. The obedience Peter describes is not a list of religious observances. It is the turning toward God that the Gospel calls for. The Spirit comes to people who are responding to God not people who are resisting him.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5

Gamaliel's Warning

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5
What Is Happening Here

The council is furious and wants to put the apostles to death. But a Pharisee named Gamaliel — a teacher of the law respected by all the people — stands up and orders the men taken outside for a little while. He addresses the council. Think carefully. Some time ago Theudas appeared claiming to be somebody important and about four hundred men joined him. He was killed, his followers were scattered, and it came to nothing. After him Judas the Galilean appeared in the days of the census and led a band of people in revolt. He was killed, his followers were scattered. Gamaliel's advice — leave these men alone. Let them go. If what they are doing is of human origin it will fail on its own. If it is from God they cannot stop it — and they will find themselves fighting against God. The council is persuaded. They call the apostles back, have them whipped, order them not to speak in the name of Jesus, and let them go.

What You Might Not Know

Gamaliel — this is Rabban Gamaliel the Elder, one of the most respected Jewish teachers of the first century. He was the grandson of the great rabbi Hillel and led the Pharisee party in the Sanhedrin. Jewish tradition remembers him so highly that it says when he died the glory of the Law ceased. The apostle Paul had been one of his students according to Acts 22. His intervention here is significant — the most respected teacher in Jerusalem is cautioning the council against rash action.

Theudas and Judas the Galilean — Gamaliel cites two earlier messianic movements that had failed. There is a historical difficulty. Josephus mentions a Theudas but dates his uprising to around 44 AD which is after Gamaliel's speech around 33 AD. Either there was an earlier Theudas Luke knew about that Josephus did not mention, or there is a historical compression in Luke's account. Judas the Galilean led a revolt around 6 AD at the time of the Quirinius census. Both movements failed completely. Gamaliel cites them as precedents — failed messianic movements eventually die on their own.

If it's from God you won't be able to stop them — Gamaliel's logic is pragmatic and theological at the same time. Movements that are merely human collapse. Movements that are from God cannot be stopped by human opposition. The council does not need to eliminate the apostles. Time will settle the question. If the movement is false it will fail. If it is genuine opposition is not just pointless but dangerous — fighting against God.

The council was persuaded — Gamaliel's argument prevailed. The apostles were not executed. But Luke's account does not endorse Gamaliel's reasoning as flawless. The principle that successful movements must be from God is not theologically sound — many harmful movements have succeeded for centuries before collapsing. And many genuinely godly movements have failed in their time. Gamaliel's advice saved the apostles' lives in this moment but it was not the last word on how to discern truth from falsehood. It was pragmatic wisdom that happened to produce the right outcome on this occasion.

Had them whipped — the council compromised. They did not kill the apostles but they did punish them. The flogging was a serious matter — likely thirty-nine lashes which was the maximum Jewish law allowed. The punishment served as an official warning and as an attempt to frighten the apostles into silence. It did neither. The apostles left the council rejoicing.

Why It Still Matters

You'll only find yourselves fighting against God. Gamaliel's warning to the council is one of the most quoted cautions in Christian history. When opposing a movement ask whether the movement might be from God before committing to destroy it. The warning is not a license for religious neutrality — there are movements that genuinely should be opposed. But it is a caution against the self-assurance that we already know which side God is on. The Sanhedrin was sure they knew. Gamaliel suggested they might not. History has borne him out. The movement they were about to crush turned out to be the one God himself was leading.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5

The Apostles Rejoice

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5
What Is Happening Here

The apostles leave the council rejoicing because they have been counted worthy of suffering disgrace for the name of Jesus. Day after day in the Temple courts and from house to house they never stop teaching and proclaiming the good news that Jesus is the Messiah.

What You Might Not Know

Rejoicing because they'd been counted worthy of suffering disgrace for the name of Jesus — this is the first time in Acts the apostles suffer physical punishment for preaching Jesus. Their response is not resentment fear or retreat. It is joy. The flogging was not something to endure bitterly. It was something to count as an honor. Being considered worthy of suffering for the name was understood as participation in what Jesus himself experienced. The early Church's theology of suffering — that sharing in Christ's sufferings is a privilege not a tragedy — is present here from the first experience of persecution.

The phrase counted worthy is significant. The apostles understood the suffering as something they had been given — something bestowed rather than merely inflicted. The council thought they were punishing the apostles. The apostles experienced the punishment as an honor granted by God who considered them worthy to carry something for his name. The reframing does not pretend the physical pain was not real. It names what the pain meant.

Day after day in the Temple courts and from house to house — the two venues of apostolic ministry from Chapter 2 continue here. The Temple courts for public preaching — the house churches for community formation. The flogging did not change the pattern. It confirmed it. What they had been doing they continued doing, in the same places, with increased boldness rather than decreased.

They never stopped teaching and proclaiming the good news that Jesus is the Messiah — the two verbs teaching and proclaiming cover both the instruction of existing believers and the announcement of the Gospel to those who had not heard. Both activities were ongoing every day. The ministry had multiple dimensions — deepening those who believed and inviting those who did not — and both continued alongside each other. A healthy church has never done only one. The apostles did both without ceasing.

Why It Still Matters

The apostles left the council rejoicing. The chapter that began with death in the church community and moved through miraculous healings prison breaks and interrogation ends with men who have just been whipped walking away rejoicing. The picture is almost absurd. Anyone observing them would have expected them to be angry or frightened or at least subdued. Instead they were joyful. The joy was not a performance. It was the natural expression of people who had understood what was really happening. They were not victims of religious persecution. They were participants in something Jesus himself had experienced. That understanding turns suffering into something that can be received with joy rather than merely endured with bitterness. It is one of the most distinctive marks of the Church at its best.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 5

Acts Chapter 6

Seven Men Are Chosen to Serve

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 6
What Is Happening Here

As the number of disciples grows the Greek-speaking Jews begin to complain about the Hebrew-speaking Jews. Their widows are being overlooked in the daily distribution of food. The twelve apostles call the whole group together. It is not right for them to give up preaching the word of God to wait on tables. The disciples should choose seven men from among themselves who are respected and full of the Spirit and wisdom. The apostles will put them in charge of this duty so they can give their full attention to prayer and teaching. The whole group is pleased. They choose Stephen a man full of faith and the Holy Spirit. They also choose Philip Prochorus Nicanor Timon Parmenas and Nicolas from Antioch who had joined the Jewish faith. They present these men to the apostles who pray and lay hands on them. The word of God continues to spread. The number of disciples grows rapidly and a large group of priests also becomes obedient to the faith.

What You Might Not Know

The Greek-speaking Jews and the Hebrew-speaking Jews — the first-century Jerusalem church contained two distinct cultural groups within the same faith. The Hebrews spoke Aramaic and Hebrew and were typically native to Judea. The Hellenists spoke Greek and were often Jews who had returned from the diaspora — born or raised in Greek-speaking cities across the Roman Empire. They shared faith in Jesus but came from different cultural worlds. Language, food customs, and social networks differed. The tension that emerged was not surprising. It was the first instance of what would become a recurring issue — how the Gospel holds together people from genuinely different backgrounds.

Their widows were being overlooked in the daily distribution of food — the complaint was specific and serious. Widows in the ancient world had few means of support. The early Church had taken responsibility for feeding them daily. But the system was breaking down along cultural lines. The Greek-speaking widows were being missed. Whether this was intentional discrimination or administrative oversight is not stated. What matters is that the problem was real, it was acknowledged openly, and it required attention.

It isn't right for us to give up preaching the word of God to wait on tables — the apostles' response has sometimes been read as a dismissive attitude toward practical ministry. That reading misses the point. The apostles are not saying food distribution is beneath them. They are saying different ministries require different people. They have been called to a specific work — prayer and teaching the word. If they abandon that work to personally administer meal distribution both ministries will suffer. The solution is not for the apostles to do everything. The solution is to identify other gifted leaders for other essential work.

Seven men from among you who are known to be respected full of the Spirit and wisdom — the qualifications are striking. The men handling food distribution had to be Spirit-filled and wise. This was not a low-level task assigned to spiritual beginners. Distributing food fairly in a community with cultural tensions required spiritual maturity and wisdom. The early Church understood that what looks like practical ministry is actually deeply spiritual work when it involves caring for people's real needs across cultural lines.

The seven names — all seven are Greek names. This was almost certainly intentional. A problem affecting the Greek-speaking community was to be solved by leaders from that community. The Hebrew-speaking majority entrusted the distribution to Hellenists. This was an act of remarkable wisdom. Rather than insisting on their own people overseeing the distribution — which would have looked like protecting the dominant group's interests — they gave the responsibility entirely to leaders from the offended community. That move almost certainly prevented the split and restored trust.

Nicolas from Antioch who had joined the Jewish faith — Nicolas is identified as a Gentile convert to Judaism. Among the seven leaders chosen to solve an intra-Jewish conflict is a Gentile background convert. The early Church already included Gentiles who had joined through Judaism. Within a few chapters Antioch itself will become the first major Gentile church. Nicolas being on this list is a small foreshadowing of the larger expansion to come.

A large group of priests also became obedient to the faith — this detail is easily missed but significant. Priests who had served in the Temple system were believing in Jesus. These were men trained in the Torah, experienced in the Temple liturgy, and deeply embedded in the religious establishment. Their conversion was costly — likely ending their priestly careers and their social standing. Their willingness to pay that cost is a testimony to how compelling the witness of the early Church was even within the religious establishment that opposed it.

Why It Still Matters

This idea pleased the whole group. The solution to the cultural tension was proposed by the apostles and then embraced by the whole community. No one was forced into it. Everyone saw the wisdom of it and agreed. That pattern — leaders proposing, the whole community affirming, then implementation following — is one of the healthiest models of church decision-making visible in Acts. The apostles did not simply decree the solution. They proposed it and waited for the community's assent. That combination of clear leadership and genuine community buy-in is worth preserving wherever it can be found.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 6

Stephen Is Opposed and Arrested

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 6
What Is Happening Here

Stephen full of God's grace and power performs amazing wonders and signs among the people. But opposition arises from members of the Synagogue of the Freedmen. These are Jews from Cyrene and Alexandria along with others from Cilicia and Asia. They argue with Stephen but cannot stand up against the wisdom and the Spirit by which he speaks. So they secretly persuade some men to say they heard Stephen speak blasphemous words against Moses and God. This stirs up the people the elders and the teachers of the law. They grab Stephen and bring him before the high council. They bring in false witnesses who say Stephen never stops speaking against the holy place and the law. They say he claims Jesus of Nazareth will destroy the Temple and change the customs Moses handed down.

What You Might Not Know

Stephen full of God's grace and power — Stephen was introduced in the first section as one of the seven chosen for food distribution. He immediately becomes more than that. He performs wonders and signs — the same language used for the apostles. His ministry expands rapidly beyond his assigned task. This is not a criticism. The apostles' decision to delegate administrative work freed not only their own time but also revealed the fuller gifts of the men they appointed. Stephen's preaching ministry would probably never have emerged if he had remained a man known only for serving widows. His Spirit-filled character produced both faithful service to widows and prophetic preaching to synagogues.

The Synagogue of the Freedmen — this was a Jerusalem synagogue associated with Jews who had been freed from slavery, likely descendants of Jewish captives who had been taken to Rome and later emancipated. The synagogue also drew members from Jewish communities in Cyrene, Alexandria, Cilicia and Asia — the Hellenistic diaspora. Stephen himself was a Hellenist. The opposition to him came from within his own cultural community. Saul of Tarsus who will appear in the next chapter was from Cilicia. It is likely that Saul was among those who argued with Stephen and failed to counter him. The man who became Paul almost certainly first crossed paths with Christian preaching in these debates he could not win.

They couldn't stand up against the wisdom and the Spirit by which he spoke — this echoes Jesus's promise in Luke 21 that his followers would be given words and wisdom their opponents could not refute. Stephen was the first clear instance in Acts of this promise being fulfilled. The Hellenistic Jews who debated him were themselves educated. They were not ignorant opponents. They lost the debates because Stephen's arguments came with a spiritual authority they could not match.

They secretly persuaded some men to say — the opposition moved from honest debate to manufactured testimony. When they could not win the argument they fabricated charges. The Greek word for secretly persuaded suggests bribery or manipulation. Luke is clear — the witnesses were false witnesses, procured by those who had lost the argument through legitimate means.

He will destroy this place and change the customs Moses handed down — the charges against Stephen are similar to the charges brought against Jesus at his trial. Both were accused of speaking against the Temple and against Moses. The parallel is probably not accidental. Luke is showing that the pattern of Jesus's trial is being repeated in the trial of his follower. What happened to the Master was going to happen to the servant. Stephen was following his Lord not only in preaching but in suffering the same charges.

The charges contained a grain of truth twisted. Jesus had predicted the destruction of the Temple. He had taught a new relationship to the Law that fulfilled rather than replaced Moses. Stephen's sermon in the next chapter will indeed argue that God is not confined to the Temple. The false witnesses were not inventing doctrines from thin air — they were distorting actual teaching into accusations designed to outrage the audience.

Why It Still Matters

They couldn't stand up against the wisdom and the Spirit by which he spoke. Stephen's opponents were not defeated by rhetorical cleverness or political maneuvering. They were defeated by wisdom joined with the Spirit. That combination is still the shape of Christian witness at its best. Arguments alone do not win hearts. The Spirit alone without wisdom can become unfocused emotion. The two together — wisdom that is Spirit-empowered — carries an authority that cannot be matched by opposition however educated or motivated. Stephen had both.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 6

Stephen's Face Shines Like an Angel

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 6
What Is Happening Here

Everyone sitting in the council looks intently at Stephen and they see that his face looks like the face of an angel.

What You Might Not Know

His face looked like the face of an angel — this detail is one of the most visually arresting moments in Acts. Luke does not explain what the radiance looked like physically. He simply reports what the council saw. The man on trial for his life had a face that shone with something the council members could only describe as angelic. The detail echoes Moses coming down from Mount Sinai in Exodus 34 with his face shining from being in God's presence. Stephen's face shining while standing before the religious establishment is a visual sign that he is standing in God's presence even while they think they are judging him.

Everyone sitting in the council looked intently at him — the council was paying close attention. They were preparing to condemn him. Instead they were seeing something that complicated their judgment. The shining face was not something Stephen produced or performed. It was something that happened to him that everyone else observed. The council's own eyes were telling them something their minds were not yet ready to accept — that the man in front of them was vindicated by God even before he spoke.

The reference to an angel's face — in the biblical tradition angels appear in glory reflecting the presence of God they come from. A face like an angel's face is a face that reflects divine presence. The council members would have understood exactly what they were seeing even if they did not want to admit it. Stephen was not just defending himself. He was about to speak from a position of divine attestation that should have stopped the trial altogether. It didn't. But it was given — as a witness to them and to the reader.

Why It Still Matters

This single sentence ends Chapter 6 and sets up Chapter 7. The sermon that follows will be the longest recorded sermon in Acts. Stephen is about to preach his last words and then be stoned to death. Luke gives us one image before that happens — his face shining like an angel. The detail matters because it tells us how to read the sermon that follows. This is not an ordinary trial defense. It is a prophetic witness from a man whose face is already reflecting the glory he is about to enter. The first Christian martyr began his final speech with a face already touched by the world he was going toward. Everything he said next should be read in that light.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 6

Acts Chapter 7

Stephen Speaks to the Council

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

The high priest asks Stephen if the charges are true. Stephen begins his defense not by addressing the charges directly but by retelling the story of Israel. The God of glory appeared to Abraham in Mesopotamia before he lived in Haran. God told him to leave his country and his relatives and go to the land God would show him.

What You Might Not Know

Brothers and fathers listen to me — Stephen opens with the standard respectful address of a Jewish man speaking to Jewish elders. He is not starting hostile. He is claiming his place as one of them — a brother in Israel addressing his fathers in the faith. The sermon that follows will turn prophetic and end in confrontation. But Stephen begins inside the family, not outside it.

The God of glory appeared to our ancestor Abraham — Stephen's first move is theological. He calls God the God of glory — the same phrase used in Psalm 29. The God he is about to describe is not a local Temple deity. He is the glorious God whose presence has been on the move since Abraham. Stephen is laying the groundwork for his eventual point that God was never confined to one building in Jerusalem.

While he was still in Mesopotamia before he lived in Haran — Stephen notes a detail that will become important. God appeared to Abraham before he ever reached the Promised Land. The revelation of God to Abraham happened outside the land, outside any temple, in Mesopotamia. This is the first example in the sermon of God meeting his people in unexpected places. It will not be the last.

Leave your country and your relatives and go to the land I'll show you — Stephen quotes Genesis 12. The original call to Abraham was a call to leave what was familiar and go where God would lead. The God who called Abraham was not tied to Abraham's homeland. He called him out of it. This pattern — God calling his people out of settled familiarity into uncertainty — runs through the whole sermon.

Why It Still Matters

Stephen does not start by defending himself. He starts by telling the story of God. That is a profound pastoral move. When accused of blasphemy he retells the tradition his accusers claim to defend. He is going to show them that their own story, read honestly, points in a direction they have not yet been willing to go. The defense is not primarily about Stephen. It is about the God who has been at work since Abraham and is still at work now — and whether the council can recognize what he is doing.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Abraham and the Promise

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

Abraham leaves the Chaldeans and settles in Haran. After his father dies God brings him to the land where the council now lives. But God gives him no inheritance there — not even a square foot of ground. God promises to give the land to his descendants even though Abraham has no child. God tells him his descendants will be strangers in a foreign country and enslaved and mistreated for four hundred years. But God will punish the nation that enslaves them and afterward they will come out and worship God in this place. Then God gives Abraham the covenant of circumcision. Abraham becomes the father of Isaac and circumcises him eight days after his birth. Isaac becomes the father of Jacob and Jacob becomes the father of the twelve patriarchs.

What You Might Not Know

God gave him no inheritance here — not even a square foot of ground — Stephen emphasizes a remarkable detail. Abraham lived in the Promised Land but never owned any of it. He was a resident alien in the very land God had promised him. The only piece of ground Abraham ever owned in Canaan was the burial cave at Machpelah he bought from Ephron the Hittite. The man who received the promise of the land died without ever possessing more than a grave in it. That is Stephen's point. The promise was bigger than real estate, and God was always bigger than any specific location.

Abraham had no child when God promised him descendants — the promise was made to a childless elderly man. The promise defied the evidence of his circumstances. God's promises often operate this way in Scripture. The inheritance is promised before the heir exists. The land is promised before the nation exists. The Messiah is promised before Israel is a people. God works by promise — attaching future realities to present empty situations. Abraham believed before he saw. That is the faith Stephen is about to say his own generation has forgotten.

Four hundred years of slavery in a foreign country — God told Abraham in advance that his descendants would spend centuries as slaves. The promise included the suffering. God did not hide the hard part. This matters for Stephen's argument. God's faithfulness is shown not in keeping his people from suffering but in bringing them through it. The same God who promised the blessing also promised the slavery before the deliverance. The council listening to Stephen has been inside the story they already know. Stephen is reminding them that it has always worked this way.

The covenant of circumcision — circumcision was given to Abraham as the sign of the covenant before the Law was given to Moses. Stephen mentions this in passing but it is an important theological marker. The people of God were marked as God's own before they had the Law. The covenant preceded the Torah. This sets up Stephen's eventual argument that what God is now doing in Jesus is not a break from God's way of working — it is continuous with a pattern that preceded even Moses.

Jacob became the father of the twelve patriarchs — the story is moving toward Joseph and then toward Moses. But Stephen pauses here to name the twelve patriarchs as a group. He is tracing the line of God's faithful promise from one generation to the next. The tradition the council claims to defend is built on these specific names. Stephen will now show that the tradition itself contains the pattern of rejection they are about to repeat.

Why It Still Matters

Abraham lived in the Promised Land as a stranger. He owned nothing. He saw very little of what was promised in his own lifetime. Yet he believed God and oriented his life toward what was promised. Stephen's sermon begins with a man who held onto God's promise without receiving most of its fulfillment. That picture of faithful life — holding onto what God has said without needing to see it all completed — will become the quiet norm of Christian existence through every generation since. We are still Abraham's children in that sense. We live by promise more than by sight.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Joseph Is Sold Into Egypt

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

The patriarchs are jealous of Joseph and sell him as a slave into Egypt. But God is with him. He rescues Joseph from all his troubles and gives him favor and wisdom before Pharaoh. Pharaoh puts him in charge of Egypt and his entire household. A famine strikes Egypt and Canaan. Jacob sends his sons to Egypt for grain. On the second visit Joseph reveals himself to his brothers and Pharaoh learns about Joseph's family. Joseph sends for Jacob and his whole family — seventy-five people in all. Jacob goes down to Egypt where he and the ancestors die. Their bodies are brought back to Shechem and placed in the tomb Abraham had bought from the sons of Hamor.

What You Might Not Know

The patriarchs were jealous of Joseph — Stephen names it directly. The patriarchs — the revered founding fathers of the tribes of Israel — were jealous of their brother and sold him into slavery. Stephen is not slandering the patriarchs. He is reading the Scripture honestly. The tradition the council holds sacred contains the story of favored brothers rejecting the brother God had chosen. That pattern is the pattern Stephen is about to reveal has repeated itself in his own generation.

But God was with him — the reversal is captured in a phrase. The brothers rejected Joseph. God was with him. Human rejection did not negate divine favor. Joseph went from a pit to a prison to the second highest position in Egypt — and from there he became the instrument through whom his rejecting brothers were saved from famine. The one they rejected became the one through whom they were delivered. Stephen is telling this story to a council that has handed over the one through whom they might be delivered.

Seventy-five people in all — the Hebrew text of Genesis 46 says seventy went down to Egypt. The Greek Septuagint — which Stephen is likely quoting — says seventy-five, probably counting additional descendants of Joseph who were already in Egypt. Stephen is using the Greek translation familiar to his Hellenistic Jewish audience. This small detail is worth noting because it shows Stephen was thoroughly grounded in the Greek Scriptures that would have shaped his synagogue community.

Their bodies were brought back to Shechem — this detail involves a historical question. Jacob was buried at Machpelah in Hebron according to Genesis 50. Joseph's bones were brought up from Egypt and buried at Shechem according to Joshua 24. Stephen seems to combine the traditions, perhaps following a later Jewish tradition that placed the bones of the other patriarchs at Shechem as well. The details are debated. What Stephen is establishing is the long history of God's people being on the move — buried in a land promised to them but never fully possessed by them, in multiple places, across generations.

Why It Still Matters

The brothers sold Joseph. God used the very betrayal to bring about their salvation from famine. The sin against Joseph was not erased by God's redemption of it — the sin was real, the brothers had to face Joseph and repent, relationships had to be restored. But the sovereign God worked through the betrayal without approving it. That same pattern will repeat itself in the story Stephen is telling. The council handed Jesus over. God raised him. The one they rejected is the one through whom they can still be saved — if they turn. Joseph's brothers eventually did. The question hanging over Stephen's sermon is whether this generation will.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Moses Is Born and Raised

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

As the time draws near for God to fulfill his promise to Abraham the people in Egypt grow greatly in number. A new king who knows nothing about Joseph becomes ruler of Egypt. He takes advantage of the people and mistreats them forcing them to abandon their newborn babies. At that time Moses is born. He is a beautiful child in the sight of God. For three months he is cared for in his father's house. When he is placed outside Pharaoh's daughter takes him and raises him as her own son. Moses is educated in all the wisdom of the Egyptians and becomes powerful in both his speech and his actions.

What You Might Not Know

As the time drew near for God to fulfill his promise — Stephen notes that the timing of God's deliverance was specific. The four hundred years God had told Abraham about were approaching their end. The slavery was real, the suffering was real, but God was on a schedule Abraham never saw and Moses would not understand until he was called. The apparent silence of God during those four centuries was not abandonment. It was the patient waiting for the appointed time.

A new king who knew nothing about Joseph — the phrase is loaded. The Pharaoh who had exalted Joseph had been forgotten. The institutional memory of Egypt had been erased by a new regime. The same will happen centuries later in other forms. Each new generation forgets the previous generation's encounter with God's work unless that memory is deliberately passed down. Stephen is speaking to a council that knew the tradition but had forgotten the pattern within it.

He forced them to abandon their newborn babies so they would die — the infanticide ordered by Pharaoh sets up the next detail. Moses was born under a death sentence simply for being born. The deliverer of Israel began his life as a baby whose life was forfeit by royal decree. Stephen's audience may or may not have caught the echo — another child born under a royal decree that demanded the death of male infants was Jesus, whose birth triggered Herod's slaughter of the innocents. Both deliverers came into the world through infanticide ordered by rulers who sensed a threat.

He was a beautiful child in the sight of God — the Greek phrase asteios to theo literally means beautiful to God or beautiful before God. Moses was not only physically striking — there was something of God's favor on him from infancy. This same language was applied to infants in Jewish tradition who were recognized as destined for great purposes. Stephen is signaling that Moses was marked by God from the beginning.

Moses was educated in all the wisdom of the Egyptians — this detail comes from Jewish tradition expanding on the brief Exodus account. Moses was raised as an Egyptian prince with access to the full education of the Egyptian court. He was not a rural peasant who rose by accident. He was a prepared deliverer — trained in the wisdom, rhetoric, and administrative skill of the greatest civilization of his day. God works through prepared people. The preparation Moses received in Pharaoh's court became part of his equipment for leading the exodus.

Why It Still Matters

Pharaoh's daughter took him and raised him as her own son. The deliverer of Israel was raised in the house of the oppressor. The infant under a death sentence grew up at Pharaoh's table. God's pattern of using unexpected paths to bring about his purposes runs all the way back. Nothing in Moses's upbringing looked like preparation for leading Israel. Every detail was exactly that. The work God was doing was invisible to everyone including Moses until the burning bush. Stephen is giving his council a lesson in recognizing God's work in places they would not naturally look.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Moses Flees to Midian

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

When Moses is forty he decides to visit his own people the Israelites. He sees one of them being mistreated by an Egyptian and helps him by killing the Egyptian. Moses thinks his people will understand that God is using him to rescue them but they do not. The next day he sees two Israelites fighting and tries to make peace. The one mistreating the other pushes Moses away and asks who made him a ruler and judge over them — is he thinking of killing him the way he killed the Egyptian. When Moses hears this he flees to Midian where he lives as a stranger and has two sons.

What You Might Not Know

Moses thought his own people would understand that God was using him to rescue them but they didn't — this is one of the key lines of Stephen's entire sermon. Moses expected recognition. He received rejection. He thought his people would see that God was raising up a deliverer among them. They did not. The pattern Stephen is establishing is that God's chosen deliverers have always been rejected by the very people they came to deliver — at least initially. Joseph, rejected by his brothers. Moses, rejected by his people. The council listening can probably see where Stephen is going.

Who made you a ruler and a judge over us — this is the exact question that will be quoted again at the turning point of the sermon. Stephen will return to this phrase to make his main argument. The rejection of Moses at age forty was not a minor misunderstanding. It was a fundamental rejection of the deliverer God had sent. The Israelites did not recognize their deliverer when he came the first time. They had to wait another forty years in slavery before God sent him back.

Moses was forty years old — Jewish tradition divided Moses's life into three forty-year periods. Forty years in Pharaoh's court. Forty years in Midian. Forty years leading Israel. Stephen is following this traditional structure. Moses had to wait in exile for forty years before God called him back. The time between rejection and commissioning was long. God was not rushed, and Moses could not shortcut the process.

He lived there as a stranger and had two sons — the Greek word for stranger is paroikos — a resident alien. Moses — raised as an Egyptian prince, claimed no country of his own — became a sojourner in Midian. He named one of his sons Gershom which in Hebrew means "I have been a stranger in a foreign land." The identity of sojourner would mark Moses for the rest of his life. Even in Israel after the exodus he would never reach the land himself. He was a stranger until he died. That identity — the faithful one who is an outsider even among his own — connects Moses to the thread running through Stephen's whole sermon.

Why It Still Matters

The forty years Moses spent in Midian looked like failure. He had acted on what he believed was God's call and been rejected. He had fled. He had built a life as a shepherd in an obscure region. By ordinary standards his moment had passed. But God was not finished with him. The appearance of the burning bush was still forty years away. Sometimes the apparent waste of a long stretch of hidden life is actually God's preparation for something the hurried version of the person could not have accomplished. Moses the prince could have led a rebellion. Moses the shepherd could lead a nation. God took the time to make the second out of the first.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

God Calls Moses at the Burning Bush

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

Forty years later an angel appears to Moses in the flames of a burning bush in the desert near Mount Sinai. Moses is amazed and goes closer to look. He hears the voice of the Lord — I am the God of your ancestors, the God of Abraham Isaac and Jacob. Moses shakes with fear and does not dare to look. The Lord tells him to take off his sandals because the place where he is standing is holy ground. God has seen the mistreatment of his people in Egypt. He has heard their groaning and has come down to set them free. Now — I'll send you back to Egypt.

What You Might Not Know

An angel appeared to Moses in the flames of a burning bush in the desert near Mount Sinai — Stephen specifies the location. The foundational revelation that launched the exodus happened in the wilderness at a desert mountain — not in a temple, not in a city, not in Israel, not even in a religious setting by any human standard. God met Moses at a burning bush in the middle of nowhere. This is one of Stephen's central points. God has never been confined to Jerusalem or to the Temple. He appeared to Abraham in Mesopotamia, to Moses in the Sinai desert, to Israel throughout the wilderness. The council's assumption that God is bound to the Temple in Jerusalem was never true of how God has actually worked.

I am the God of your ancestors — the God of Abraham Isaac and Jacob — God identifies himself to Moses by his ongoing relationship to the patriarchs. Note the tense. Not I was the God of Abraham, who is now dead. I am the God of Abraham. Jesus will cite this same passage in Luke 20 to prove the resurrection — God is not the God of the dead but of the living. The patriarchs are alive to God. The God Moses meets at the bush is the same God who has been faithful across generations, whose relationships with his people do not end when they die.

Take off your sandals for the place where you're standing is holy ground — the ground Moses stood on was made holy by the presence of God. It was not intrinsically holy. It was a piece of desert dirt. It became holy because God was there. This is one of Stephen's most important theological points. Holiness is not a quality of locations. Holiness is the presence of God wherever he chooses to be. A building in Jerusalem can be called holy only as long as God is present in it. If God is not confined there, then no building is inherently sacred.

I have certainly seen the mistreatment of my people in Egypt — God had not been absent during the four hundred years of slavery. He had been watching. He had heard the groaning. He came down at the appointed time. The silence was not absence. Stephen's audience — whose forefathers had waited centuries for the first exodus — should have understood that God's silence in a later generation did not mean God was gone. The same God who came down at the bush comes down again. Stephen is arguing that God has now come down again in Jesus. The pattern is consistent.

Now come — I'll send you back to Egypt — the one who was rejected is sent back. Moses had fled Egypt in failure. Forty years later God sends him back as a deliverer. The rejection did not disqualify Moses. It positioned him. The man who had failed the first time was the man God used the second time. Stephen is about to connect this to Jesus — the one the council rejected will also return, and the question is whether they will recognize him this time.

Why It Still Matters

God met Moses in the middle of the desert at a mountain outside the Promised Land. Holiness was not in a building. It was not in a city. It was in the presence of the God who spoke from the fire. Stephen is telling the council that the same God who did that has not changed. He still shows up where he chooses. He still makes whatever ground he stands on holy. Confining God to one address in Jerusalem misunderstands who God is and has always been. The implications of that simple theological point will reshape how the Church thinks about sacred space for the next two thousand years.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Israel Rejects Moses — and God

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

This is the same Moses the people had rejected with the words who made you a ruler and a judge. God sent him to be their ruler and deliverer through the angel who appeared in the bush. He led them out of Egypt and performed wonders and signs in Egypt at the Red Sea and in the wilderness for forty years. This is the Moses who told the Israelites God would raise up a prophet like him from their own people. He was in the assembly in the wilderness with the angel who spoke to him at Sinai and with the ancestors. He received living words to pass on to the people. But the ancestors refused to obey him. They rejected him and in their hearts turned back to Egypt. They told Aaron to make them gods. They said they did not know what had happened to Moses. That was when they made an idol in the form of a calf. They brought sacrifices and celebrated what their own hands had made. But God turned away from them and gave them over to the worship of the stars. This agrees with what the prophets wrote — did Israel bring sacrifices for forty years in the wilderness? They lifted up the shrine of Molech and the star of the god Rephan. So God will send them into exile beyond Babylon.

What You Might Not Know

This is the same Moses they had rejected — Stephen pivots sharply here. The exact words the Israelites used to reject Moses — who made you a ruler and a judge — Stephen now repeats and lingers on. The deliverer God sent was the deliverer Israel initially rejected. The council sitting in front of Stephen claims Moses as their authority. Stephen is pointing out that their ancestors rejected the very Moses they now claim to follow. The pattern of rejecting God's deliverer is ancient. It is not a new development.

He received living words to pass on to us — the phrase "living words" translates zonta logia. The same word logia is used for sacred Scripture. Moses received the Torah at Sinai to pass on. These words were living — alive, life-giving, powerful. The tradition the council defends originally came as living words to a living people. Stephen's implication is that by the time these words reached the council they had become something else — treated as dead regulations to defend rather than as living words to hear.

They rejected him and in their hearts turned back to Egypt — even after the exodus, even after the Red Sea, even after seeing the glory on Sinai, the people's hearts turned back toward the slavery they had been delivered from. The pattern of rejection was not a one-time failure. It was a recurring heart condition. Stephen is suggesting that the same heart condition has persisted in Israel — preferring the familiar form of religion over the living voice of God when the living voice demands what they do not want to hear.

The golden calf — Stephen brings up the most iconic failure of Israel in the wilderness. While Moses was on the mountain receiving the Torah the people were at the base making an idol. They brought sacrifices to it and celebrated. The worship of the golden calf was not a complete abandonment of Yahweh — they claimed it represented the God who had brought them out of Egypt. But it was worship on their own terms rather than God's. Stephen is building toward his point that the Temple itself had eventually become a form of worship on Israel's terms rather than God's.

God turned away from them and gave them over to the worship of the stars — this is a sobering statement. When Israel refused to worship God as he actually was, God let them have the idols they wanted. The judgment was not God striking them down. It was God stepping back and letting them have what they insisted on. That form of judgment — being given over to what you chose against God's will — runs through Scripture. Paul repeats the pattern in Romans 1. Stephen is showing that the council's own history includes this pattern of God giving his people over to the idolatry they preferred.

The shrine of Molech and the star of Rephan — Stephen quotes from Amos 5. Molech was the Canaanite god associated with child sacrifice. Rephan was an astral deity. The prophet Amos had accused Israel of worshiping these pagan gods even during the wilderness wandering while pretending to worship Yahweh. Stephen's use of Amos's indictment is pointed. The long history of Israel includes pretended worship of God while actually serving other gods. The question implied is whether the council itself is doing something similar with the Temple.

So I'll send you into exile beyond Babylon — the Amos passage ends with the threat of exile. Stephen quotes the threat. Israel had gone into exile because of their idolatry. The threat Amos made had been fulfilled. Stephen is reminding the council that the pattern they are standing in has consequences. Rejecting God's deliverers and persisting in idolatry led to exile before. It could again.

Why It Still Matters

Stephen's argument has been gentle to this point. Here it turns prophetic. The history of Israel contains a persistent pattern of rejecting the deliverer God sends and of preferring idolatry on their own terms to worship on God's terms. The council would have understood this as ordinary prophetic preaching — the kind Amos or Jeremiah did. What they did not yet realize was that Stephen was not just reviewing ancient history. He was about to say that the same pattern had just reached its fullest expression in them. They had rejected the greatest deliverer God had ever sent. The history was not just history. It was the lead-up to an indictment they were about to hear leveled against themselves.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

The Tabernacle and the Temple

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

The ancestors had the Tabernacle of the Covenant with them in the wilderness. It was made as God directed Moses according to the pattern he had seen. After receiving the Tabernacle Israel under Joshua brought it into the land when they took the land from the nations God drove out before them. It remained in the land until the time of David. David found favor in God's sight and asked to provide a dwelling place for the God of Jacob. But Solomon was the one who built a house for him. Yet the Most High does not live in houses made by human hands. As the prophet says — heaven is my throne and the earth is my footstool. What kind of house will you build for me, says the Lord. Or where will my resting place be? Has not my hand made all these things?

What You Might Not Know

The Tabernacle of the Covenant — Stephen draws a careful distinction between the Tabernacle and the Temple. The Tabernacle was a tent. It was mobile. It went with Israel wherever they went. It was made according to the pattern God showed Moses on the mountain. It was designed by God to be portable — because God was not confined to one location. The Tabernacle from the beginning was a theological statement about how God dwelt with his people. He went with them. He did not require them to come to him at one address.

David found favor in the sight of God and asked that he might provide a dwelling place — Stephen notes that the impulse to build a permanent temple came from David. David wanted to build a house for God. God responded in 2 Samuel 7 by essentially telling David that he would build David a house — a dynasty — not the other way around. The temple-building impulse was human, not divine. God permitted Solomon to build the Temple but the Tabernacle had already been sufficient for God's presence with his people for centuries.

But it was Solomon who built a house for him — Stephen's language is careful here. He does not say Solomon built God's house. He says Solomon built a house for him. The distinction matters. The Temple was a building Solomon built for God — not a building that God needed. Stephen is laying the groundwork for the quotation that follows.

Yet the Most High doesn't live in houses made by human hands — this is the theological climax of the long historical section of Stephen's sermon. God does not live in houses made by human hands. The Greek phrase cheiropoietos (made by hands) is loaded. It is the same word used in the Septuagint for idols. In one phrase Stephen has put the Temple in the same category as the idols of the nations — things made by human hands that cannot contain the God they are supposed to represent. This was the charge the false witnesses had accused Stephen of — speaking against the Temple. Stephen is now giving a version of that teaching directly, grounded in the prophet Isaiah.

Heaven is my throne and the earth is my footstool — Stephen quotes Isaiah 66. The prophet himself had said centuries earlier that no building could contain God. Heaven is God's throne. Earth is his footstool. Any house humans build for him is necessarily too small. This is not Stephen inventing a new idea. It is Stephen quoting a recognized prophet whose words were in the council's own Scriptures.

Has not my hand made all these things? — the rhetorical question ends the quotation. God made everything. The Temple, however beautiful, was made by Solomon's hands from materials God had already made. The hierarchy is unmistakable. God made the world. Humans make buildings out of materials God made. No building can be big enough or impressive enough to contain the one who made everything. That was always true. Stephen is simply reminding the council of what their own prophet had already said.

Why It Still Matters

Stephen is about to be killed for what he has just said. The charge that he spoke against the Temple was true in the sense that he agreed with Isaiah — God does not live in buildings made by human hands. That teaching was not new. It was scriptural. But it cut at the heart of the religious establishment's claim to control access to God. If God was not confined to the Temple in Jerusalem, then the council did not control access to God. The Temple was a gift from God to his people — never a cage God was locked inside. The early Church's eventual spread across the Roman Empire without being tied to Jerusalem was already theologically launched in this sermon. Stephen died for an understanding of God that would become the foundation of everything Acts describes after this chapter.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Stephen Confronts the Council

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

Stephen turns directly on the council. You stiff-necked people. Your hearts and ears are still closed. You are just like your ancestors — you always resist the Holy Spirit. Was there ever a prophet your ancestors did not persecute? They even killed those who predicted the coming of the Righteous One. And now you have betrayed and murdered him — you who received the law given through angels but never obeyed it.

What You Might Not Know

You stiff-necked people — Stephen uses the exact language Moses used to describe Israel in the wilderness. In Exodus 32 after the golden calf God himself called Israel stiff-necked. Stephen is applying the same label to the council. They are not the faithful remnant defending the tradition. They are the same stiff-necked people their ancestors were. Stephen is taking the tradition they claim to represent and turning it against them. Their own Scriptures called their ancestors stiff-necked. Stephen is saying the pattern has not stopped — it has continued into the present generation, and they are it.

Your hearts and ears are still closed — the Greek word for closed is the same word used for uncircumcised. Hearts uncircumcised. Ears uncircumcised. The metaphor is deliberate. The council defended circumcision as the mark of covenant identity. Stephen is saying that what actually matters — circumcision of the heart — is exactly what they lack. They have the outer mark. They do not have the inner reality. Paul will develop this same theme in Romans 2. Stephen gets there first.

You always resist the Holy Spirit — Stephen's indictment reaches its core. The pattern of rejecting God's deliverers and preferring idolatry to real worship was never really about specific historical moments. It was about the consistent resistance of the human heart to the Spirit of God. The ancestors resisted. The current generation resists. The resistance is continuous. Stephen is not accusing the council of a technical violation. He is naming a spiritual condition that has persisted for generations and has now reached its fullest expression in them.

Was there ever a prophet your ancestors didn't persecute — the question is rhetorical and devastating. Israel's history is littered with prophets who were ignored, imprisoned, beaten, and killed. Jeremiah was thrown in a cistern. Isaiah was sawed in two according to tradition. Zechariah son of Jehoiada was stoned in the Temple courts. The prophets who spoke God's word to Israel were consistently rejected by the very people they were sent to. Stephen is reminding the council that this is their history — and suggesting that it is also their present.

Those who predicted the coming of the Righteous One — the prophets consistently pointed forward to a coming Righteous One who would fulfill the covenant. The council's ancestors had killed the prophets who predicted him. Now the council itself had killed him when he came. The parallelism is exact. First the messengers were killed. Then the one they pointed to was killed. The sequence is complete.

You have betrayed and murdered him — Stephen names the crime directly. The council handed Jesus over and he was killed. Betrayal is the language of Judas. Murder is the language of an unjust killing. Stephen does not soften either word. He indicts the council in two ways simultaneously — they betrayed him to those who killed him, and in doing so they bear the moral weight of the murder itself. This is the sentence that makes their response inevitable.

You who received the law given through angels but never obeyed it — the tradition held that the Torah was mediated through angels. Paul mentions this in Galatians 3. Stephen is saying the council had received the highest gift — the Law given through angels — but had never actually obeyed it. The Law itself testified against them. The very tradition they were about to execute Stephen for supposedly offending was a tradition they themselves had never kept.

Why It Still Matters

Stephen's confrontation is one of the most direct moments of prophetic preaching in all of Acts. He does not soften anything. He does not leave escape routes. He tells them plainly that they have resisted the Spirit, that they are continuing the pattern of their ancestors, and that they have killed the Righteous One their own prophets had predicted. The response is predictable. Prophetic truth spoken directly to power almost always produces violence. But the truth had to be spoken, and the council had to hear it. Whether they repented or killed the messenger was their choice. They chose. Stephen did what the prophets before him had done — he told the truth knowing what it would cost, and he paid the cost.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Stephen Is Stoned

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7
What Is Happening Here

The council members are furious and gnash their teeth at Stephen. But Stephen full of the Holy Spirit looks up to heaven and sees the glory of God and Jesus standing at the right hand of God. Look — he says — I see heaven open and the Son of Man standing at the right hand of God. The council covers their ears and yelling at the top of their voices rushes at him. They drag him out of the city and stone him. The witnesses lay their coats at the feet of a young man named Saul. While they stone him Stephen prays — Lord Jesus receive my spirit. Then he falls on his knees and cries out — Lord do not hold this sin against them. When he had said this he fell asleep.

What You Might Not Know

They were furious and gnashed their teeth at him — the Greek phrase literally means they were cut to the heart and ground their teeth. The same word for cut to the heart was used in Acts 2 to describe the crowd at Pentecost — but there it led to repentance. Here it leads to rage. The same conviction of the Spirit can produce either response depending on what the heart does with it. Pentecost conviction became repentance. Stephen's conviction became murder.

Stephen full of the Holy Spirit looked up to heaven and saw the glory of God — the detail that Stephen was full of the Holy Spirit is repeated deliberately. This is not a man defending himself with rhetorical skill. This is a man seeing through the ceiling. The Spirit that filled him opened his eyes to see what was already there. The glory of God was present. Jesus was present. Stephen saw what the council could not see because they had refused to.

Jesus standing at the right hand of God — the detail that Jesus was standing is unique to this vision. Throughout the New Testament Jesus is described as seated at the right hand of God — the posture of finished work and royal authority. Here he is standing. Why? Several possibilities. He may be standing as a witness — standing to testify at the trial that was actually happening in heaven while the council thought they were conducting it on earth. He may be standing to welcome his first martyr home. He may be standing in solidarity with his suffering servant. Whatever the specific reason Luke preserves the detail. Jesus was on his feet for Stephen.

The Son of Man — Stephen uses the title Jesus had used for himself more than any other. This was a messianic title from Daniel 7 where one like a son of man was given authority and glory by the Ancient of Days. By applying this title to Jesus standing at the right hand of God Stephen was making an unambiguous messianic claim in the presence of the council that had executed Jesus for making that very claim. The blasphemy they had accused Stephen of was now confirmed from Stephen's own mouth in their hearing.

They covered their ears and yelling at the top of their voices rushed at him — the council ceases to function as a legal body. They abandon procedure. They cover their ears so they will not hear what Stephen is saying. They yell to drown out his words. They rush at him as a mob. The trial becomes a lynching. Stephen was not technically executed — he was murdered by an enraged crowd that included the judges themselves.

The witnesses laid their coats at the feet of a young man named Saul — this is the first appearance of Saul — later known as Paul — in Acts. Under Jewish law the witnesses had to throw the first stones. They took off their cloaks to throw more freely and laid them with a young man named Saul. Saul is introduced as a bystander — but Luke is signaling his importance. The man who will become the apostle to the Gentiles first appears on the page as a consenting witness to the first Christian martyr. Acts is setting up the transformation. Saul watched Stephen die. Whatever Saul did next in his persecution of the Church cannot be separated from what he saw that day.

Lord Jesus receive my spirit — Stephen's prayer echoes Jesus's own prayer from the cross — Father into your hands I commit my spirit. The difference is important. Jesus prayed to the Father. Stephen prays to Jesus. The first Christian martyr addresses Jesus with divine prayer — prayer that belongs to God. The worship of Jesus as Lord is not a late development in the Church. It is there at the beginning from the lips of the first martyr.

Lord do not hold this sin against them — Stephen's second prayer also echoes Jesus. Jesus had prayed Father forgive them for they do not know what they are doing. Stephen prays the same kind of prayer for his own killers. The forgiveness of enemies as they are killing you is the most distinctive marker of the way of Jesus. Stephen had learned it well enough to do it in the moment. That prayer will echo through Christian martyrdom for two thousand years.

He fell asleep — the language of dying as falling asleep is characteristic of early Christian writing. Death for Stephen was not a final ending. It was a sleep. The resurrection he had been preaching was his own certainty in the moment of dying. The violent killing the council had intended to silence the Gospel instead produced the first Christian testimony to faithful death — a death that looked like sleep because the one dying knew what waited on the other side.

Why It Still Matters

Stephen's death is the hinge of Acts. His stoning triggered the first great persecution which scattered the church and sent it out from Jerusalem — starting the fulfillment of Acts 1:8. The man watching with approval — Saul of Tarsus — would himself be stopped on the road to Damascus and transformed into the apostle whose ministry would dominate the rest of the book. And the first Christian martyr showed the Church what faithful death looks like — eyes lifted to heaven, confession of Jesus as Lord, prayer for the killers, and a peaceful falling asleep. Every Christian martyr since has drawn on the pattern Stephen established. He was the first to die for the Name. He would not be the last. But the shape of his dying became the shape of faithful dying from that moment forward.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 7

Acts Chapter 8

The Church Is Persecuted and Scattered

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8
What Is Happening Here

Saul is there giving his full approval to Stephen's death. On that very day a great persecution breaks out against the church in Jerusalem. All the believers except the apostles are scattered throughout Judea and Samaria. Godly men bury Stephen and mourn deeply for him. But Saul begins to destroy the church. He goes from house to house dragging off both men and women and throwing them in prison.

What You Might Not Know

Saul was there giving his full approval to Stephen's death — Luke returns to Saul's involvement. He does not leave it at the feet-and-coats detail from Chapter 7. Luke wants the reader to understand that Saul was not a neutral spectator. He approved. The Greek word is suneudokon — to approve wholeheartedly, to agree with full consent. Saul was not pulled into the persecution reluctantly. He was on board from the beginning and became one of its leaders. When Luke later describes Saul's conversion the scope of what is being forgiven will be proportional to the scope of what is being acknowledged here.

All the believers except the apostles were scattered — this detail is significant. The apostles stayed in Jerusalem. The rest of the church scattered. This pattern would have seemed strange — normally the leaders would be the primary targets and would need to flee. Instead the ordinary believers fled while the apostles remained. This may reflect the specific nature of the persecution — targeting Hellenistic Jewish believers like Stephen whose preaching was more confrontational to the Temple system. It may also reflect the apostles' decision to stay and maintain the Jerusalem church as a base. Either way the result was that the scattered believers carried the Gospel outward while the apostles held the center.

Godly men buried Stephen and mourned deeply for him — the mourning is described with a specific Greek word koptomai that refers to formal lamentation including beating the breast. Stephen's death was honored with full public mourning despite the fact that the authorities had just executed him as a blasphemer. The godly men who buried him — probably the same kind of pious men who buried Jesus — risked their own standing by giving Stephen an honorable burial. Courage in honoring the dead was part of the faithfulness of the early Church.

Saul began to destroy the church — the Greek word is lumaino which was used for ravaging or devastating a field or laying waste to a city. It describes violent systematic destruction. Saul was not just arresting a few individuals. He was trying to wipe out the movement. Luke's word choice communicates the severity. This was not ordinary religious harassment. It was an attempt at extermination.

He went from house to house — this detail is important. The early church met in homes. Saul's persecution followed them there. Private homes were not safe. Family gatherings were raided. Men and women were dragged out in front of their children and neighbors. The trauma of these raids shaped the early Christian community's understanding of what faith cost. Paul himself will later write that he was the chief of sinners — partly because he remembered these raids and what they did to the families he tore apart.

Why It Still Matters

Those who had been scattered preached the word wherever they went. This single line at the start of the next section captures what the persecution accomplished. What Saul meant to destroy he inadvertently spread. The believers who fled Jerusalem carried the Gospel into Judea and Samaria. The persecution accomplished exactly what Jesus had commanded in Acts 1:8 — witnesses in Jerusalem, in Judea and Samaria, and to the ends of the earth. The Church was obeying Jesus's command even as it thought it was just running for its life. God does not need ideal conditions to spread his Gospel. Sometimes the mechanism of spreading it is the very opposition that was meant to stop it.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8

Philip Preaches in Samaria

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8
What Is Happening Here

Those who have been scattered preach the word wherever they go. Philip goes to a city in Samaria and tells the people about the Messiah. When the crowds hear Philip and see the signs they all pay close attention. Evil spirits come out of many with loud shrieks. Many who are paralyzed or cannot walk are healed. There is great joy in the city. A man named Simon had been practicing sorcery in the city and had amazed everyone. He bragged that he was someone great and all the people from the least to the greatest gave him their attention. They said he had the power of God called the Great Power and followed him because his magic had amazed them for a long time. But when they believe Philip as he preaches the good news about the kingdom of God and the name of Jesus the Messiah they are baptized — both men and women. Even Simon himself believes and is baptized. He follows Philip everywhere amazed by the great signs and miracles.

What You Might Not Know

Philip — this is Philip the evangelist, one of the seven chosen in Chapter 6, not Philip the apostle. Like Stephen he was a Hellenistic Jew appointed to serve tables who turned out to have a much larger calling. The men chosen to handle food distribution for widows became preachers evangelists and martyrs. The apostles' willingness to delegate administrative work uncovered gifts that might otherwise have stayed hidden. Philip is about to become the first Christian missionary to cross a major cultural boundary — from Jews to Samaritans.

A city in Samaria — Samaritans were the descendants of the northern kingdom of Israel mixed with the peoples the Assyrians had settled there after 722 BC. They worshiped Yahweh but on Mount Gerizim rather than in Jerusalem. They accepted only the five books of Moses. Jews and Samaritans had centuries of mutual hostility. Jesus had passed through Samaria and spoken with the woman at the well in John 4, but his ministry had not established a significant Samaritan following. Philip's mission to Samaria is the first major expansion of the church beyond Judaism. The scattering caused by persecution put Philip in a city Jews normally avoided — and the Gospel took root there.

Great joy in the city — Luke's note that great joy came to the city captures something important. The Gospel does not arrive as a new oppression. It arrives as liberation. Evil spirits left. The sick were healed. The community that had been controlled by Simon's dark power was being set free. Joy was the mark of what God was doing — not somber religious observance but genuine gladness that reached across the whole city.

Simon who had been practicing sorcery — Simon is identified as a magos, a practitioner of magic. In the ancient world magic was a serious business — a combination of ritual, incantation, and what we might call early pharmacology. Simon had built a reputation over time. The Samaritans called him the Great Power — essentially identifying him with divine power. Simon had cultivated this identity deliberately. He had the whole city from the least to the greatest giving him attention.

Even Simon himself believed and was baptized — Luke records Simon's belief and baptism without yet revealing the problem. This matters. Simon's baptism was genuine as far as the community could tell. He professed belief. He was baptized. He followed Philip. Only later will Peter's discernment reveal that Simon's heart was not actually right. External conformity to the process of belief and baptism did not necessarily indicate the inner transformation. Luke tells the story honestly. Not every baptism in Acts was a baptism of a changed heart. The community cannot always tell the difference. Sometimes only subsequent actions reveal what was really happening inside.
