Amazed by the great signs and miracles — Simon was still in the posture he had cultivated for his own career. He had amazed others. Now he was the one amazed. His fascination with Philip's miracles was not wrong in itself. But it was the fascination of a professional watching another professional, not the transformation of a sinner encountering grace. That difference will become clear when Peter arrives.

Why It Still Matters

They believed Philip as he preached the good news about the kingdom of God and the name of Jesus the Messiah. The content of Philip's preaching is specified. He preached the kingdom of God and the name of Jesus the Messiah. The Samaritans heard about the kingdom — the rule of God breaking into the world — and about Jesus specifically as the Messiah. Philip did not offer a generic religious message adjusted to Samaritan sensitivities. He preached the Jewish Messiah to Samaritans and they believed. The Gospel did not need to be diluted to cross the cultural boundary. It needed to be preached clearly — and the Spirit did the work of opening hearts to receive it.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8

Simon Tries to Buy the Holy Spirit

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8
What Is Happening Here

When the apostles in Jerusalem hear Samaria has accepted the word they send Peter and John. When they arrive they pray for the new believers to receive the Holy Spirit because the Spirit had not yet come on any of them. They had only been baptized in the name of the Lord Jesus. Peter and John place their hands on them and they receive the Holy Spirit. Simon sees that the Spirit is given when the apostles lay their hands on people and he offers them money. Give me this power too he says so that anyone I lay my hands on will receive the Holy Spirit. Peter answers — may your money be destroyed along with you because you thought you could buy God's gift with money. You have no part in this ministry because your heart is not right before God. Turn away from this evil and pray to the Lord. Maybe he will forgive you for having such a thought in your heart. I can see that you are full of bitterness and trapped by sin. Simon says — pray to the Lord for me so that nothing you have said will happen to me. After Peter and John testify and speak the word of the Lord they return to Jerusalem. On the way they preach the good news in many Samaritan villages.

What You Might Not Know

The Jerusalem apostles sent Peter and John — this detail is important. The Samaritan mission was not left to Philip alone. The leadership of the Jerusalem church sent two of the most prominent apostles to confirm that what was happening in Samaria was authentic and to extend apostolic recognition to the new Samaritan believers. This was not about controlling the mission. It was about welcoming the Samaritans fully into the church by apostolic witness. John the son of Zebedee — who had once asked Jesus to call down fire on a Samaritan village that rejected him — now goes to Samaria to affirm Samaritan believers in the faith. His own transformation is visible in this trip.

The Holy Spirit had not yet come on any of them — this has generated discussion for two thousand years. The Samaritans had believed Philip's preaching, been baptized, and seen miracles. Why had the Spirit not yet come? Several possibilities. Luke may be describing a deliberate divine timing — the Spirit's public coming on the Samaritans was held until the Jerusalem apostles were present so the Samaritan inclusion could not be questioned as second-class. Jerusalem and Samaria had centuries of religious separation. If the Samaritans had received the Spirit without Jerusalem's involvement the church might have split into a Jerusalem church and a Samaritan church. God timed the outpouring to make the unity of the church explicit through the apostolic witness.

They received the Holy Spirit — the text does not say exactly what was visible when this happened but in the context something observable occurred. Simon saw that the Spirit was given when the apostles laid hands on people — which means he saw something. Some kind of physical manifestation similar to Pentecost likely occurred — speaking in tongues prophesying or praising God. Luke does not specify which. What is clear is that the giving of the Spirit was publicly visible and Simon wanted to reproduce it.

Offered them money — this moment gave birth to a word that has lasted for two thousand years. The sin of simony — the attempt to buy or sell spiritual positions or ministries — is named after this Simon. Throughout church history popes bishops and councils have had to condemn simony. It began here. The impulse to commodify spiritual power for personal profit was present in the church's first week outside Jerusalem.

May your money be destroyed along with you — Peter's response is severe. The Greek phrase is literally "may your silver be with you into destruction." Peter ties Simon's fate to his money. If he continues identifying himself with his money he will share its destruction. This is not Peter wishing Simon harm — it is Peter warning Simon plainly of where he is headed. The warning is followed by a call to repent.

Turn away from this evil and pray to the Lord. Maybe he'll forgive you — Peter offers Simon a path back. Repentance and prayer. But notice the word "maybe." Peter does not guarantee forgiveness. The sin of trying to buy the Spirit is serious enough that Peter cannot assure Simon in advance that his repentance will be accepted. The uncertainty is sobering. Most New Testament appeals to repent promise forgiveness on the other side. Peter's appeal is more cautious here. This is one of the clearest passages showing that belief and baptism do not automatically produce a changed heart, and that heart-level sin after baptism is a serious matter requiring serious repentance.

Full of bitterness and trapped by sin — Peter's diagnosis is spiritual. Simon is not merely making a bad business offer. He is full of bitterness and trapped. The language echoes Deuteronomy 29 where the root of bitterness is connected to idolatry. Simon's career as a sorcerer had formed his heart in ways that baptism had not yet undone. He had changed religions but not his soul. Peter saw what Philip had not seen.

Pray to the Lord for me — Simon asks Peter to pray for him. We do not learn what became of Simon. Early church tradition developed elaborate stories about him — that he became a heretic and was destroyed by Peter in Rome — but Luke does not tell us his end. Luke leaves the story at the moment of appeal. Simon recognized his danger and asked for prayer. Whether he actually repented beyond that we do not know. The ambiguity is part of the honesty of Acts — some stories do not resolve neatly.

Why It Still Matters

You thought you could buy God's gift with money. Peter's rebuke establishes one of the most important principles of the Christian tradition. The gifts of God cannot be purchased. Spiritual power is not for sale. Positions of ministry are not to be bought. Whenever the church has forgotten this principle it has fallen into corruption. Whenever it has remembered it it has preserved something essential about the Gospel — that the grace of God is given, not sold. Peter's stand against Simon in the first week of the Gentile mission was a stand that has had to be made again and again throughout church history. The line Peter drew is still where the line must be.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8

Philip and the Ethiopian Official

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8
What Is Happening Here

An angel of the Lord tells Philip to go south to the desert road from Jerusalem to Gaza. Philip gets up and goes. On the way he meets an Ethiopian official — a eunuch who serves as chief treasurer for the queen of Ethiopia. He had gone to Jerusalem to worship and is now on his way home sitting in his chariot reading from Isaiah. The Spirit tells Philip to go over to the chariot and stay near it. Philip runs up and hears the man reading Isaiah. He asks if the man understands what he is reading. The man says how can I unless someone explains it. He invites Philip to sit with him. The passage he is reading describes one led like a sheep to be killed, like a lamb silent before its shearer, denied justice, his life taken from the earth. The official asks who the prophet is talking about — himself or someone else. Philip starts with that passage and tells him the good news about Jesus. As they travel they come to some water. The official asks what is keeping him from being baptized. He orders the chariot to stop. Both Philip and the official go down into the water and Philip baptizes him. When they come up the Spirit of the Lord takes Philip away. The official does not see him again but goes on his way full of joy.

What You Might Not Know

The desert road from Jerusalem to Gaza — the angel sends Philip to a desert road. Luke notes the detail. Philip had been in a Samaritan city with great success, preaching to crowds. He is now sent to a single traveler on an empty road. The Spirit moves him from mass ministry to one-on-one ministry without apology. Both are equally the work of God. The successful urban evangelist is sent to a quiet encounter with one person. The contrast teaches something about how the Spirit directs mission. Sometimes the most strategic thing is the single conversation.

An Ethiopian official — a eunuch who served as chief treasurer for the queen of Ethiopia — Ethiopia in the ancient world was the kingdom of Nubia south of Egypt. It was ruled by a line of queens who carried the title Candace. The man Philip meets is the royal treasurer — the equivalent of a finance minister. He is a Gentile, a eunuch, and from the ends of the earth — Ethiopia represented the southern edge of the known world. Every one of those identity markers is important. Deuteronomy 23 had excluded eunuchs from the assembly of the Lord. Isaiah 56 had reversed this, promising that eunuchs who held to God's covenant would have a name better than sons and daughters. Philip is about to baptize the first person whose inclusion fulfills Isaiah 56 explicitly.

He had gone to Jerusalem to worship — the Ethiopian had traveled a great distance to worship at the Temple. But as a eunuch he could not enter the inner courts. He would have stood in the Court of the Gentiles, barred from full participation in the worship he had come so far to offer. He is returning home a devout seeker who had been admitted only partway into the community he wanted to belong to. The man Philip meets is already deeply serious about God but has hit a wall that the old covenant could not remove.

Reading from the book of the prophet Isaiah — he was reading from Isaiah 53, the suffering servant passage that had also been Stephen's theme. The Ethiopian was reading one of the most important messianic prophecies in the entire Hebrew Bible — but reading it without understanding. Many Jewish readers at the time understood Isaiah 53 as describing Israel collectively suffering among the nations. Some understood it as describing the prophet Isaiah himself. The messianic reading — that this was about one specific person who would suffer for the sins of the people — was contested. The Ethiopian needed someone to explain.

How can I unless someone explains it to me — this line captures something fundamental about the Scriptures. The Bible is not self-interpreting for every reader in every culture. Sometimes a text requires someone to open it up — to explain the context, the prophecy, the fulfillment. The Ethiopian was literate, devout, and wealthy. He could read the words. He could not by himself unlock their meaning. He needed a teacher. The church has always had a role as the community that passes on the interpretation of Scripture from one generation to the next. Philip fulfilled that role on a desert road for one traveler.

Philip started with that very passage and told him the good news about Jesus — Philip's approach is a model of biblical evangelism. He did not start with a canned presentation. He started with what the man was already reading. Isaiah 53 led directly to Jesus. The suffering servant was Jesus. The one led like a sheep to slaughter was Jesus. The one denied justice and taken from the earth was Jesus. Everything the Ethiopian had been puzzled by found its answer in the Gospel. Philip met the man where he was and opened what was already in front of him.

What's keeping me from being baptized — the Ethiopian's question is one of the most moving moments in Acts. What is keeping me from being baptized. The answer according to the old categories would have been — a lot. He was a Gentile. He was a eunuch. He was a foreigner. Under the old system his access was limited. The new covenant in Jesus removed every one of those barriers. Nothing was keeping him from baptism. Philip baptized him immediately. The kingdom of God had room for a eunuch, a foreigner, and a Gentile all in one person.

The Spirit of the Lord suddenly took Philip away — the disappearance of Philip is one of the more unusual events in Acts. The Greek suggests something like a sudden removal — Philip was there, and then he was not. This kind of miraculous translation happens elsewhere in Scripture — Elijah, for example. Philip reappears at Azotus. The point is not the mechanism but the completeness of Philip's work with the Ethiopian. Once the baptism was done Philip's mission to him was complete. The Ethiopian did not need Philip. He had Jesus.

He went on his way full of joy — the last detail of the story. The Ethiopian returned to his country rejoicing. Church tradition says he took the Gospel back to Ethiopia and that the Ethiopian church traces its origins to this encounter. Whether that specific tradition is historical or not, the principle it represents is certainly true — a single encounter on a desert road produced a converted man going back to his country with joy. The ripples of that one encounter reached places Philip himself would never go. God's math is not the math of efficiency. One conversation with one man on one road produced fruit Philip could never have imagined.

Why It Still Matters

The Ethiopian eunuch was the first Gentile convert recorded in Acts — before Cornelius, before the mission to Antioch, before Paul's travels. The Gospel reached the ends of the earth in Chapter 8 through one unnamed official on a desert road. The pattern is worth noticing. The big public missions matter. The single conversations on empty roads also matter. The Spirit works in both. Sometimes the kingdom advances through crowds. Sometimes it advances through one person reading in a chariot who needed someone to explain. Both are equally the work of God. Neither is more glorious than the other.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8

Philip Preaches Along the Coast

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8
What Is Happening Here

Philip appears at Azotus and travels through the region preaching the good news in all the towns until he reaches Caesarea.

What You Might Not Know

Azotus — the ancient Philistine city of Ashdod, about twenty miles north of Gaza on the Mediterranean coast. The city had been renamed Azotus in the Hellenistic period. Philip appears there — the Greek word suggests he was found there, that he reappeared after his sudden departure from the Ethiopian. From Azotus he traveled up the coast preaching as he went.

Through the region preaching in all the towns — this single summary sentence covers a ministry of perhaps weeks or months. Philip moved northward along the Mediterranean coast preaching in town after town. Cities along this route included Lydda and Joppa — both of which will appear later in Acts when Peter ministers there. Philip was laying the groundwork in those coastal towns. When Peter arrives in Lydda and Joppa in Chapter 9 he finds disciples already there. Philip was likely the one who first reached them.

Until he reached Caesarea — Caesarea was the Roman provincial capital on the coast, a substantial Gentile city with a Jewish minority. Philip apparently settled there. When Paul visits Caesarea many years later in Acts 21 he stays in the house of Philip the evangelist who has four unmarried daughters who prophesy. Philip's ministry ended where it had been leading all along — in a largely Gentile Roman city with a growing Christian household. The scattered servant who had been a deacon in Jerusalem became a settled evangelist in Caesarea, and his home became a center of hospitality and prophetic gift for decades to come.

Why It Still Matters

Philip's story between Chapter 8 and his reappearance in Chapter 21 is mostly hidden. He preached. He raised daughters. He kept a home where travelers found rest. The flash of Chapter 8 — the Samaritan revival, the meeting with the Ethiopian, the sudden travel up the coast — was followed by decades of ordinary faithful ministry Luke does not describe. Most Christian lives look more like Philip's hidden years than his dramatic moments. Most of what we do for God happens in towns along the coast that never make the book. But it all matters. Philip's scattered-through-persecution ministry planted seeds in Lydda, Joppa, and Caesarea that bore fruit for the rest of the first century and beyond. The same may be true of what we are doing that no one is writing down.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 8

Acts Chapter 9

Saul Meets Jesus on the Road

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

Saul is still breathing out murderous threats against the disciples. He goes to the high priest and asks for letters to the synagogues in Damascus. He wants to find anyone who belongs to the Way — men or women — so he can bring them as prisoners to Jerusalem. As he nears Damascus a light from heaven flashes around him. He falls to the ground and hears a voice — Saul Saul why do you persecute me. Who are you Lord, Saul asks. I am Jesus whom you are persecuting, he replies. Now get up and go into the city and you will be told what you must do. The men with Saul stand there speechless. They hear the sound but do not see anyone. Saul gets up but cannot see anything. They lead him by the hand into Damascus. For three days he is blind and does not eat or drink.

What You Might Not Know

Breathing out murderous threats — the Greek phrase captures Saul's state of mind vividly. He was not just planning persecution. Persecution was the air he breathed. Luke has already told us Saul was dragging people from their houses and throwing them in prison. Now we see his scope expanding. Damascus was about a hundred and thirty miles north of Jerusalem — a journey of six or seven days on foot. Saul was willing to travel a week to pursue Christians in another country. The Jewish community of Damascus was large — the Jewish historian Josephus mentions thousands of Jews living there — and Saul wanted to prevent the Way from taking root there as it had in Jerusalem.

The Way — this is one of the earliest names for the Christian movement. It appears several times in Acts. The name comes from Jesus's statement in John 14 — I am the way. Before the disciples were called Christians — a name first used in Antioch — they called themselves the Way. The name emphasizes that following Jesus is not primarily a set of beliefs but a path of life. It was a path with a specific direction and shape. Saul was trying to close that path.

Letters to the synagogues — Saul's legal authority came from the high priest's letters. The synagogues in Damascus would recognize these as official communication from the supreme religious authority in Jerusalem. Saul was not a rogue vigilante. He was an authorized agent of the Jerusalem establishment. His credentials were impeccable. That matters for what happens next. The man on the road to Damascus was not a spiritual seeker looking for God. He was a certified agent of the religious power structure on official business to destroy the Church.

A light from heaven flashed around him — Luke describes the event in three telling places in Acts. Here in Chapter 9. Again when Paul himself tells the story to the Jerusalem crowd in Chapter 22. And a third time before King Agrippa in Chapter 26. The three accounts emphasize different details. This is not because the accounts contradict. It is because the event was so foundational that Paul retold it for the rest of his life and different audiences heard different aspects of it. Luke preserves all three tellings. The event was that important.

Saul Saul why do you persecute me — the double address is striking. Jesus calls Saul by name and says it twice. This same pattern appears with Moses at the bush — Moses Moses — and with Abraham at the binding of Isaac — Abraham Abraham. The double address in the Hebrew tradition signals a moment of profound personal calling. Saul is being called by name in exactly the way the great figures of Israel were called. Jesus is doing something specific and personal with this persecutor.

Why do you persecute me — Jesus does not say why do you persecute my followers. He says why do you persecute me. The identification of Jesus with his church is total. To persecute the Church is to persecute Jesus himself. This is one of the earliest and most profound statements of what Paul will later call the body of Christ theology. The Church is not a group Jesus has sent into the world. The Church is Jesus's own body, and attacks on it fall on him personally.

The men traveling with Saul stood there speechless — the companions experienced something — they heard the sound, they saw the light — but they did not see Jesus and did not understand the words. Different accounts in Acts describe the sensory experience slightly differently. The consistent point is that the encounter was personal to Saul. The men around him were witnesses but they were not the subject of the encounter. Jesus met Saul specifically.

For three days he was blind and didn't eat or drink — three days of complete fasting and darkness. Paul enters a period that parallels Jesus's own three days in the tomb. Saul was spiritually dead to what God was doing — he had been fighting against God's work with religious zeal. The three days are a kind of burial. When Ananias arrives and Saul's sight is restored, something new is coming out of the tomb. The man who was certain about everything becomes a man who has to be led by the hand. The man of fierce action becomes a man fasting in silence for three days. Before Saul could be useful to God he had to be brought all the way down to blindness.

Why It Still Matters

I am Jesus whom you are persecuting. Saul had been so sure he was serving God. He had letters from the high priest. He had training from Gamaliel. He had the convictions of his ancestors. And in a moment of blinding light he learned that everything he had been so sure about had been wrong. He had not been serving God — he had been persecuting Jesus. That kind of total reversal of certainty is possible only when God himself intervenes. Saul did not reason his way to Christianity. He was knocked to the ground by the one he was persecuting. Some conversions still happen this way — not through argument but through encounter with the living Jesus who stops the persecutor and asks why.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

Ananias Is Sent to Saul

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

In Damascus there is a disciple named Ananias. The Lord calls to him in a vision — Ananias. Yes Lord he answers. The Lord tells him to go to the house of Judas on Straight Street and ask for a man from Tarsus named Saul. He is praying right now. In a vision he has seen a man named Ananias come and place his hands on him to restore his sight. Ananias answers — Lord I have heard many reports about this man and all the harm he has done to your people in Jerusalem. He has come here with authority from the leading priests to arrest everyone who calls on your name. But the Lord tells Ananias to go. This man is his chosen instrument to proclaim his name to the Gentiles and their kings and to the people of Israel. He will show Saul how much he must suffer for his name. Ananias goes to the house and enters it. He places his hands on Saul and says — Brother Saul the Lord Jesus who appeared to you on the road has sent me so that you may see again and be filled with the Holy Spirit. Immediately something like scales falls from Saul's eyes and he can see again. He gets up and is baptized and after taking some food regains his strength.

What You Might Not Know

Ananias — this is a different Ananias from the one who died in Chapter 5. This Ananias is a Jewish Christian in Damascus described as a devout man highly respected by the Jewish community according to Paul's later telling in Acts 22. He is not an apostle. He is a regular disciple who had been living in Damascus when the church spread there. His name means Yahweh is gracious — which fits perfectly what he is about to become to Saul.

Straight Street — this street actually existed and still exists today in Damascus. It was a Roman-style main east-west road running through the city. Luke's geographic detail is precise. The house of Judas was on an actual named street in an actual city and Ananias could walk to it. The specificity tells you Luke is reporting real events with real addresses, not constructing a symbolic narrative.

He's praying right now — Saul's three days of blindness were not a spiritual collapse. They were a spiritual awakening. He was praying. The man who had been breathing out murderous threats had become a man praying in the dark. He had also been given a vision of Ananias coming to him. God was simultaneously preparing both men — giving Saul a vision of who would come and giving Ananias a vision of who to go to. The Lord is working both sides of the meeting before either man knows the other.

Lord I have heard many reports — Ananias's protest is understandable. Saul's reputation had reached Damascus. Ananias knew exactly who was being sent to his city and why. Going to meet Saul in that house on Straight Street felt like walking into a trap. Ananias's hesitation is not cowardice. It is appropriate caution. He does not flatly refuse. He names what he knows and lets the Lord address it.

He is my chosen instrument to proclaim my name to the Gentiles — the Lord gives Ananias a glimpse of what Saul is going to become. Not just a forgiven sinner. A chosen instrument. The Greek word is skeuos which means a vessel, a tool, or an instrument. Saul is a vessel God has chosen to carry the Gospel to places and people Ananias would never imagine. The word chosen suggests this was not a second-chance conversion. It was a purposeful calling that had been prepared. Paul would later write that God had set him apart from his mother's womb. The chosen instrument was being commissioned on Straight Street.

To proclaim my name to the Gentiles and their kings — this is the first clear statement of Paul's future mission. Gentiles kings and Israel. The scope is staggering. Paul will eventually stand before Roman governors, appeal to Caesar, and witness to King Agrippa. He will plant churches across the Roman world. All of that is already present in this word from the Lord to Ananias. Paul's entire future is summarized in one sentence before he has even received his sight back.

I'll show him how much he must suffer for my name — this detail is sobering. Saul's calling comes with suffering built into it. The Lord does not promise an easy ministry. He promises much suffering. Paul himself will later list his sufferings in 2 Corinthians 11 — beatings, shipwrecks, imprisonments, dangers of every kind. All of it was foreshadowed on Straight Street. The chosen instrument would be beaten, rejected, and finally executed. Paul knew from the beginning what his ministry would cost.

Brother Saul — Ananias's greeting is the most pastorally perfect moment in Acts. He does not call Saul by his past reputation. He does not interrogate or test him. He calls him brother. The man who had been traveling to Damascus as an enemy is welcomed as family on his first morning as a believer. That single word accomplished something no argument could have accomplished. Saul received his first word of acceptance from the very community he had come to destroy.

Something like scales fell from his eyes — the physical restoration of sight was accompanied by a symbolic detail. Something like scales fell. Saul's old way of seeing had been covering his eyes and had to be removed before he could see the new reality. Scales suggest fish scales — hard coverings over the eyes. The man who had been so sure he saw clearly had in fact been looking through scales. Only when they fell could he see Jesus for who he really was.

He got up and was baptized and after taking some food regained his strength — the sequence is tight. Sight. Baptism. Food. Strength. The three-day fast ended with baptism and a meal. Saul's new life began with the sacraments of the community he had once persecuted and the simple physical act of eating with his new brothers.

Why It Still Matters

Ananias is one of the most quietly important figures in the New Testament. He is not an apostle. He does not appear again after this chapter. He is briefly mentioned by Paul years later in Acts 22 and then vanishes from the record. But without Ananias the conversion of Saul might never have been completed. Ananias was the one who walked into the trap, laid hands on the persecutor, called him brother, and baptized him. The ministry of Ananias is the ministry most Christians are called to — not the ministry of an apostle but the ministry of a faithful disciple willing to do the next thing God asks even when it feels dangerous. The kingdom is full of Ananiases. They rarely make history. They are the reason history happens the way it does.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

Saul Preaches in Damascus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

Saul spends several days with the disciples in Damascus. Right away he begins to preach in the synagogues that Jesus is the Son of God. All who hear him are astonished and ask — is this not the man who raised havoc in Jerusalem among those who call on this name? Has he not come here to take them as prisoners to the leading priests? Yet Saul grows more and more powerful and baffles the Jews living in Damascus by proving that Jesus is the Messiah.

What You Might Not Know

Right away he began to preach — Saul did not wait for a period of quiet discipleship. He began preaching immediately. This was almost certainly too soon by later standards. The Paul who would write pastoral letters to Timothy decades later would counsel against appointing new converts to leadership. But Saul could not be quiet. The same intensity that had driven his persecution now drove his preaching. He had more knowledge of the Scriptures than any new convert before or since — he had been trained by Gamaliel. And he had just met the risen Jesus on the road. The combination was explosive.

Jesus is the Son of God — this is the first time in Acts this specific title is given to the content of apostolic preaching. Peter had preached Jesus as Messiah and Lord. Stephen had preached Jesus as the Righteous One and the Son of Man. Saul preaches Jesus as the Son of God. This title — Son of God — would become central to Paul's theology for the rest of his life. He opens Romans by describing Jesus as the Son of God declared by resurrection. The confession he made on his first days as a believer was the confession he would spend the rest of his life unpacking.

All those who heard him were astonished — the Jewish community of Damascus was not expecting this. Saul was supposed to be hunting Christians. He was preaching Christ. The shock was total. The man they had been warned about had arrived preaching the opposite of what he had come to do. Their confusion is captured in the question — is this not the man who raised havoc in Jerusalem. The name and face were the same. Everything else had changed.

Saul grew more and more powerful — the Greek word is enedunamouto which means to become empowered or strengthened. Saul was not getting weaker in his new faith. He was getting stronger. His preaching was becoming more effective and more convincing with time. The opposite is more common — new converts often start strong and fade. Saul started strong and kept strengthening. Something specific was happening in him that did not weaken with familiarity.

Baffled the Jews living in Damascus by proving that Jesus is the Messiah — the Greek word for baffled is suncheo which means to confound, confuse, or pour together. Saul's arguments from the Scriptures combining the messianic prophecies with the life and death and resurrection of Jesus could not be answered by the Damascus synagogues. They had heard these Scriptures their whole lives. They had not heard them read this way. Saul had been taught by Gamaliel to use Scripture with legal precision. Now he was using that precision to demonstrate that Jesus was the Messiah the Scriptures had always pointed to. The arguments were devastating.

Why It Still Matters

Saul went to Damascus to arrest Christians and ended up preaching Christ in their synagogues. God's providence sometimes turns the weapons of the opposition into the tools of the Gospel. The training Saul had received to defend Judaism against Jesus became the training he used to announce Jesus as the fulfillment of Judaism. The travel letters from the high priest brought him to the very city where he would first preach the Gospel. Not a single piece of Saul's preparation was wasted. It was all useful. Even his enmity toward the Church became part of his testimony. God works with what he has in front of him, including and especially the enemies he turns into servants.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

Saul Escapes and Goes to Jerusalem

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

After many days some Jews conspire to kill Saul. He learns of their plan. Day and night they watch the city gates to kill him. His followers take him by night and lower him in a basket through an opening in the wall. When he arrives in Jerusalem he tries to join the disciples but they are all afraid of him. They do not believe he is really a disciple. But Barnabas takes him and brings him to the apostles. He tells them how Saul had seen the Lord on his journey and that the Lord had spoken to him and how in Damascus he had preached fearlessly in the name of Jesus. Saul stays with them and moves about freely in Jerusalem speaking boldly in the name of the Lord. He talks and debates with the Greek-speaking Jews but they try to kill him. When the brothers learn of this they take him down to Caesarea and send him off to Tarsus.

What You Might Not Know

After many days — Luke's phrase covers a substantial period. In Galatians 1 Paul himself tells us that after his conversion he went to Arabia before returning to Damascus, and that three years passed before he went to Jerusalem. Luke compresses this. The many days of Acts 9 include an extended time Paul spent in Arabia — likely in the Nabatean kingdom south of Damascus — receiving direct revelation from the Lord. Paul's theology was not developed by committee in Jerusalem. It was developed in Arabia and Damascus during a time of direct encounter with Jesus. By the time he returned to Damascus and then to Jerusalem his message was fully formed.

Lowered him in a basket through an opening in the wall — Paul would later refer to this escape in 2 Corinthians 11 as his proudest humiliation. The great Pharisee who had come to Damascus with official letters had to be smuggled out in a basket. The word in Acts for the basket — spuris — refers to a large woven basket typically used for fish. Paul was lowered through a window in a house built into the city wall. The mighty hunter fled in the dark in a basket. He remembered it ever after as the beginning of what suffering for the Name would look like.

They were all afraid of him. They didn't believe he really was a disciple — the Jerusalem church's reaction was reasonable. Saul had done enormous damage there. Many of them had lost family members to his raids. They remembered Stephen's death. The sudden appearance of their former persecutor claiming to be a disciple could easily have been a trick — a way to infiltrate their gatherings and arrest more people. The fear was not paranoia. It was trauma.

Barnabas took him and brought him to the apostles — this is the Barnabas introduced in Chapter 4 as Son of Encouragement. He earned the name. When no one else in Jerusalem would vouch for Saul, Barnabas did. He took Saul — the Greek word suggests he took hold of him or grasped him — and brought him directly to the apostles. Barnabas staked his own reputation on what he believed about Saul. Without Barnabas's willingness to advocate, Saul might never have been received in Jerusalem at all. One of the great moments in the history of the Church was a man willing to vouch for a former enemy when no one else would.

He told them how Saul had seen the Lord — Barnabas carried Saul's testimony to the apostles. Whether Barnabas had been to Damascus, or had heard the story from others, or had spent time with Saul himself, Luke does not say. What is clear is that Barnabas had done the investigation, believed what he had learned, and was now vouching for Saul's authenticity. Good advocacy does not bypass verification. It follows verification. Barnabas did the work before he did the advocating.

He talked and debated with the Greek-speaking Jews — these were the Hellenists — Saul's own people culturally. The same Hellenistic synagogues that had argued with Stephen and lost now faced Saul. The pattern repeated itself. They could not answer him. They tried to kill him. Saul was experiencing from the Hellenists what he had once planned to do to others. The persecutor had become the persecuted. The intensity of the Hellenists against Stephen had now turned on Saul for exactly the opposite reason.

They took him down to Caesarea and sent him off to Tarsus — Saul was sent to his hometown. Tarsus was in Cilicia — a major Hellenistic city and the capital of the Roman province. The church in Jerusalem sent him home not as a punishment but as a protective measure. Luke leaves Saul in Tarsus. He will not reappear in Acts until Chapter 11 when Barnabas comes looking for him. Between these chapters several years passed — years Luke does not describe. Saul's hidden years in Cilicia were not wasted. They were a long formation that no one was writing down. Eventually Barnabas will remember that Saul was in Tarsus, come to find him, and bring him to Antioch. The hidden years ended when the church was ready for what Saul had been becoming in obscurity.

Why It Still Matters

Barnabas took him. Those three words have shaped Christian history. Without Barnabas's willingness to take Saul when no one else would, the apostle Paul as we know him might never have existed. The church had a former enemy claiming to be a brother and one man who had the courage to believe him. Barnabas was neither an apostle nor a major preacher nor an author of Scripture. But he was the man who could see what others could not see and risked his reputation to vouch for it. Every Saul in the history of the Church has needed a Barnabas. The ministry of taking in the difficult person, the former enemy, the one no one else trusts — that ministry is still the shape of the Gospel at work.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

The Church Grows in Peace

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

The church throughout Judea Galilee and Samaria enjoys a time of peace and is strengthened. Living in the fear of the Lord and encouraged by the Holy Spirit it grows in numbers.

What You Might Not Know

A time of peace — Luke provides a summary verse that covers a substantial period. The intense persecution driven by Saul had ended. Saul himself had been converted and removed to Tarsus. The Jewish religious authorities had other concerns — Roman political pressure and threats to Jewish life under the emperor Caligula demanded attention. The result was a season of peace for the Church. Not every season is a persecution season. God also gives seasons of rest during which the Church can deepen and grow.

Throughout Judea Galilee and Samaria — this geographic note is important. Luke has been tracking the spread of the Gospel through the geographic outline of Acts 1:8 — Jerusalem, Judea and Samaria, the ends of the earth. By this point the Church has filled the first two regions. There are believers throughout Judea, Galilee, and Samaria. The first two stages of Jesus's commission are essentially complete. The next chapters will move the story toward the third stage — the ends of the earth — starting with Peter's encounter with Cornelius.

Living in the fear of the Lord and encouraged by the Holy Spirit — Luke describes the inner life of the growing church in two phrases. Fear of the Lord — a reverent awareness of God's holy presence. Encouragement of the Spirit — the active ministry of the Paraclete in the community. The two belong together. Fear of the Lord without encouragement becomes grim religiosity. Encouragement without fear becomes shallow sentimentalism. The healthy church holds both.

It grew in numbers — growth continued during the peaceful period. Persecution had spread the Church geographically. Peace allowed the Church to deepen and multiply numerically. Both seasons contributed to what the Church became. The persecution season scattered the believers who carried the Gospel outward. The peace season consolidated what had been scattered and drew in new believers where the earlier scattering had planted seeds.

Why It Still Matters

The church had seasons of persecution and seasons of peace. Both were received from the same God. The season of persecution in Chapter 8 had scattered the believers and spread the Gospel. The season of peace in Chapter 9 strengthened and multiplied the Church. Neither season was the permanent condition. Neither was a sign of God's favor or disfavor. The Church learned to be faithful in both. That capacity — to be faithful in persecution and faithful in peace — is harder than it sounds. Many Christians are strong in one and weak in the other. The mature Church knows how to serve in both conditions and to receive both as gifts from the same hand.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

Peter Heals Aeneas in Lydda

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

As Peter travels about the country he visits the believers in Lydda. There he finds a man named Aeneas who is paralyzed and has been bedridden for eight years. Aeneas Peter says — Jesus the Messiah heals you. Get up and roll up your mat. Immediately Aeneas gets up. All those who live in Lydda and Sharon see him and turn to the Lord.

What You Might Not Know

As Peter traveled about the country — Luke shifts focus back to Peter. Saul has been sent to Tarsus. Now Peter is traveling through the coastal region preaching and visiting the scattered believers. Peter is not tied to Jerusalem. He is moving as an apostle among the churches. This section sets up Peter's eventual arrival in Joppa and from there to Caesarea where the Gentile mission will formally begin. Luke is positioning his pieces.

Lydda — this was a town on the coastal plain about twenty-five miles northwest of Jerusalem. It was on the main road from Jerusalem to the coast. Philip had likely been through this area during his coastal preaching tour in Chapter 8. By the time Peter arrives there is already a community of believers — the believers who lived in Lydda. Philip's scattered ministry had planted the seeds. Peter's itinerant ministry waters what has been planted.

Paralyzed and had been bedridden for eight years — the detail about the duration matters. Eight years of paralysis is a long condition. This was not a recent injury or a temporary illness. The community knew Aeneas as the man who had been unable to get out of bed for eight years. His healing would be instantly undeniable to everyone who knew him. Like the lame man at the Temple gate, the long visibility of his condition made the suddenness of his healing a public sign.

Jesus the Messiah heals you — Peter is careful to name who actually performs the healing. Not I heal you. Jesus the Messiah heals you. Peter stands in the long apostolic tradition of refusing to claim credit for what God does through him. The name invoked is Jesus. The authority is Jesus. Peter is the conduit through which Jesus heals.

Get up and roll up your mat — the command echoes Jesus's own words to the paralyzed man at the pool of Bethesda in John 5. Peter is not only speaking in Jesus's name. He is using Jesus's words. The continuity between the ministry of Jesus and the ministry of his apostles is deliberate. What Jesus did in Galilee his apostles are doing in Judea and Samaria. The same voice is speaking through the same kind of command producing the same kind of healing. The Church is not doing something different from what Jesus did. It is continuing what he began.

All those who lived in Lydda and Sharon saw him and turned to the Lord — the response is extraordinary. Not only did individuals believe. Whole regions turned. Lydda and the entire plain of Sharon saw what happened to Aeneas and responded by turning to the Lord. Luke does not say every single person converted but he does say the population broadly turned. A single healing in one town produced a regional awakening. The faithfulness of the believers who had lived in Lydda before Peter arrived had prepared the ground. The healing completed what their witness had been establishing.

Why It Still Matters

Eight years of paralysis and a single moment of healing. Long conditions sometimes end quickly when God acts. The slow years of waiting in apparently permanent trouble do not always mean permanent trouble. Aeneas spent eight years watching other people walk past his mat. On an ordinary day Peter came through town and everything changed. Most days are ordinary days. Some ordinary days turn out to be the day that changes a decade of waiting. That possibility is enough to keep hope alive through the long years when nothing is visibly happening.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

Peter Raises Tabitha from the Dead

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9
What Is Happening Here

In Joppa there is a disciple named Tabitha — in Greek Dorcas. She is always doing good and helping the poor. She becomes sick and dies. Her body is washed and placed in an upstairs room. Lydda is near Joppa so when the disciples hear Peter is in Lydda they send two men to urge him to come at once. Peter goes with them and when he arrives is taken upstairs. All the widows stand around him crying and showing him the robes and other clothing Dorcas had made while she was still with them. Peter sends them out of the room, gets down on his knees and prays. Turning toward the dead woman he says — Tabitha get up. She opens her eyes and sits up. He takes her by the hand and helps her to her feet. He calls the believers especially the widows and presents her alive. This becomes known all over Joppa and many believe in the Lord. Peter stays in Joppa for some time with a tanner named Simon.

What You Might Not Know

Tabitha — in Greek Dorcas — both names mean gazelle. Luke provides both her Aramaic name and her Greek name. Joppa was a coastal port city with a mixed Jewish and Gentile population. Tabitha was bilingual, known by both names depending on who was speaking about her. This cultural bilingualism marked the coastal cities Luke is describing — places that spoke Aramaic and Greek and where the Gospel would need to travel in both languages.

She was always doing good and helping the poor — Luke's introduction of Tabitha emphasizes her ministry. She is not introduced as someone with a gift or title. She is introduced as someone whose life was filled with good works and helping the poor. The Greek phrase describes continuous activity — she was always doing this. Her charity was not occasional. It was the pattern of her life. When she died the community lost a woman who had been quietly caring for them every day.

Her body was washed and placed in an upstairs room — Jewish burial custom required quick burial, usually within the same day. The fact that Tabitha's body had been washed but not yet buried tells us something. The community was preparing for burial but was also holding off. They had sent for Peter. The urgency of their message — please come at once — suggests they were hoping against hope that something might happen if Peter arrived in time.

All the widows stood around him crying — Luke describes the specific group most affected by Tabitha's death. The widows. Widows in the ancient world were economically vulnerable. Without a husband's income many lived in real poverty. Tabitha had been making clothes for them — the robes and other clothing Dorcas had made. Her death was not just the loss of a friend. It was the loss of their clothier, their care, perhaps their lifeline. The widows' grief was real and had practical as well as emotional dimensions.

Showing him the robes and other clothing Dorcas had made — this detail is touching. The widows showed Peter the physical evidence of what Tabitha had been to them. She had made these with her own hands for us. Look at these. This is what we are losing. Every stitch was a gesture of care. Every garment was a ministry. Tabitha's ministry was not preaching. It was sewing. And the grief of the community was measured in the number of garments in their hands.

Peter sent them all out of the room — this detail echoes Jesus's procedure when he raised Jairus's daughter in Mark 5. Jesus had sent the crowd out of the room before the raising. Peter does the same. He is not putting on a show. He is not performing for the grieving community. He clears the room, kneels, and prays.

Tabitha get up — Peter uses her Aramaic name. The command is simple and direct. Jesus had said Talitha koum to Jairus's daughter — little girl arise. Peter says Tabitha cumi — Tabitha arise. The words are almost identical. The servant is doing in Jesus's name what his master had done, using words that echo his master's words. The raising of the dead was one of the signs of the kingdom Jesus had demonstrated. Peter now demonstrates the same sign in the same way.

She opened her eyes and seeing Peter she sat up — the simplicity of the description matches the simplicity of what happened. There is no elaborate narration. She opened her eyes. She saw Peter. She sat up. He took her hand and helped her stand. The widows got their Tabitha back.

Peter stayed in Joppa for some time with a tanner named Simon — this detail at the end sets up Chapter 10. Simon the tanner was the host where Peter would receive the vision that would launch the Gentile mission. A tanner worked with dead animal skins — a ceremonially unclean occupation by Jewish standards. The fact that Peter was staying with a tanner already suggests that his strict scruples about ceremonial purity were softening. The next chapter will show how far that softening was about to go.

Why It Still Matters

Tabitha's ministry was sewing clothes for widows. Luke preserves her name and her story in the Scripture that has been read by every generation of the Church for two thousand years. The ministry that seemed small in her lifetime — making robes for poor widows in a coastal port city — turned out to be a ministry God thought worth recording forever. Not everyone will preach to thousands or plant churches across continents. Some will sew. Some will cook. Some will quietly care for the vulnerable every day. That ministry is not less than the other. Tabitha's life and death and resurrection are in the Bible because what she did mattered to God. What the world would have missed if she had stayed dead — and what God restored when she got up — was a ministry of garments and care. That was enough to be worth a miracle.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 9

Acts Chapter 10

Cornelius Receives a Vision

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10
What Is Happening Here

In Caesarea there is a man named Cornelius, a centurion in the Italian Regiment. He is a devout man who fears God as does everyone in his household. He gives generously to the poor and prays to God continually. One afternoon about three o'clock he has a vision. He clearly sees an angel of God who tells him his prayers and gifts to the poor have come up as an offering before God. He is told to send men to Joppa to bring back a man named Simon who is called Peter, staying with Simon the tanner whose house is by the sea. When the angel leaves Cornelius calls two servants and a devout soldier and tells them everything. He sends them to Joppa.

What You Might Not Know

Caesarea — this was the Roman provincial capital of Judea, built by Herod the Great and named in honor of Caesar Augustus. It was a thoroughly Roman city with a harbor, a theater, a hippodrome, and a large Gentile population. The Roman governor resided there. Roman soldiers were stationed there. For a Jewish person Caesarea represented the world of Roman power and Gentile culture. The fact that the Gentile mission begins in Caesarea rather than in a more Jewish-friendly setting is not incidental. God chose the most Roman city in Judea as the place where the wall between Jew and Gentile would first be formally broken down.

A centurion in what was known as the Italian Regiment — a centurion commanded approximately eighty to one hundred soldiers. The Italian Regiment was a cohort recruited from Italy rather than from local populations. Cornelius was career Roman military — the kind of man who represented everything the Jewish people had reason to resent and fear. He was an occupier. His presence in Judea was part of Rome's domination of Israel. And yet he was also a devout man who feared God, gave to the poor, and prayed continually.

A God-fearer — Cornelius is described as one who feared God. This was a recognized category in the ancient world. God-fearers were Gentiles who had been attracted to Jewish monotheism and ethics, attended synagogue, observed some Jewish practices, and gave to Jewish causes — but had not fully converted to Judaism through circumcision. There were many such people throughout the Roman world. They were drawn to the God of Israel but remained outside the full covenant community. Cornelius represents this large population of devout Gentiles who were spiritually serious but technically outside the people of God.

Your prayers and your gifts to the poor have come up as an offering before God — the angel's words to Cornelius are extraordinary. His prayers and charitable giving had been received by God as an offering — the language of Temple sacrifice. A Gentile's prayers had been accepted by the God of Israel. This statement is theologically significant before anything else happens in the chapter. God had been attending to Cornelius before Peter arrived. The Gospel did not introduce God to Cornelius. It brought Cornelius into full relationship with a God who had already been receiving his prayers.

About three o'clock — the same hour Peter and John were going up to the Temple for prayer in Chapter 3. The same hour Jesus died on the cross. The hour of the evening sacrifice. God met Cornelius at the hour of sacrifice — the hour of atonement. The timing was not random. It placed the Gentile centurion's vision at the moment in the daily Jewish calendar most associated with the forgiveness of sins. Something is about to happen for Cornelius that corresponds to what the Temple sacrifices had always pointed toward.

Why It Still Matters

Cornelius was a good man before Peter arrived. He was devout, generous, prayerful, and respected. The Gospel did not come to Cornelius because he was a wreck who needed saving from moral ruin. It came to him because even a good man's goodness cannot complete what only Jesus can complete. Cornelius had prayers that rose to God and gifts that reached the poor. What he did not yet have was the full knowledge of Jesus — the one through whom forgiveness and the Spirit are given. The Gospel is not only for the broken. It is for the devout and the searching who have not yet heard the name through which everything they have been reaching toward becomes available.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10

Peter's Vision on the Rooftop

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10
What Is Happening Here

The next day as the men from Cornelius are approaching Joppa Peter goes up on the roof to pray about noon. He becomes hungry and while the meal is being prepared he falls into a trance. He sees heaven opened and something like a large sheet let down to earth by its four corners containing all kinds of four-footed animals reptiles and birds. A voice tells him to get up kill and eat. Peter answers — surely not Lord. I have never eaten anything impure or unclean. The voice speaks a second time — do not call anything impure that God has made clean. This happens three times and the sheet is taken back to heaven.

What You Might Not Know

Peter went up on the roof to pray about noon — flat rooftops in Palestinian homes were used as living space and places of prayer. Peter was on the roof of a tanner's house by the sea. The tanner worked below with animal hides — an occupation that made the house ceremonially unclean by strict Jewish standards. Peter was already staying somewhere that a strict Pharisee would have avoided. God was preparing Peter for a larger shift by first placing him in a context that had already softened his ceremonial boundaries.

Something like a large sheet let down by its four corners — the vision is vivid and specific. The sheet came from heaven. It held every kind of animal — clean and unclean according to the dietary laws of Leviticus 11. The four corners may suggest the four corners of the earth — all nations, all peoples gathered together in one vessel lowered from above. The image was designed to shock Peter out of a framework he had held his entire life.

I have never eaten anything impure or unclean — Peter's protest reveals how deeply the Jewish food laws were embedded in his identity. He had followed Jesus for three years. He had seen Jesus heal Gentiles and speak with Samaritans. He had been present at Pentecost when every nation heard the Gospel. And still — the dietary laws held. The food laws were not just rules. They were markers of Jewish identity that separated Israel from the nations. Eating with Gentiles meant eating their food. For Peter that boundary had never been crossed.

Do not call anything impure that God has made clean — the voice does not argue about Leviticus. It makes a declaration. God has made clean. The present perfect tense matters. The cleansing has already happened. What God has already done Peter has no authority to reverse with his categories. The vision is not primarily about food. Peter will realize almost immediately that it is about people. The sheet full of unclean animals is a symbol for Gentiles — people Peter had been taught to regard as unclean and to avoid.

This happened three times — the repetition three times is significant. Three times Peter denied Jesus. Three times Jesus asked Peter do you love me to restore him. Now three times the vision is given to Peter before he will accept it. Peter needed to hear it three times to be sure. God was patient with Peter's slowness to receive something that overturned a lifetime of practice. The grace shown to Peter in the repetition is characteristic of how God works with people whose formed habits of mind take time to change.

Why It Still Matters

Do not call anything impure that God has made clean. This is one of the most important sentences in Acts. It did not just open the door for Peter to eat pork. It opened the door for the Gospel to reach every nation on earth. The categories of clean and unclean that had defined the boundary between Israel and the nations were being declared obsolete. Not because the Law was wrong. Because what the Law was pointing toward had arrived. The wall between peoples that the food laws had maintained was being taken down by the same God who had put it up. What God declares clean no human tradition has authority to call unclean again.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10

The Men from Cornelius Arrive

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10
What Is Happening Here

While Peter is still wondering about the vision the men sent by Cornelius find Simon's house and stop at the gate. They call out asking if Simon called Peter is staying there. While Peter is still thinking about the vision the Spirit tells him — three men are looking for you. Get up and go downstairs. Do not hesitate to go with them for I have sent them. Peter goes down and tells the men he is the one they are looking for. They explain they have come from Cornelius the centurion — a righteous and God-fearing man respected by all the Jewish people. A holy angel told him to ask Peter to come so he could hear what Peter has to say. Peter invites the men in as his guests.

What You Might Not Know

While Peter was still wondering about the meaning of the vision — the vision had ended but Peter had not yet understood it. The Spirit did not wait for Peter to figure it out on his own. The men arrived at the gate while he was still puzzling. God arranged the timing so that the interpretation of the vision arrived at the same moment as the people the vision was about. Peter would not have to reason his way to the application. The living illustration showed up at the gate while the symbol was still fresh in his mind.

The Spirit said to him Simon three men are looking for you — the Spirit speaks directly and specifically. Not a general prompting. A specific message. Three men. Go downstairs. Do not hesitate. I have sent them. The Spirit names the number of men before Peter has seen them. This is confirmation that what is about to happen is not Peter's initiative. It is God's initiative. Peter is being directed by the Spirit to do something he would not have done on his own.

Don't hesitate to go with them — the Greek word for hesitate is diakrinomenos which means to doubt, to discriminate, or to make distinctions. The same word will appear in Peter's defense of his actions in Chapter 11. The Spirit told him not to make the distinction he would normally make — the distinction between Jews who could be received and Gentiles who could not. God was overriding Peter's trained instinct to discriminate.

Peter invited the men in as his guests — this detail is easy to overlook but it is the first step of Peter's actual crossing of the boundary. He did not just agree to go with them. He invited them in. Three Gentile men — soldiers — were welcomed into the house where Peter was staying. They ate and slept there. Peter had already begun doing what the vision had commanded before he ever arrived at Cornelius's house. The boundary was being crossed step by step.

Why It Still Matters

I have sent them. The Spirit's identification of these Gentile men as sent by God is one of the most important statements in Acts. The men at the gate were not a spiritual threat to be avoided. They were messengers from God to be welcomed. That reframing — from potential contamination to divine messenger — is the reframing the vision had been preparing Peter for. When God sends someone to your door they deserve hospitality not suspicion. Peter got that right immediately. He invited them in before he fully understood everything that was about to unfold.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10

Peter Arrives at the House of Cornelius

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10
What Is Happening Here

The next day Peter starts out with them and some brothers from Joppa go along. The following day he arrives in Caesarea. Cornelius is expecting them and has called together his relatives and close friends. As Peter enters the house Cornelius meets him and falls at his feet in reverence. Peter makes him stand up — I am only a man myself. Peter goes inside and finds a large gathering of people. He tells them — you know it is against our law for a Jew to associate with or visit a Gentile. But God has shown him not to call anyone impure or unclean. So when sent for he came without objection. He asks why they sent for him. Cornelius tells him the whole story of the angel's visit four days earlier. Now we are all here in the presence of God to listen to everything the Lord has commanded you to tell us.

What You Might Not Know

Some of the brothers from Joppa went along — Peter did not go alone. He brought Jewish believers with him as witnesses. This was wise. What was about to happen would need witnesses who could testify to the Jerusalem church about what God had done. Peter knew he would have to answer for entering a Gentile home. Having witnesses who saw everything with their own eyes would be essential. Their presence was not just supportive. It was strategic preparation for the defense Peter would have to make in Chapter 11.

Cornelius fell at his feet in reverence — the Greek word proskuneo is the same word used for worship. Cornelius's response to Peter was essentially an act of worship. Peter corrected him immediately — stand up I am only a man myself. The early Church consistently refused divine honors. Peter healed the lame man and immediately redirected attention to Jesus. Paul and Barnabas will tear their clothes when the Lystrans try to sacrifice to them in Chapter 14. The apostles were clear and consistent — they were instruments not objects of veneration.

You know it's against our law for a Jew to associate with or visit a Gentile — Peter begins by naming the elephant in the room. He acknowledges the cultural and religious barrier he is crossing by being there. He does not pretend the barrier did not exist. He names it and then explains why he crossed it. The honesty is part of what makes his testimony credible. He is not pretending the old categories were wrong. He is explaining that God has shown him something new that supersedes them.

But God has shown me that I shouldn't call anyone impure or unclean — this is Peter's explicit interpretation of the vision. The sheet full of animals was not about food. It was about people. Peter understood this correctly and states it plainly. No person — regardless of ethnicity, background, or prior religious status — is to be called impure or unclean by the people of God. The vision that began on a rooftop in Joppa finds its intended meaning in a large room full of Gentile people in Caesarea.

Now we are all here in the presence of God — Cornelius's framing of the gathering is theologically precise. This is not a social visit. It is not a political meeting. It is a gathering in the presence of God to hear what the Lord has commanded Peter to say. Cornelius assembled his household and friends not for entertainment or curiosity but for a divine appointment. The readiness of this Gentile household to receive the word is a striking picture of prepared hearts waiting for the message that would complete what their prayers had already begun.

Why It Still Matters

Stand up — I am only a man myself. Peter's correction of Cornelius's worship is a template for every Christian leader who has ever been put on a pedestal. The temptation to accept elevated status — to receive the reverence that belongs only to God — is a temptation that has destroyed ministries and corrupted churches throughout history. Peter did not enjoy the moment and then gently redirect. He stopped it immediately. The man who had been given the keys of the kingdom refused to be worshiped. That refusal is part of what kept the early Church grounded in the reality that it was Jesus who was Lord — not his apostles.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10

Peter Preaches to the Gentiles

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10
What Is Happening Here

Peter begins to speak. He now realizes how true it is that God does not show favoritism but accepts from every nation the one who fears him and does what is right. He tells them about the message God sent to Israel — the good news of peace through Jesus the Messiah who is Lord of all. They know what happened throughout Judea beginning in Galilee after John's baptism — how God anointed Jesus of Nazareth with the Holy Spirit and power and how he went around doing good and healing all who were under the power of the devil because God was with him. The apostles are witnesses of everything he did in the country of the Jews and in Jerusalem. They killed him by hanging him on a cross but God raised him on the third day and caused him to be seen — not by all the people but by chosen witnesses who ate and drank with him after he rose. He commanded them to preach and testify that he is the one God appointed as judge of the living and the dead. All the prophets testify that everyone who believes in him receives forgiveness of sins through his name.

What You Might Not Know

I now realize how true it is that God doesn't show favoritism — Peter opens his sermon with a personal confession of learning. He now realizes something he had not fully grasped before. The word now is important. Something has changed in Peter's understanding. The vision on the rooftop, the men at the gate, the journey to Caesarea, the large gathering of Gentile people waiting to hear — all of it has produced a genuine theological shift. Peter is not delivering a prepared sermon. He is preaching from a place of fresh discovery.

But accepts from every nation the one who fears him and does what is right — this statement must be read carefully. Peter is not saying that every sincere religious person is already saved regardless of Jesus. The sermon that follows is specifically about Jesus — his life death resurrection and the forgiveness of sins available through his name. What Peter is saying is that God's acceptance is not racially or ethnically limited. The Cornelius-shaped person — devout, God-fearing, generous, prayerful — exists in every nation, and God receives such people as candidates for the Gospel. They are not already complete. They are ready.

The good news of peace through Jesus the Messiah who is Lord of all — the phrase Lord of all is significant in the context of a Roman centurion's household. Caesar was lord of all in the Roman world. Roman power expressed itself through the military men standing in Cornelius's house. Peter announces a Lord of all whose lordship supersedes Caesar's without being a political competitor to Rome. The peace Jesus brings is not the pax romana — enforced by military might. It is peace with God — accomplished by the cross and offered to every nation.

How he went around doing good and healing all who were under the power of the devil — Peter's summary of Jesus's ministry to Gentiles who had not been in Judea is brief and clear. Jesus healed. He did good. The power he opposed was demonic. The picture Peter paints is of a man going from place to place liberating people from darkness and disease. This is the most accessible framing of Jesus's ministry — not theological argument but narrative description of what he actually did.

By witnesses who ate and drank with him after he rose from the dead — Peter grounds the resurrection in physical evidence. The risen Jesus ate and drank with the apostles. The meals after the resurrection — fish on the beach in John 21, the breaking of bread on the road to Emmaus in Luke 24 — were not spiritual visions. They were physical meals with a physically risen person. The early Church's claim about the resurrection was always that it was bodily and historical. Peter states this plainly in a Gentile home.

He is the one God appointed as judge of the living and the dead — this is the first time in Acts that Jesus's role as universal judge is stated to a Gentile audience. The resurrection is not just good news about forgiveness. It is a declaration that Jesus is the one before whom every person who has ever lived will stand. The one Cornelius is hearing about is not just a Jewish teacher or a miracle worker. He is the judge of all humanity. That claim demands a response — which is exactly the response Peter will see in the next few verses.

Why It Still Matters

Everyone who believes in him receives forgiveness of sins through his name. This is the first time the word everyone appears in connection with the forgiveness available through Jesus in Acts. Not everyone in Israel. Not everyone who has been circumcised. Everyone who believes. The universality of the offer is stated plainly in a Gentile home to a Gentile audience. Peter did not water down the message for his audience. He declared the full scope of what Jesus offers to the people who needed most to hear that the offer included them. That pattern — the full Gospel for every audience — is the pattern apostolic preaching follows throughout Acts.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10

The Holy Spirit Falls on the Gentiles

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10
What Is Happening Here

While Peter is still speaking the Holy Spirit comes on all who hear the message. The Jewish believers who came with Peter are astonished that the gift of the Holy Spirit has been poured out even on Gentiles. For they hear them speaking in tongues and praising God. Peter says — surely no one can stand in the way of their being baptized with water. They have received the Holy Spirit just as we have. He orders them to be baptized in the name of Jesus the Messiah. Then they ask Peter to stay with them for a few days.

What You Might Not Know

While Peter was still speaking — the Spirit did not wait for Peter to finish his sermon and give an invitation. The Spirit fell while Peter was still talking. This is one of the most striking details in Acts 10. God was not waiting for the preacher to complete a theological argument before acting. The hearts in that room were prepared. The word was being spoken. The Spirit moved without being asked and without waiting for a formal response. The Gentile Pentecost arrived mid-sentence.

The Jewish believers who had come with Peter were astonished — the word astonished is the same word used elsewhere for being beside oneself with shock. These Jewish believers had come as witnesses. What they witnessed went far beyond what they had expected. They had seen the Spirit come at Pentecost. They had seen the Spirit come on the Samaritans. But Gentiles — uncircumcised Romans in a centurion's house — receiving the same gift they had received at Pentecost? This was not in their framework. The astonishment is a historical marker. Something had happened that no one in that room had predicted or planned for.

They heard them speaking in tongues and praising God — the evidence of the Spirit's coming on the Gentiles was the same as the evidence at Pentecost. The Jewish witnesses heard what they had heard before. They could not deny that what was happening to these Gentiles was identical to what had happened to them. The Spirit was not giving Gentiles a lesser version of what Jews had received. He was giving them the same gift in the same way. The equality of the giving was the point.

Surely no one can stand in the way of their being baptized with water — Peter's logic is immediate and practical. The Spirit has already come. The greater baptism has already happened. Who could possibly object to the lesser sign — the water baptism — following? The rhetorical question assumes the answer is no one. No human objection could override what God had just visibly done. Peter does not ask Jerusalem's permission. He does not send for instructions. He acts on what he has witnessed. God had already decided. The baptism was the human church catching up to what God had already accomplished.

They have received the Holy Spirit just as we have — this phrase will become the center of Peter's defense in Chapter 11 and the decisive argument at the Jerusalem Council in Chapter 15. Just as we have. The Gentiles received the same Spirit in the same way as the original Jewish believers at Pentecost. The equality of the gift is the foundation of the equality of the standing. If God gave them the same Spirit he gave us, who are we to treat them as anything less than equal members of the same community?

He ordered that they be baptized in the name of Jesus the Messiah — Peter orders the baptism. He does not perform it himself — he orders it, meaning others carried it out. The baptism of Cornelius's household was a communal act done in the name of Jesus. The same name that had been rejected by the Jerusalem establishment was now being declared over a Roman centurion and his household in the provincial capital. The name that the council had tried to suppress was spreading in exactly the direction they had tried to prevent.

They asked Peter to stay with them for a few days — the new Gentile believers immediately wanted more. They had heard the Gospel, received the Spirit, been baptized — and their first request was for Peter to stay and teach them further. The hunger for more of the word that follows genuine conversion is a consistent mark in Acts. The Spirit produces not just an experience but an appetite.

Why It Still Matters

Acts 10 is the chapter that made the universal Church possible. Without it the Gospel might have remained a Jewish sect. What happened in Cornelius's house — the Spirit falling on Gentiles before baptism, without circumcision, without Torah observance, on the basis of hearing and believing alone — established the principle that would drive the entire Gentile mission. Peter did not plan this. He did not argue for it theologically ahead of time. He witnessed it and then submitted to what God had done. The humility of Peter's response — God has clearly acted, therefore we must follow — is the model for how the Church handles the new things God does in every generation. When God moves the Church's job is to recognize it, not to prevent it.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 10

Acts Chapter 11

Peter Explains Himself in Jerusalem

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11
What Is Happening Here

The apostles and brothers throughout Judea hear that Gentiles have received the word of God. When Peter goes up to Jerusalem the Jewish believers criticize him — you went into the house of uncircumcised men and ate with them. Peter explains everything exactly as it happened. The vision of the sheet. The voice telling him not to call anything impure that God has made clean. The three men arriving from Caesarea at the exact moment the vision ended. The Spirit's command to go without hesitation. The six brothers who accompanied him. Cornelius's account of the angel. And then — as he began to speak the Holy Spirit came on them just as he had come on the apostles at the beginning. Peter remembered Jesus's words — John baptized with water but you will be baptized with the Holy Spirit. If God gave them the same gift he gave those who believed in the Lord Jesus the Messiah who was he to stand in God's way. When they hear this they have no further objections and praise God — so then God has granted even the Gentiles the chance to turn to him and live.

What You Might Not Know

The Jewish believers criticized him — the Greek word is diekrinonto meaning they contended with him or took issue with him. The criticism came not from unbelievers but from believers — Jewish Christians who had not yet understood what God was doing. This is important. The resistance to the Gentile mission came from inside the Church. These were not enemies of the Gospel.
