They were people whose understanding of the Gospel had not yet caught up to what God was actually doing. Peter did not dismiss them or shame them. He explained everything step by step and let the evidence speak.

You went into the house of uncircumcised men and ate with them — notice what the criticism was about. Not that Cornelius had believed. Not that Cornelius had been baptized. The criticism was about table fellowship — eating with Gentiles. Food was the boundary marker. Shared meals in the ancient world implied shared identity. For Jewish believers to eat with Gentiles was to blur the line between Israel and the nations that the Law had maintained for centuries. The critics were not wrong that a line had been crossed. They were wrong about whether that line should still exist.

These six brothers also went with me — Peter had brought six witnesses from Joppa. He names them not as character witnesses for himself but as corroborating testimony for what God had done. The six men could confirm every detail. Peter was not asking the Jerusalem church to take his word alone. He was presenting a case with multiple witnesses — exactly the standard Jewish law required for establishing a matter. Peter had anticipated the need to defend his actions before he had even taken them.

He'll bring you a message through which you and your whole household will be saved — Peter quotes the angel's words to Cornelius. This detail does not appear in Luke's original account of the Cornelius story in Chapter 10. Peter's retelling includes something Luke had not included in the original narrative. This is characteristic of Acts — when events are retold the retellings sometimes add details the original account omitted. Luke is not contradicting himself. He is showing how Peter recalled and emphasized certain details when making his defense.

As I began to speak the Holy Spirit came on them just as he had come on us at the beginning — the key phrase is at the beginning. Peter connects what happened in Cornelius's house directly to Pentecost. Not a lesser version. Not a Gentile adaptation. The same thing that happened at the beginning happened again in Caesarea. The beginning was the standard. Cornelius's household met that standard completely.

Who was I to stand in God's way — Peter's final question is one of the most important in Acts. He does not claim to have made a courageous decision. He claims to have recognized what God was already doing and gotten out of the way. The Gentile mission was not Peter's initiative. It was God's. Peter's role was to follow. That humility — I was not the decision-maker here, God was — is the theological foundation of everything the Gentile mission will build on.

God has granted even the Gentiles the chance to turn to him and live — the Jerusalem church's response is one of the great moments in Acts. They had criticized Peter when he arrived. After hearing his explanation they praise God. The criticism gives way to worship. This is the mature community doing something difficult — being willing to be changed by evidence they had not anticipated. They did not double down on their initial objection. They followed the evidence to a new conclusion and praised God for it.

Why It Still Matters

Peter's defense before the Jerusalem church is a model for how the Church handles unexpected movements of God. He did not act unilaterally and then hide it. He went to Jerusalem and explained everything in order step by step. He brought witnesses. He grounded his defense in Scripture and in the direct action of the Holy Spirit. And the community listened, weighed the evidence, and changed their minds. That combination — transparent accountability from the one who acted, and genuine openness from the community that was surprised — is the pattern of a healthy Church navigating something new. Neither side dug in. Both followed the evidence where it led.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11

The Gospel Spreads to Antioch

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11
What Is Happening Here

Those scattered by the persecution after Stephen's death travel as far as Phoenicia Cyprus and Antioch spreading the word only among Jews. But some men from Cyprus and Cyrene go to Antioch and begin speaking to the Greeks as well telling them the good news about the Lord Jesus. The hand of the Lord is with them and a great number of people believe and turn to the Lord. News reaches Jerusalem and they send Barnabas to Antioch. When he arrives and sees the evidence of God's grace he is glad and encourages them all to remain true to the Lord with all their hearts. He is a good man full of the Holy Spirit and faith and a great number of people are brought to the Lord.

What You Might Not Know

Spreading the word only among Jews — the default assumption of the scattered believers was still that the Gospel was for Jewish people. Even after Pentecost, even after Philip's ministry in Samaria, even after the Ethiopian eunuch, the majority of the scattered believers continued preaching only to Jews. The Gentile mission did not happen automatically or immediately. It happened in pockets, driven by specific people responding to specific moments. Most of the Church was still operating within the old framework even while God was breaking it down at the edges.

Men from Cyprus and Cyrene — these were Hellenistic Jews from the diaspora. They had grown up in Greek-speaking cities. They had Gentile neighbors and Gentile friends their whole lives. The cultural barrier between Jew and Gentile was lower for them than for the Jerusalem believers. What seemed like a revolutionary crossing of boundaries to a Jerusalem Jewish Christian may have seemed more natural to someone who had grown up in a city like Cyrene where Jews and Greeks lived side by side. God often uses people whose background has already prepared them for the next step the Church needs to take.

Antioch — this was the third largest city in the Roman Empire after Rome and Alexandria. It was the capital of the province of Syria and a major commercial hub. Its population was diverse — Romans Greeks Jews Syrians and people from across the eastern Mediterranean. It was a city where cultures mixed constantly. The choice of Antioch as the base for the Gentile mission was not random. It was the perfect place for a multiethnic church to form and from which to launch missions to the wider Gentile world.

The hand of the Lord was with them — this phrase from the Hebrew Bible indicates God's active empowerment. The same phrase is used of David and Solomon when God was working powerfully through them. Luke applies it to anonymous believers from Cyprus and Cyrene preaching to Greeks in Antioch. These men are not named. They are not apostles. They are scattered believers who made a decision to cross a cultural boundary with the Gospel and God showed up powerfully when they did. The unnamed evangelists of Antioch are among the most consequential figures in Christian history. They are remembered by their fruit not their names.

Barnabas went to Antioch — Jerusalem sent Barnabas rather than Peter or John. The choice was wise. Barnabas was a Levite from Cyprus — he had diaspora background and Hellenistic cultural sensitivity. He was also the man who had vouched for Saul when no one else would. He was a man with a proven track record of seeing potential others missed and building bridges others were unwilling to cross. He was exactly the right person for a young multiethnic church that needed affirming and strengthening.

When he arrived and saw the evidence of God's grace he was glad — Barnabas did not arrive in Antioch with a checklist of theological concerns to verify before approving what was happening. He arrived and saw it and was glad. His first response was joy not suspicion. That posture — looking for evidence of God's grace rather than looking for things to correct — is what made Barnabas the right man for Antioch. He saw grace and celebrated it.

He encouraged them all to remain true to the Lord with all their hearts — Barnabas's ministry in Antioch was encouragement and deepening. The church was new and growing rapidly. What it needed was someone to help it go deep rather than just wide. Barnabas provided that — calling the new believers to whole-hearted commitment rather than just initial response. The Son of Encouragement was doing exactly what his name said.

Why It Still Matters

A great number of people believed and turned to the Lord. The Antioch church grew because anonymous believers from Cyprus and Cyrene made a decision to cross a cultural boundary and speak to Greeks. They had no official commission. They had no apostolic authorization. They had the Gospel and the willingness to share it with people who were different from them. The largest expansion of the early Church happened through ordinary believers doing the obvious thing — telling the people around them about Jesus. That pattern has never changed. The most effective evangelism in every generation has been done by unnamed people who simply talked to their neighbors about the Lord.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11

Barnabas Brings Saul to Antioch

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11
What Is Happening Here

Barnabas goes to Tarsus to look for Saul and when he finds him brings him back to Antioch. For a whole year Barnabas and Saul meet with the church and teach great numbers of people. It is in Antioch that the disciples are first called Christians.

What You Might Not Know

Barnabas went to Tarsus to look for Saul — the Greek word for look for is anazateo meaning to search for carefully or to seek out. Barnabas did not casually pass through Tarsus and happen to find Saul. He made a deliberate journey to find a specific person for a specific purpose. Tarsus was Saul's hometown — a large city in Cilicia about a hundred and fifty miles from Antioch. The journey required real effort. Barnabas saw what the Antioch church needed and went to find the one person he knew could provide it.

The hidden years — Saul had been sent to Tarsus in Chapter 9 and has not appeared in Acts since. Between his departure and Barnabas finding him several years had passed — likely five to eight years depending on the chronology. Luke tells us nothing about those years. Paul's letters suggest he was active in ministry during this period — his letter to the Galatians mentions time in Syria and Cilicia. But the details are lost to history. What is clear is that Barnabas knew where Saul was and believed his gifts were exactly what Antioch needed. The man the Jerusalem church had sent away was being retrieved by the same man who had originally vouched for him.

For a whole year Barnabas and Saul met with the church and taught great numbers of people — a year of sustained teaching. Not a revival meeting or a short-term mission. A full year of regular instruction for large numbers of people. This was the formation of a theologically grounded church that could eventually send missionaries across the Roman world. The investment of a whole year in teaching produced a community with the depth to sustain the mission that would launch from Antioch in Chapter 13.

The disciples were first called Christians — the name Christian means follower of Christ or belonging to Christ. The suffix ian in Greek and Latin indicated belonging to a person or party — as in Herodians, followers of Herod. Christians meant people of Christ or Christ-people. This name was apparently given by outsiders in Antioch — the Greek-speaking population of the city observing this new community and giving them a label. Whether it was meant as mockery or simply as description we do not know. The community accepted it. Two thousand years later the name is still in use. The Antioch observers who coined the term had no idea they were naming a movement that would reshape the world.

Why It Still Matters

Barnabas went to find Saul because he recognized that the Antioch church needed what Saul had. This is one of the most important acts of discernment in the New Testament. Barnabas could have built the Antioch church around himself. He had the gifts and the reputation. Instead he identified what was missing and went to find the person who could supply it even though that person was largely unknown and had been sent away. That kind of leadership — building the team the church needs rather than the team that enhances the leader's own position — is the rarest and most valuable kind. Barnabas made Paul possible. He did it twice — once in Jerusalem and once in Tarsus. The world has rarely seen a more fruitful act of selfless recognition.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11

A Famine Is Predicted

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11
What Is Happening Here

During this time some prophets come down from Jerusalem to Antioch. One of them named Agabus stands up and through the Spirit predicts a severe famine that will spread over the entire Roman world. This happened during the reign of Claudius. The disciples each giving what they could decide to send help to the brothers living in Judea. They do this sending their gift to the elders by Barnabas and Saul.

What You Might Not Know

Prophets came down from Jerusalem to Antioch — this is the first mention in Acts of traveling prophets moving between churches. The gift of prophecy was an active and recognized ministry in the early Church. These were not people who had been ordained to a formal office. They were people with a recognized gift of Spirit-inspired speech operating across the network of churches. Agabus will appear again in Acts 21 prophesying about Paul's arrest in Jerusalem. He was a known and trusted prophetic voice.

A severe famine would spread over the entire Roman world — ancient sources confirm widespread famines during the reign of Claudius who ruled from 41 to 54 AD. The Jewish historian Josephus describes a severe famine in Judea around 46 AD. The historian Suetonius mentions repeated crop failures during Claudius's reign. Luke notes that this happened during the reign of Claudius — a rare explicit synchronization of Acts chronology with Roman history. The prophecy was fulfilled. Agabus was accurate.

Each giving what they could — the Greek phrase kath' horistos hekastos means according to what each one determined or as each one prospered. The giving was proportional and voluntary. No one was required to give a specific amount. No one was excluded from participating because they had little. Each person gave according to their means. This is the pattern of New Testament giving — proportional, voluntary, and motivated by genuine care for others rather than external compulsion.

Sending their gift to the elders — this is the first mention of elders as the leadership structure of the Jerusalem church. Earlier in Acts the apostles had been the central leadership. By this point elders — a recognized Jewish communal leadership structure — had been adopted by the Jerusalem church. The transition from apostolic direct leadership to an elder-based structure was already underway. Barnabas and Saul deliver the gift to the elders rather than to the apostles. The institutional shape of the Church was developing as it grew.

The Antioch church sending relief to Jerusalem — this is a remarkable reversal. Jerusalem had sent Barnabas to Antioch to affirm and strengthen the new Gentile church. Now the Gentile church in Antioch is sending financial relief to the Jewish church in Jerusalem. The relationship between the two churches was genuinely reciprocal. Jerusalem had given the Gospel and apostolic leadership. Antioch was returning material support in a time of crisis. That pattern of mutual interdependence between Jewish and Gentile believers is exactly what Paul will argue for theologically in Romans 15 — the Gentiles have shared in the spiritual blessings of Israel and they owe it to share their material blessings in return.

Why It Still Matters

The Antioch church heard that believers in Judea were going to face hardship and immediately decided to help. They did not wait to be asked. They did not debate whether it was their responsibility. They heard the need and responded. The Gentile church's first recorded act of generosity toward Jewish believers was the relief offering for the Judean famine. That act established a principle the early Church would return to again and again — the suffering of one part of the body is the concern of every part. Distance and cultural difference do not dissolve the obligation of care. The church in Antioch and the church in Jerusalem were one body. What happened to one happened to both. They acted accordingly.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 11

Acts Chapter 12

James Is Killed and Peter Is Arrested

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12
What Is Happening Here

King Herod begins to persecute some who belong to the church. He has James the brother of John put to death with the sword. When he sees that this pleases the people he arrests Peter as well during the Festival of Unleavened Bread. He puts Peter in prison guarded by four squads of four soldiers each. Herod intends to bring him out for public trial after Passover.

What You Might Not Know

King Herod — this is Herod Agrippa I, grandson of Herod the Great who had ordered the massacre of infants in Bethlehem. Agrippa had been raised in Rome and was personally close to the emperors Caligula and Claudius. He had eventually been given rule over most of his grandfather's territory. Unlike the Herods who had ruled before him Agrippa was genuinely popular with the Jewish people because he observed Jewish customs, supported the Temple, and presented himself as a devout Jew. His persecution of the church was at least partly motivated by a desire to maintain that popularity.

He had James the brother of John put to death with the sword — this is the only account in Acts of the death of one of the twelve apostles. Luke gives it one sentence. James the son of Zebedee who had been present at the Transfiguration, who had asked Jesus for a seat at his right hand in glory, who had fallen asleep in Gethsemane — is executed by a king trying to please a crowd and Luke records it in a single clause. The brevity is striking. James's death was not a theological crisis for Luke. It was a fact. The Gospel had always included the possibility of death for those who proclaimed it. James was the first of the twelve to die for the Name. He would not be the last.

When he saw that this pleased the people — the execution of James was politically motivated. Herod watched the public reaction and saw that killing an apostle was popular. He immediately moved to repeat the success by arresting Peter. The persecution of the Church was not driven by genuine theological conviction. It was driven by the calculation of a politician reading the crowd. That dynamic — religious persecution driven by political calculation rather than genuine principle — has repeated itself throughout history. Rulers who attack the Church for popularity rather than conviction tend to find that the Church outlasts them.

Four squads of four soldiers each — sixteen soldiers total to guard one fisherman. The detail reveals how seriously Herod took the threat of another escape. Perhaps he had heard about the previous jailbreak in Chapter 5. The security arrangements were extreme — two soldiers chained to Peter on either side, guards at the door, multiple squads rotating through. Human security arrangements that seemed impenetrable. They would prove insufficient.

During the Festival of Unleavened Bread — Passover season. The same season Jesus had been arrested and executed. The same season the Church had been born at Pentecost. Peter was arrested at Passover — the feast that commemorated Israel's deliverance from slavery in Egypt. Luke's readers would have noticed the timing. The one whose preaching had begun the new exodus was arrested at the feast of the old exodus. The parallel to Jesus's own arrest at Passover was unmistakable.

Why It Still Matters

James died and Peter was rescued. Both were apostles. Both were faithful. The outcome was different for each. Luke does not explain why James was killed and Peter was spared. He simply records both. The early Church had to learn to hold this — that faithfulness does not guarantee survival, that God sometimes rescues and sometimes does not, and that both outcomes are within his sovereignty. James's one-sentence death is as much a part of the story of the Church as Peter's miraculous release. The Church that can only trust God when he rescues has not yet learned to trust him fully.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12

Peter Is Freed from Prison

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12
What Is Happening Here

Peter is kept in prison while the church prays fervently to God for him. The night before his trial Peter is sleeping between two soldiers bound with two chains with guards at the entrance. An angel appears and light shines in the cell. The angel strikes Peter on the side and wakes him. Quick get up. The chains fall off his wrists. The angel tells him to put on his clothes and sandals and to wrap his cloak and follow. Peter follows but thinks he is seeing a vision. They pass the first and second guards and come to the iron gate leading to the city. It opens by itself and they go through. After walking the length of one street the angel leaves him. Peter comes to his senses. Now he knows for certain the Lord has sent his angel and rescued him from Herod's clutches and from everything the people were hoping would happen.

What You Might Not Know

The church was praying fervently to God for him — the word fervently translates ektenos meaning stretched out, strained, or intense. It is the same word used for Jesus's prayer in Gethsemane in Luke 22. The prayer of the Church for Peter was not casual petition. It was the kind of prayer that costs something — urgent, sustained, concentrated. Luke notes this before describing the rescue. The rescue did not happen in spite of prayer. It happened in the context of it. The connection between fervent prayer and God's action is not mechanical — James died despite whatever prayers were offered for him. But Luke records the praying as part of the story. It mattered.

Peter was sleeping — this detail is one of the most humanly vivid in Acts. The night before his expected execution Peter was asleep. Not sitting up in terror. Not pacing the cell. Asleep between two soldiers. The peace of a man who had settled something with God about his own life and death. Whether this was the peace of resignation or the peace of trust Luke does not say. But the image of a man asleep the night before his trial — chained to soldiers in a prison cell — has stayed with readers for two thousand years. Something about Peter had changed since he had denied Jesus three times and wept. He had learned to rest in what he could not control.

He thought he was seeing a vision — Peter's assumption that the rescue was a vision is understandable. He had seen visions before. The reality of the escape was so improbable that his mind defaulted to a visionary explanation. He was not fully conscious of what was happening until the angel left him and the night air of Jerusalem was real around him. The rescue was so far outside ordinary experience that it required the departure of the angel before Peter could accept that it was actually happening.

The iron gate opened by itself — this detail is specific and deliberate. The outer gate was not opened by the angel striking it or by a key. It opened on its own. The Greek word automatos — from which we get automatic — is used. The gate opened automatically. Every human barrier between Peter and freedom was removed — the chains, the guards, the locked doors, the iron gate — each one by a different means, all of them without Peter having to do anything. The rescue was entirely God's work. Peter followed. He did not escape.

Now I know for certain — the Greek word alethos means truly or really. Peter came to his senses and recognized reality. This was not a dream. The Lord had actually sent an angel and actually rescued him. The recognition of what God has just done — the moment when extraordinary grace becomes undeniable — is a recurring experience in the lives of the people Acts describes. Peter stood alone on a Jerusalem street in the middle of the night and recognized that God had just done something for him that he could not have done for himself.

Why It Still Matters

The chains fell off Peter's wrists. The iron gate opened by itself. Every barrier yielded. The detail that Peter was asleep when the angel arrived is the detail that carries the most weight for ordinary believers. He was not awake praying. He was not in a heightened spiritual state. He was asleep — and God acted anyway. The rescue of Peter is not a reward for Peter's spiritual preparation. It is the sovereign action of God on behalf of his servant regardless of what Peter was doing. Sometimes God acts while we are sleeping. The night before what looked like Peter's execution was also the night of his rescue. He didn't know which it was when he fell asleep. He trusted and slept. That is enough.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12

Peter Knocks at Mary's Door

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12
What Is Happening Here

Peter goes to the house of Mary the mother of John Mark where many people have gathered and are praying. He knocks at the outer entrance. A servant named Rhoda comes to answer the door. When she recognizes Peter's voice she is so overjoyed she runs back without opening the door and tells everyone — Peter is at the door. You are out of your mind they tell her. When she keeps insisting they say it must be his angel. But Peter keeps knocking and when they open the door and see him they are astonished. Peter motions for quiet and describes how the Lord brought him out of prison. Tell James and the other brothers about this he says and then leaves for another place.

What You Might Not Know

The house of Mary the mother of John Mark — this is the first appearance of John Mark in Acts. His mother Mary was a woman of means — her house was large enough to host a significant prayer gathering and had a gateway with a separate entrance and a servant. John Mark will travel with Barnabas and Saul on the first missionary journey and his abandonment of that journey will become the cause of a serious dispute between Paul and Barnabas in Chapter 15. His eventual rehabilitation is noted in Paul's later letters. His connection to Peter — referred to as my son Mark in 1 Peter — would eventually produce the Gospel of Mark.

Rhoda — this servant girl is one of the most charming figures in Acts. She is given a name when many more prominent people in Acts are not named. She recognizes Peter's voice, becomes so overwhelmed with joy that she forgets to open the door, runs back to announce what she has heard, is told she is crazy, insists she is right, and is eventually vindicated when everyone else opens the door and sees Peter standing there. Her joy was right. Her identification was right. The community that was praying for Peter's release did not believe the answer to their prayer when it arrived at the gate. Rhoda did.

It must be his angel — the idea that a person had a guardian angel who could appear in their form was a Jewish belief of the period. When the prayer group rejected Rhoda's report they defaulted to a supernatural explanation that fit their framework better than the obvious one — that the man they were praying for was standing at the door. The irony is complete. They were praying for Peter's release. God had released Peter. Their prayers were being answered while they stood inside telling the girl who had seen the answer that she was out of her mind.

Tell James and the other brothers about this — the James Peter singles out is James the brother of Jesus not James the son of Zebedee who has just been executed. James the brother of Jesus is emerging as the leader of the Jerusalem church. Peter's instruction to report to James confirms that by this point James had become the primary leader in Jerusalem. Peter was the traveling apostle. James was the resident leader. Their different roles would become clearer at the Jerusalem Council in Chapter 15.

He left for another place — Luke does not tell us where Peter went. The vagueness is probably deliberate — if Herod was searching for Peter naming his destination would have been dangerous. Peter disappears from Acts at this point and does not reappear until the Jerusalem Council in Chapter 15. The man who had been the central figure of the first twelve chapters steps back. The second half of Acts belongs to Paul. Peter's exit from the narrative is quiet and unheroic — he knocked on a door, told his story, gave instructions, and left in the dark for an undisclosed location.

Why It Still Matters

They were praying for Peter and did not believe he was at the door when God answered. That gap — between praying for something and recognizing the answer when it arrives — is one of the most honest observations in Acts. The prayer group was not hypocritical. They were genuinely gathered and genuinely praying. But the answer came in a form and at a speed they had not expected and they almost missed it. Rhoda's joy was the right response. The community's skepticism was the understandable one. Both are recorded because both are true of how people actually pray and receive. The answer to prayer is sometimes standing at the gate while the people who prayed for it are inside arguing about whether it is possible.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12

Herod Searches for Peter

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12
What Is Happening Here

In the morning there is no small commotion among the soldiers about what has become of Peter. After Herod has a thorough search made and does not find him he cross-examines the guards and orders that they be executed. Then Herod goes from Judea to Caesarea and stays there.

What You Might Not Know

No small commotion — Luke's understatement is almost dry humor. Sixteen soldiers had been assigned to guard one prisoner. The prisoner was gone. The chains were still in the cell. The doors were still locked. The guards were still at their posts. No one had seen anything. The commotion was probably considerable. The soldiers knew what the consequences of losing a prisoner were under Roman military law. Their cross-examination and execution were not unusual — Roman law held guards personally responsible for prisoners who escaped. The men who had been assigned to prevent Peter's escape paid for that escape with their lives. It is one of the uncomfortable edges of the rescue story that Luke does not resolve.

Herod went from Judea to Caesarea — the shift in location is significant. Herod's base of power was moving. Caesarea was the Roman administrative center. The king who had tried to strengthen his position with the Jewish people by executing James and arresting Peter was now dealing with other political pressures — a dispute with Tyre and Sidon that required attention. The Church was not Herod's only problem. It was not even his biggest one. Rulers who oppose the work of God tend to find that their opposition consumes political capital while the work continues without them.

Why It Still Matters

Herod searched for Peter and could not find him. The most powerful man in Judea with the full resources of a Roman-backed monarchy could not locate one escaped prisoner. The limits of human power against the purposes of God are quietly on display in this brief section. Herod's thorough search produced nothing. His execution of the guards changed nothing about where Peter was. The king raged. The prisoner remained free. The word of God continued to spread. The contrast between Herod's frantic searching and the calm continuation of the Gospel is part of what Luke is showing his readers. Kingdoms that oppose God tend to exhaust themselves in the effort.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12

The Death of Herod

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12
What Is Happening Here

Herod has been quarreling with the people of Tyre and Sidon. They seek an audience with him and after securing the support of Blastus a trusted aide ask for peace because they depend on Herod's territory for their food supply. On the appointed day Herod wearing his royal robes sits on his throne and delivers a public address. The people shout — this is the voice of a god not of a man. Immediately because Herod does not give praise to God an angel of the Lord strikes him down and he is eaten by worms and dies.

What You Might Not Know

Herod had been quarreling with the people of Tyre and Sidon — these were major Phoenician port cities on the Mediterranean coast that were economically dependent on Herod's territory for grain. The dispute had economic stakes on both sides. The delegation from Tyre and Sidon was seeking a diplomatic resolution — peace in exchange for continued trade. The occasion that would kill Herod was a diplomatic public appearance arranged to settle a trade dispute. The mundane political machinery of the ancient world became the setting for divine judgment.

Wearing his royal robes sat on his throne and delivered a public address — the Jewish historian Josephus describes this same event. He says Herod wore a garment made entirely of silver that shone brilliantly in the early morning sun. The people who saw him gleaming in his silver robe began to call out that he was more than human. Josephus and Luke agree on the essential detail — Herod accepted divine honor that belonged only to God and was struck down immediately. The agreement between Luke and Josephus on this event is one of the places where the historical reliability of Acts is confirmed by an independent ancient source.

Because Herod did not give praise to God — the reason for the judgment is stated plainly. Herod heard the crowd calling him a god and did not correct them. Compare this to Peter in Chapter 10 telling Cornelius to stand up — I am only a man. Compare it to Paul and Barnabas tearing their clothes in Chapter 14 when the Lystrans try to worship them. The apostles consistently and immediately refused divine honors. Herod accepted them. The contrast is the point Luke is making. The men who preached in the name of Jesus refused glory. The king who killed them accepted it. The outcomes were correspondingly different.

He was eaten by worms and died — the Greek word skolekobrotos means literally consumed by worms. The man who had executed James with the sword and thrown Peter in prison died of a worm infestation. The contrast between his silver robe and his death is the contrast between the pretension of earthly power and its ultimate fragility. Herod the king who robed himself in silver and accepted worship as a god was eaten by worms. The grandiosity of his self-presentation and the ignominy of his death are placed side by side without comment. Luke lets the contrast speak for itself.

Why It Still Matters

Herod did not give praise to God. That simple failure — receiving glory that belongs to God without redirecting it to its proper source — is the deepest form of idolatry. It is not worship of a carved image. It is the acceptance of a position that belongs only to God. Herod may not have believed he was a god. He simply did not refuse the claim when it was made. The willingness to let the adulation stand — to enjoy the moment without correcting it — is the sin Luke identifies. The judgment was immediate. The principle is permanent. Human beings who position themselves where only God belongs find that position untenable in the end.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12

The Word of God Keeps Spreading

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12
What Is Happening Here

But the word of God continued to spread and flourish. When Barnabas and Saul had finished their mission they returned from Jerusalem taking with them John also called Mark.

What You Might Not Know

But the word of God continued to spread and flourish — this summary verse follows immediately after Herod's death. The contrast is deliberate. Herod killed James. Herod arrested Peter. Herod was struck down and eaten by worms. The word of God continued to spread and flourish. Luke has placed the summary in exactly the right position. The king who opposed the Gospel is dead. The Gospel is not. The word outlasted the king. That pattern — the Gospel outlasting every power that tries to stop it — is one of the organizing convictions of the entire book of Acts.

The word of God continued to spread and flourish — the Greek word auxano means to grow or increase. It is a word from agriculture — the growth of a plant. Luke uses it several times in Acts for the spread of the Gospel. The image is organic rather than mechanical. The Gospel grows the way a living thing grows — not by force or political power but by the nature of what it is. The death of Herod removed one obstacle. The word grew on. It would have grown on regardless because growth is what living things do when they are healthy.

Taking with them John also called Mark — the casual mention of John Mark at the end of the chapter sets up Chapter 13. Barnabas and Saul are returning to Antioch. They bring Mark with them. Mark will accompany them on the first missionary journey and then leave before it is completed. That departure will have serious consequences. Luke introduces Mark here without comment — just a name at the end of a sentence. By the end of the next chapter the name will carry weight.

Why It Still Matters

But the word of God continued to spread and flourish. The word but is the most important word in the verse. Herod had done everything a powerful king could do to stop the movement. He had executed a leading apostle. He had imprisoned another. He had used his soldiers, his courts, and his public platform against the Church. And then he died eaten by worms. But the word of God continued. The but signals that everything Herod attempted was irrelevant to the ultimate outcome. Human power deployed against the Gospel is not nothing — James is dead, and that is real. But it is also not enough — the word continues. That combination of honest acknowledgment of loss and confident declaration of the Gospel's continuation is the note on which Luke closes Chapter 12. It is the note the Church has returned to in every generation of opposition since.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 12

Acts Chapter 13

Barnabas and Saul Are Sent Out

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13
What Is Happening Here

In the church at Antioch there are prophets and teachers — Barnabas, Simeon called Niger, Lucius from Cyrene, Manaen who had been brought up with Herod the tetrarch, and Saul. While they are worshiping the Lord and fasting the Holy Spirit says — set apart Barnabas and Saul for the work I have called them to do. After fasting and praying they place their hands on them and send them off.

What You Might Not Know

Prophets and teachers — the Antioch church had a leadership team of five men from remarkably diverse backgrounds. Barnabas was a Levite from Cyprus. Simeon called Niger — the Latin word for black — was likely a man of African descent, possibly from North Africa. Lucius was from Cyrene in modern Libya. Manaen had been raised alongside Herod Antipas the tetrarch who had executed John the Baptist — meaning he had grown up in the household of one of the most powerful and corrupt rulers in the region. Saul was a Pharisee from Tarsus trained by Gamaliel. The leadership team of the church that would launch the Gentile mission looked like the world it was about to reach.

While they were worshiping the Lord and fasting — the commission for the first missionary journey came during a time of corporate worship and fasting. Not during a strategy meeting. Not after a committee reviewed the budget. During worship. The word for worshiping is leitourgounton from which we get the word liturgy — a word that describes formal public service or ministry to God. The church was serving God in worship and God spoke in the middle of it. The direction for the mission came from God's initiative in the context of the church's devotion.

Set apart Barnabas and Saul for me — the commission was specific. Not a general call to mission. Specific people, specifically named, specifically set apart. The Holy Spirit identified who was to go. The church did not vote or nominate. They heard the Spirit's direction and confirmed it. The two men being sent were the two most prominent leaders in the Antioch church. The community was not sending its spare capacity. It was releasing its best people at the Spirit's direction.

For the work I have called them to do — the work had already been called before it was publicly commissioned. God had been preparing Barnabas and Saul for this before the church heard it in worship. Paul's letter to the Galatians says God set him apart from his mother's womb. The commissioning in Antioch was the public recognition of a calling that had been in place long before this moment. The church was not originating the mission. It was releasing what God had already initiated.

They placed their hands on them and sent them off — the laying on of hands was an act of identification and blessing. The community was identifying itself with the mission by laying hands on the missionaries. The sending was communal — the church sent them, not just the Spirit. Both are true simultaneously. The Spirit said go. The church prayed, fasted, laid hands on them, and sent them. The divine initiative and the human community act together. This is the shape of apostolic mission in Acts — Spirit-initiated, church-sent, personally committed.

Why It Still Matters

The first missionary journey began in worship. The direction came from God speaking to a community that was fasting and seeking him together. Not from a visionary leader with a strategic plan. Not from a wealthy donor with a specific interest. From the Holy Spirit speaking to worshipers. The Antioch model of mission — leadership teams of diverse backgrounds, communal discernment through worship and fasting, Spirit-initiated direction, church-confirmed sending — has been the healthiest model of mission in every generation since. Missions that begin this way tend to go somewhere worth going. Missions that begin otherwise tend to drift toward the agenda of the most powerful person in the room.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13

On the Island of Cyprus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13
What Is Happening Here

Sent out by the Holy Spirit they go to Seleucia and sail to Cyprus. They arrive at Salamis and preach in the Jewish synagogues with John as their helper. They meet a Jewish sorcerer and false prophet named Bar-Jesus who is an attendant of the governor Sergius Paulus. The governor is an intelligent man who sends for Barnabas and Saul because he wants to hear the word of God. But Elymas the sorcerer opposes them and tries to turn the governor away from the faith. Saul — also called Paul — filled with the Holy Spirit looks straight at Elymas and declares him a child of the devil full of lies and tricks who will not stop twisting the Lord's straight paths. The Lord's hand is against him — he will be blind and unable to see the sun for a while. Immediately mist and darkness come over Elymas and he gropes around looking for someone to lead him by the hand. The governor sees what has happened and believes because he is amazed at the teaching about the Lord.

What You Might Not Know

Cyprus — Barnabas was from Cyprus according to Acts 4. The first stop on the first missionary journey was Barnabas's home island. The team began where at least one of them had roots and relationships. This is a wise missionary strategy — beginning in territory where someone on the team has prior connections and cultural familiarity. The Gospel traveled along existing networks before it moved into entirely new territory.

Saul who was also called Paul — this is the moment in Acts where the name transition happens. From this point forward Luke calls him Paul rather than Saul. Several explanations have been offered. Paul was his Roman name — many Jews of the diaspora had both a Hebrew name and a Roman name. The transition may simply reflect that as Paul moves into the Gentile world his Roman name becomes more prominent. It may also signal a narrative transition — the man who was Saul the persecutor has fully become Paul the apostle to the Gentiles. Luke does not explain the transition. He simply makes it here and continues with Paul for the rest of Acts.

Bar-Jesus whose other name was Elymas — Bar-Jesus means son of Jesus or son of Joshua in Aramaic. The irony of a false prophet opposing the Gospel bearing a name that means son of Jesus would not have been lost on Luke's readers. Elymas is likely a translation or interpretation of his name meaning sorcerer or wise man in Arabic. He occupied an influential position as an attendant — a court advisor — to the Roman governor. His opposition to Paul and Barnabas was not random hostility. It was the self-interested resistance of a man whose influence would be threatened if the governor believed the Gospel.

You are a child of the devil — Paul's confrontation of Elymas is the most direct denunciation in Acts outside of Stephen's confrontation of the council in Chapter 7. Paul does not debate Elymas or try to persuade him. He names what he sees — a man working actively against the Gospel, twisting the straight paths of the Lord, opposing the work of God in the life of someone who was genuinely seeking. The directness was proportional to the stakes. A man's soul was hanging in the balance and another man was trying to prevent him from hearing the truth.

You will be blind for a while — the blindness of Elymas deliberately echoes Paul's own blindness on the road to Damascus. The man who had been blinded by encountering the living Jesus now pronounces blindness on a man who is blocking others from encountering him. The symmetry is pointed. Paul knew from the inside what it meant to be blind and then to see. The temporary blindness of Elymas was not only judgment. It was potentially an invitation — the same kind of blindness that had preceded Paul's own transformation.

Sergius Paulus was an intelligent man — the Roman governor who believed is described specifically as intelligent. Luke is making an implicit argument. The Gospel was not accepted only by the uneducated or the desperate. The first recorded Gentile convert on the missionary journey was a Roman proconsul — a sophisticated, well-educated imperial official who evaluated what he saw and heard and concluded it was true. The amazement that produced his faith was amazement at the teaching — not just at the miracle of Elymas's blindness. The content of the Gospel convinced him alongside the demonstration of its power.

Why It Still Matters

Elymas tried to turn the governor away from the faith. The opposition to the Gospel in Acts is rarely just intellectual disagreement. It is usually self-interested resistance — people whose position, income, or influence would be threatened by the Gospel taking root in the people around them. Elymas was not a philosophical skeptic raising honest questions. He was a man protecting his access to power by keeping a powerful man from hearing the truth. That pattern — opposition to the Gospel driven by self-interest rather than honest inquiry — is still the most common form of resistance the Church encounters. Recognizing it for what it is matters for how to respond to it.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13

In Pisidian Antioch

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13
What Is Happening Here

From Paphos Paul and his companions sail to Perga in Pamphylia. There John leaves them and goes back to Jerusalem. From Perga they go on to Pisidian Antioch. On the Sabbath they go into the synagogue and sit down. After the reading from the Law and the Prophets the synagogue leaders invite them to speak a word of encouragement if they have one.

What You Might Not Know

John left them and went back to Jerusalem — this brief notice will have significant consequences. John Mark's departure from the mission at Perga becomes the cause of the split between Paul and Barnabas in Chapter 15. Luke gives no reason for the departure here. Was Mark sick? Homesick? Afraid of the difficult journey into the interior of Asia Minor? Theologically uncomfortable with the direction the mission was taking? We do not know. Luke records the departure without explanation. Paul's later refusal to take Mark on a second journey suggests he regarded the departure as a failure of commitment. Barnabas eventually rehabilitated Mark and traveled separately with him. Both responses are recorded without editorial judgment. Something happened at Perga that the text does not fully explain.

The journey from Perga to Pisidian Antioch was a serious undertaking. The road climbed through the Taurus mountains — steep, dangerous terrain known for bandits and difficult conditions. Paul's letter to the Galatians may reference an illness he suffered in this region. The missionaries who pressed on after Mark left were walking into some of the most physically demanding terrain of the entire journey. The ease of Cyprus had given way to something considerably harder.

Pisidian Antioch — this was a Roman colony in the region of Pisidia near the border of Galatia. It was not the same as Syrian Antioch where the missionaries had been sent from. It was a strategically located city on a major Roman road with a substantial Jewish community and a significant Gentile population of God-fearers who attended the synagogue. Paul consistently chose strategically located cities as bases for his missionary work — places where roads converged and populations mixed. Pisidian Antioch fit that pattern perfectly.

After the reading from the Law and the Prophets — the synagogue service followed a set pattern. Scripture was read from the Torah and from the Prophets. Then there was typically an opportunity for a visiting teacher or rabbi to speak. Paul and Barnabas were invited into an existing pattern of worship that gave them a natural platform. The Jewish synagogue system — with its network of communities across the Roman world, its habit of reading Scripture together, and its openness to visiting teachers — provided Paul with a ready-made entry point into every city he visited. He used it consistently until the synagogue doors closed against him.

Why It Still Matters

Please speak if you have a word of encouragement for the people. The synagogue leaders issued an open invitation to traveling teachers. They did not know what they were inviting when they gave the floor to Paul. The word encouragement is paraklesis — the same word used for the work of the Holy Spirit as Paraclete. They asked for a word of comfort and strengthening. What they received was the most comprehensive presentation of the Gospel Paul gives anywhere in Acts. Sometimes an open invitation to speak produces more than the person who issued the invitation expected. The synagogue at Pisidian Antioch asked for encouragement and received the message that would divide the city.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13

Paul Preaches in the Synagogue

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13
What Is Happening Here

Paul stands up, motions with his hand, and addresses both Israelites and Gentile God-fearers. He traces the history of Israel from Egypt through the wilderness the conquest and the judges to Samuel and the monarchy. Saul was given when Israel asked for a king. After removing Saul God gave them David — a man after his own heart who would do everything God wanted. From David's descendants God has brought Israel the Savior Jesus as promised. Before Jesus came John preached repentance and baptism and pointed to the one coming after him whose sandals he was not worthy to untie. The people of Jerusalem and their rulers did not recognize Jesus but in condemning him they fulfilled the prophets read every Sabbath. Though they found no proper reason for a death sentence they had Pilate execute him. When everything written about him had been carried out they took him down and laid him in a tomb. But God raised him from the dead and for many days he was seen by those who had traveled with him from Galilee to Jerusalem — now his witnesses to the people. What God promised the ancestors he has fulfilled for their children by raising Jesus. Psalm 2 — you are my Son today I have become your Father. He will never decay as the holy and sure blessings promised to David. The Holy One will not see decay. David served his generation and died and decayed. But the one God raised did not see decay. Through Jesus the forgiveness of sins is announced. Through him everyone who believes is set free from every sin — a freedom the law of Moses could not give. A warning from the prophets — do not be mockers who die in amazement at what God is doing in their days that they would never believe even if told.

What You Might Not Know

Paul's sermon in Pisidian Antioch is the most complete example of his early preaching preserved in Acts. It follows the same pattern as Peter's Pentecost sermon — beginning with Israel's history, connecting it to Jesus through the Scriptures, announcing the resurrection, and calling for a response. But Paul's sermon has a distinctively Pauline theological addition that Peter's Pentecost sermon does not — the declaration that through Jesus everyone who believes is set free from every sin in a way the law of Moses could not provide. That addition is the seed of everything Paul will develop theologically in Romans and Galatians.

God put up with them in the wilderness — Paul's summary of Israel's history includes the failures honestly. He does not present a sanitized version. He notes the period of wilderness wandering — the years of complaint and rebellion and God's patient forbearance. Paul is following Stephen's pattern from Chapter 7 — reading Israel's history with honest eyes rather than with the selective memory that sees only the glorious moments. The audience listening to Paul was hearing their own story told with more honesty than they may have been accustomed to.

A man after my own heart who will do everything I want — David is presented as the pivot of the story. Everything from Abraham to David was building toward the Davidic line. The promise to David — that one of his descendants would reign forever — is what Paul is about to say has been fulfilled in Jesus. The sermon moves from Israel's history to David to Jesus in a continuous line. The resurrection of Jesus is the fulfillment of the Davidic promise. The Son of David has been raised and seated at the right hand of God — exactly what the Psalms had said about the Davidic king.

In condemning him they fulfilled the words of the prophets that are read every Sabbath — this is one of Paul's most pointed observations. The people who condemned Jesus were reading the prophecies about the Messiah every week in their synagogues. They read the words but did not see the fulfillment standing in front of them. The condemnation was not despite the prophecies — it was in fulfillment of them. God was working through the ignorance and hostility of Jerusalem's leaders to accomplish exactly what the prophets had predicted.

A freedom you couldn't get through the law of Moses — this is the theological distinctive that sets Paul's preaching apart from Peter's. Peter had preached forgiveness of sins through Jesus. Paul preaches that same forgiveness and adds — the law of Moses could not give you this. The law defined sin. It could not remove it. It could not justify the sinner before God. Only Jesus could do that. This claim — that Jesus accomplishes what the law could not — will become the center of Paul's theology and the source of his most intense conflicts with Jewish Christians who believed the law still had a role in justification.

What you would never believe even if someone told you — Paul ends his sermon with a warning from Habakkuk 1. The prophet had warned Israel about a coming judgment so unexpected that people would refuse to believe it even when told. Paul applies this to the response to the Gospel. If the people in this synagogue dismiss what they have just heard they will be among the mockers who die in amazement at what God was doing around them while they refused to see it. The warning is serious. The invitation is real. The response is their choice.

Why It Still Matters

Through him everyone who believes is set free from every sin — a freedom you couldn't get through the law of Moses. Paul's addition to the standard apostolic proclamation is the theological heart of his entire ministry. The law reveals sin. It cannot remove it. The best the law can do is show a person what they are and what they owe. Jesus does what the law cannot — he justifies the one who believes. The freedom Paul announces is not freedom from moral responsibility. It is freedom from the condemnation that comes from failing to meet a standard no human being can consistently meet. That freedom — complete, not partial, available to everyone who believes — is still the most radical offer in the world.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13

The Gentiles Respond with Joy

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13
What Is Happening Here

As Paul and Barnabas are leaving the synagogue the people invite them to speak more the next Sabbath. Many Jews and devout converts follow them and Paul and Barnabas urge them to continue in the grace of God. On the next Sabbath almost the whole city gathers to hear the word of the Lord. The Jewish leaders see the crowds and are filled with jealousy. They speak abusively against Paul and insult him. Paul and Barnabas answer boldly — they had to speak the word of God to the Jews first. But since the Jews reject it and do not consider themselves worthy of eternal life they are turning to the Gentiles. This is what the Lord commanded — I have made you a light for the Gentiles to bring salvation to the ends of the earth. When the Gentiles hear this they are glad and praise the word of the Lord. And all who were appointed for eternal life believed.

What You Might Not Know

Almost the whole city gathered — the response between the first and second Sabbath was remarkable. Within one week the word had spread from the synagogue congregation to virtually the entire city. The people who had heard Paul speak or heard others describing what they had heard wanted to come themselves. The city was drawn to what it was hearing. The scale of the response was the very thing that provoked the Jewish leaders' jealousy. Paul's message was reaching people the synagogue had been unable to reach — and reaching them faster than anything the religious establishment had ever produced.

Filled with jealousy — Luke identifies the motive of the opposition directly. Not theological conviction. Jealousy. The same word used for Herod's motive in Chapter 12. The Jewish leaders were not primarily concerned that Paul was theologically wrong. They were concerned that he was drawing the crowds that should have been theirs. The Gospel's popularity threatened their position. That combination — theological objection driven by social or positional threat — is one of the most common forms of religious opposition in every generation.

We had to speak the word of God to you first — Paul's explanation of his missionary method is explicit. The Gospel goes to the Jewish community first. This is not a preference or a cultural habit. It is a theological obligation rooted in the covenant — to Israel first and then to the nations. Paul follows this pattern in every city he enters. He goes to the synagogue. He preaches. When the synagogue rejects the message he turns to the Gentiles. The pattern is consistent and deliberate.

Since you reject it and do not consider yourselves worthy of eternal life — the phrase do not consider yourselves worthy is careful. Paul is not saying the Jewish leaders are actually unworthy of eternal life. He is saying their rejection of the Gospel is a self-exclusion. They are treating themselves as people who do not need what God is offering. The judgment on them comes not from God arbitrarily excluding them but from their own refusal of what was freely offered.

I have made you a light for the Gentiles — Paul quotes Isaiah 49 which was a servant song about the mission of God's servant to bring salvation to the ends of the earth. The servant in Isaiah was Israel collectively and ultimately the Messiah specifically. Paul applies it to his own mission — he and Barnabas are fulfilling the servant's vocation by bringing salvation to the Gentiles. This is not Paul inventing a new mission. It is Paul recognizing that the ancient vocation of Israel — to be a light to the nations — is now being fulfilled through the Church's mission to the Gentiles.

All who were appointed for eternal life believed — this is one of the most theologically loaded phrases in Acts. Luke attributes the belief of the Gentiles to divine appointment. Those who were appointed believed. This is not a statement that others were appointed for damnation. It is a statement that the faith of the Gentile believers was not finally their own achievement. Their believing was itself a gift — the result of God's prior work in them. Paul will develop this theology extensively in his letters. Luke states it as a simple observation of what he saw. Not everyone who heard believed. Of those who believed, all of them had been appointed.

Why It Still Matters

When the Gentiles heard this they were glad and praised the word of the Lord. The Gentile response to being explicitly included in the offer of salvation was joy. Not obligation. Not cautious acceptance. Gladness. The people who had always been on the outside of the covenant — who had attended the synagogue as God-fearers without full membership, who had respected the God of Israel without being able to fully belong to his people — heard that the salvation of Israel's God was for them too and their response was joy and praise. That response tells you something about how deep the longing had been. The Gospel lands as good news precisely where people have known most acutely what they were missing.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13

Paul and Barnabas Are Driven Out

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13
What Is Happening Here

The word of the Lord spreads through the whole region. But the Jewish leaders stir up the prominent religious women and leading men of the city and start a persecution against Paul and Barnabas driving them out of the area. Paul and Barnabas shake the dust off their feet as a warning to them and go to Iconium. And the disciples are filled with joy and with the Holy Spirit.

What You Might Not Know

The word of the Lord spread through the whole region — before the persecution came the word had already done its work across a wide area. The persecution could drive Paul and Barnabas out of the city. It could not undo the spread of the Gospel that had already happened. The pattern of Acts is consistent — the opposition comes after the word has already taken root, not before. By the time Paul and Barnabas are driven out the seed has been planted too widely to be uprooted.

They stirred up the prominent women of religion and the leading men of the city — the Jewish leaders used their social networks to mobilize opposition. They approached the most influential women in the city's religious community and the most prominent civic leaders. In a Roman colonial city these connections could produce real political pressure. The opposition was not a street mob. It was an organized campaign using existing social and political relationships. The method was sophisticated even if the motive was jealousy.

Prominent women of religion — this phrase is sometimes translated devout women of high standing. These were women who were God-fearers — Gentiles attached to the Jewish community through worship and philanthropy. They had social influence and civic connections. Their mobilization against Paul and Barnabas is a reminder that religious opposition to the Gospel has never been limited to one gender or social class. The most effective opposition often comes from people with religious seriousness and social standing who feel threatened by what the Gospel is doing.

Shook the dust off their feet — Jesus had instructed his disciples to do this when a town rejected their message in Luke 9 and 10. The gesture was a symbolic act with Jewish roots — Jews returning to Israel from Gentile territory would shake off the dust of the foreign land to leave its defilement behind. Paul and Barnabas are treating Pisidian Antioch as a place that has rejected what was offered and leaving its judgment in its own hands. The gesture was a warning — what happens to this city now is between the city and God. The messengers have delivered the message and are moving on.

The disciples were filled with joy and with the Holy Spirit — the disciples left behind in Pisidian Antioch after Paul and Barnabas were driven out were not devastated. They were filled with joy and the Holy Spirit. The persecution that had driven out the missionaries had not broken the new community. If anything it had strengthened it. The same Spirit that had empowered the mission was now sustaining the community the mission had produced. New believers left without their teachers were not abandoned. They had the Spirit.

Why It Still Matters

The disciples were filled with joy and with the Holy Spirit. Paul and Barnabas had been driven out of the city. By ordinary measures the mission had failed — the missionaries expelled, the community left without leadership, the opposition in control. And yet the disciples left behind were filled with joy and the Holy Spirit. The measure of a mission's success is not whether the missionaries got to stay. It is whether the Spirit stayed with those who believed. The Spirit stayed. The community was not only intact — it was joyful. What looks like the failure of a mission from the outside can be the confirmation of its success from the inside. The fruit was there. It had roots. It would last.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 13

Acts Chapter 14

Paul and Barnabas in Iconium

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14
What Is Happening Here

In Iconium Paul and Barnabas go as usual into the Jewish synagogue. They speak so effectively that a great number of both Jews and Greeks believe. But the Jews who refuse to believe stir up the Gentiles and poison their minds against the brothers. Paul and Barnabas spend considerable time there speaking boldly for the Lord who confirms the message of his grace by enabling them to perform signs and wonders. The people of the city are divided — some side with the Jews others with the apostles. A plot forms among both Gentiles and Jews together with their leaders to mistreat and stone them. They find out about it and flee to the Lycaonian cities of Lystra and Derbe and the surrounding country where they continue to preach the good news.

What You Might Not Know

They went as usual into the Jewish synagogue — the phrase as usual captures Paul's consistent missionary method. Every new city, same first step. Go to the synagogue. This was not just strategy. It was theological conviction. The Gospel was for the Jew first. The community that already had the Scriptures was the community that most needed to hear that the Scriptures had been fulfilled. Paul never abandoned this pattern even after repeated rejection. He was driven to the synagogue by something deeper than habit.

Poisoned their minds — the Greek word ekakosan means to embitter or corrupt. The opposition was not just persuasion. It was active contamination of the community's attitude toward Paul and Barnabas. The opponents worked on the emotions and loyalties of the Gentile population — people who had no prior investment in the theological argument but who could be turned against the missionaries through sustained social pressure and false characterization. This kind of opposition — working on the crowd's feelings rather than engaging the argument — is one of the most effective forms of resistance to the Gospel and one of the hardest to counter.

They spent considerable time there — despite the growing opposition Paul and Barnabas stayed. They did not leave at the first sign of trouble. The signs and wonders God enabled were confirmation that staying was the right decision. The length of time they invested in a hostile city reflects a conviction that the people there mattered enough to stay for even when the environment was difficult. Mission that only happens in favorable conditions is not the mission Acts describes.

The people of the city were divided — division is consistently the result of the Gospel's arrival in Acts. Not everyone believes. Not everyone rejects. The city splits along the line the Gospel draws. Jesus had said he came not to bring peace but a sword — not because conflict was his goal but because truth divides people who respond to it differently. The division at Iconium was not a failure of Paul's preaching. It was evidence that the preaching had been heard and that people were being required to make a genuine choice.

A plot by both Gentiles and Jews together — Iconium produced something unusual. The Jewish opposition and the Gentile opposition joined forces. Two communities that were normally in tension with each other found common cause in opposing the Gospel. The unity of the opposition was a measure of how threatening the Gospel felt to both communities. The message that crossed the boundary between Jew and Gentile by making both equally welcome in Christ was perceived as a threat by both communities' gatekeepers.

They found out about it and fled — Paul and Barnabas did not stay and seek martyrdom. They left when they learned the plot. This is consistent with Jesus's instruction in Matthew 10 — when they persecute you in one city flee to the next. Faithful mission is not the same as reckless exposure to danger. The missionaries used wisdom to preserve their lives for continued ministry. The Gospel needed them alive in the next city more than it needed them dead in this one.

Why It Still Matters

The Lord confirmed the message of his grace by enabling them to perform signs and wonders. The signs were not the message. They confirmed the message. The distinction is important. Paul and Barnabas were not miracle workers who attracted crowds and then preached. They were preachers whose message God confirmed with signs. The signs pointed beyond themselves to the truth of what was being proclaimed. A mission built on signs tends to collapse when the signs stop. A mission built on the word — confirmed by signs — endures because the word endures. Paul and Barnabas kept that order right even when the signs were dramatic.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14

A Lame Man Is Healed in Lystra

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14
What Is Happening Here

In Lystra there is a man who has been lame from birth and has never walked. He listens to Paul speaking. Paul looks directly at him and sees that he has faith to be healed. He calls out — stand up on your feet. The man jumps up and begins to walk.

What You Might Not Know

Lystra was a Roman colony in the region of Lycaonia — a rural area with a strong indigenous culture and its own language. Unlike the previous cities which had established Jewish communities with synagogues, Lystra appears to have had no synagogue. Paul was preaching in a different kind of setting — to a largely Gentile population without the Jewish Scriptural framework that his previous audiences had brought with them. The healing of the lame man was the entry point for this community because words alone could not bridge the cultural gap as quickly.

Paul looked directly at him — the same language used for Peter looking directly at the lame man at the Temple gate in Chapter 3. The deliberate gaze that sees the person specifically rather than the crowd generally. Paul was not performing a miracle for the audience. He was responding to what he saw in one specific person — a man listening intently, a man with faith.

He saw that he had faith to be healed — this is one of the rare places in Acts where the faith of the person being healed is specifically noted as the occasion for the healing. The lame man at the Temple in Chapter 3 had not asked for healing and his faith is not mentioned. This man's faith was visible to Paul — something in his attentiveness, his response to the preaching, communicated to Paul that this was the moment. The healing was not random. It was a specific response to a specific person's specific faith.

Stand up on your feet — the command is direct and public. Shouted out in the middle of Paul's preaching. The Greek word for stand up is anástēthi — the same root as resurrection, anastasis. Stand up — rise up. The word Paul uses to command the lame man to walk is the word the New Testament uses for resurrection from the dead. The healing is a small picture of the larger reality Paul had been preaching about — the God who raises the dead was now raising this man's useless legs.

Why It Still Matters

The man had been lame from birth and had never walked. The limitation was complete and lifelong. Paul did not offer physical therapy or gradual improvement. He called out a command and the man jumped up. The God Paul had been preaching about — the one who raised Jesus from the dead — was now demonstrating in front of the whole city that he could do what no human healer could do. The healing was a visual sermon. The crowd that had been listening to words they may not have fully understood now saw the power behind those words in a form no one could miss. The preaching and the sign belonged together.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14

The Crowd Mistakes Paul and Barnabas for Gods

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14
What Is Happening Here

When the crowd sees what Paul has done they shout in the Lycaonian language — the gods have come down to us in human form. They call Barnabas Zeus and Paul Hermes because he is the chief speaker. The priest of Zeus whose temple is just outside the city brings bulls and wreaths to the city gates because he and the crowd want to offer sacrifices to them. When Barnabas and Paul hear of this they tear their clothes and rush out into the crowd. Friends why are you doing this — they are only human just like the crowd. They are bringing good news telling them to turn from worthless things to the living God who made heaven earth sea and everything in them. In the past God let all nations go their own way. Yet he never left himself without a witness — he showed kindness by giving rain and crops and plenty of food and joy. Even with these words they have difficulty keeping the crowd from sacrificing to them.

What You Might Not Know

They shouted in the Lycaonian language — the crowd's reaction was in their own indigenous language which Paul and Barnabas did not understand. This is why the apostles did not immediately realize what was happening. The preparations for sacrifice were underway while Paul and Barnabas were unaware. By the time someone translated or explained what was happening the priest of Zeus was already approaching with bulls and garlands. The cultural and linguistic gap between the missionaries and the Lystran crowd nearly produced a disaster.

Zeus and Hermes — in Greek mythology Zeus was the king of the gods and Hermes was his messenger and the god of communication and travel. The identification of Barnabas as Zeus and Paul as Hermes reflected the crowd's observation — Barnabas was the more imposing physical presence and Paul was the primary speaker. The local tradition in Lystra held that Zeus and Hermes had once visited the region in human form. The story was told in the poet Ovid's Metamorphoses — two divine visitors had been turned away by everyone except an elderly couple named Philemon and Baucis who received them hospitably. The rest of the population had suffered for their inhospitality. The Lystrans were not going to make that mistake again.

They tore their clothes — this was the standard Jewish expression of horror at blasphemy. When the high priest tore his clothes at Jesus's trial he was expressing outrage at what he considered blasphemy. Paul and Barnabas tore their clothes at what they considered blasphemy — the worship of human beings. The gesture was instinctive and immediate. They were not calculating a response. They were horrified and their bodies showed it before their words did.

We are only human just like you — Paul and Barnabas refused divine honors immediately and completely. Compare this to Herod in Chapter 12 who accepted the crowd's acclamation of divinity and died for it. The contrast Luke has set up is deliberate. The men who preached in the name of Jesus refused what Herod accepted. The king who claimed divine status was eaten by worms. The apostles who rejected divine status went on to preach the Gospel across the Roman world.

Paul's sermon to the Lystrans is different from every other sermon in Acts. There is no appeal to the Hebrew Scriptures. There is no reference to Israel's history. The Lystrans did not have that framework. Paul begins where they are — with the natural world, with rain and harvests and food and joy. He is arguing from creation to Creator. The living God who made everything has been present to all peoples through the evidence of his provision even when they did not know his name. This is the earliest version of what Paul will develop more fully in Romans 1 and 2 — that God has not left himself without witness even among those who have not received special revelation.

In the past he let all nations go their own way — Paul does not condemn the Lystrans for their polytheism. He describes it as God allowing them to go their own way — a temporary situation that is now being corrected by the arrival of the Gospel. The patience of God with Gentile ignorance is being replaced by direct proclamation. The time of overlooking is ending. That transition — from God permitting Gentile idolatry to God calling all people everywhere to repent — will be stated even more explicitly in Paul's Areopagus speech in Chapter 17.

Why It Still Matters

We are bringing you good news telling you to turn from these worthless things to the living God. Paul's description of the idols as worthless things is direct but not contemptuous. He is not mocking the Lystrans for what they have believed. He is telling them plainly that what they have been worshiping cannot do what they need done. The living God who makes rain and produces harvests and fills hearts with joy is real. The statues are not. That contrast — between the God who actually sustains life and the objects that cannot — is the foundation of every missionary encounter with people who have not yet heard the Gospel. The question is never whether people are spiritually serious. It is whether what they are serious about is real.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14

Paul Is Stoned and Left for Dead

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 14
What Is Happening Here

Then some Jews come from Antioch and Iconium and turn the crowd against Paul and Barnabas. They stone Paul and drag him outside the city thinking he is dead. But after the disciples gather around him he gets up and goes back into the city. The next day he and Barnabas leave for Derbe.

What You Might Not Know

Jews came from Antioch and Iconium — the opposition followed Paul across cities. The Jewish leaders who had driven Paul out of Pisidian Antioch and plotted against him in Iconium traveled to Lystra to continue their campaign.
