The city that the ancient world admired for its wisdom was from Paul's perspective a city drowning in idolatry. His distress was the distress of someone who loves both God and people and cannot be indifferent when he sees both dishonored.

Epicureans and Stoics — the two dominant philosophical schools of Paul's era held fundamentally different views about God and the good life. Epicureans believed the gods were distant and uninvolved in human affairs, that the highest good was pleasure understood as the absence of pain and anxiety, and that death was simply the end — no afterlife, no judgment. Stoics believed in a rational divine principle permeating the universe, in living according to nature and reason, and in the virtue of the disciplined mind. Both schools had parts of their worldview that Paul could use as bridges and parts that his message would directly challenge. His sermon at the Areopagus engages both.

What is this babbler trying to say — the Greek word spermologos literally means seed-picker — a bird that picks up scattered seeds. It was used as a slang term for a person who picks up scraps of ideas from different places and cobbles them together without real understanding. The Athenian philosophers were dismissing Paul as an intellectual lightweight who had gathered bits and pieces of ideas he did not fully comprehend. The condescension of the most educated city in the world toward a traveling Jewish tentmaker was considerable. Paul would show them otherwise.

He seems to be promoting foreign gods — the accusation echoed the charge against Socrates — introducing foreign deities to Athens. The irony was sharp. Athens was proud of its hospitality to ideas. The city that prided itself on openness to every new thought was about to try to dismiss the most important new thought it had ever heard by comparing it to the charge that had gotten their greatest philosopher executed.

Why It Still Matters

Paul did not leave Athens when the philosophers called him a babbler. He did not retreat to the synagogue where he had a more sympathetic audience. He stayed in the marketplace reasoning with whoever would engage. The intellectual dismissal that the Athenians offered was not a reason to stop. It was a reason to find a clearer way to communicate. Paul's response to condescension was not withdrawal. It was more careful, more contextual, more patient engagement. The Areopagus speech he was about to deliver was the most carefully constructed piece of contextual communication in Acts. It began with what the Athenians already knew and moved toward what they needed to hear.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 17

Paul Speaks at the Areopagus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 17
What Is Happening Here

Paul stands up at the Areopagus and addresses the people of Athens. He observes they are very religious in every way — even finding an altar inscribed TO AN UNKNOWN GOD. He will tell them about this God they worship without knowing who he is. The God who made the world and everything in it is the Lord of heaven and earth. He does not live in temples built by human hands. He is not served by human hands as if he needed anything. He himself gives everyone life and breath and everything else. From one man he made all the nations to inhabit the whole earth. He marked out their appointed times and the boundaries of their lands so that people would seek him and perhaps reach out for him and find him — though he is not far from any one of us. As one of their own poets said — in him we live and move and have our being. Another said — we are his offspring. Since they are God's offspring they should not think the Divine Being is like gold or silver or stone shaped by human design. In the past God overlooked such ignorance but now he commands all people everywhere to turn away from their sins. He has set a day when he will judge the world with justice through the man he has appointed — and he has given proof of this to everyone by raising him from the dead.

What You Might Not Know

The Areopagus — this was the ancient council of Athens that met on the hill of Ares (Mars Hill) west of the Acropolis. In the classical era it had been the supreme court of Athens. By Paul's time its formal judicial powers had been reduced but it retained authority over matters of religion and education. Paul was not being put on trial. He was being given a formal hearing before the body that decided what teachings could be introduced to Athens. The audience was not a random crowd. It was the intellectual and civic leadership of the most philosophically sophisticated city in the world.

An altar with the inscription TO AN UNKNOWN GOD — this altar is mentioned by several ancient writers. The Athenians, being thorough in their religious hedging, had erected altars to gods they might have accidentally overlooked. The existence of such an altar was an admission built into the city's own religious practice — that their polytheism might be incomplete, that there might be a God they did not know. Paul did not invent a bridge. He found one the Athenians had built themselves and walked across it.

He doesn't live in temples built by human hands — Paul quotes the Athenians' own philosophical tradition back to them. The Stoics and many Greek philosophers had long argued that the divine could not be contained in physical structures. The critique of temple religion was not new to his audience. Paul was using their own intellectual tradition to establish common ground. He was not asking them to abandon everything they knew. He was showing them that their best thinking was pointing toward something their religion had not yet reached.

From one man he made all the nations — this statement has profound implications. If all humanity descended from one source then the divisions between Greek and barbarian, Roman and foreigner, Jew and Gentile, were not ontological. They were historical and cultural. All people share the same origin. All people are made in the image of the same God. The unity of humanity under one Creator was not just a theological claim. It was the basis for the universality of the Gospel's offer and the universality of the coming judgment.

As one of your own poets has said — Paul quotes from Aratus's Phaenomena and possibly from Cleanthes's Hymn to Zeus. Both were Stoic texts. Paul is not endorsing Stoic theology. He is finding in texts his audience already accepted statements that pointed toward the truth he was proclaiming. The method is careful and respectful. He begins with what they know and trust and shows how it points beyond itself. This is not compromise. It is communication — meeting people where they are and leading them toward where they need to go.

In the past God overlooked such ignorance but now he commands all people everywhere to turn away from their sins — this is the turn in the sermon. Everything before it was building toward this. The God who made everything and has been seeking all nations through the evidence of creation and human conscience is no longer content to be unknown. The arrival of Jesus — announced by the resurrection — has ended the era of overlooked ignorance. The universal command to repent is grounded in the universal lordship of the risen Jesus who has been appointed as the judge of all. Paul has moved his audience from their own poets to the resurrection of a specific person in history.

He has given proof of this to everyone by raising him from the dead — Paul names the resurrection as the proof of everything he has been arguing. Not a philosophical argument. Not a consensus among wise men. A specific historical event — the raising of a specific man from the dead — as the publicly verifiable evidence that the unknown God has revealed himself and that judgment is coming. The resurrection is not a mythological symbol for Paul. It is the pivot on which everything else turns. Whatever the Athenians made of the rest of the sermon the resurrection was the claim that required a response.

Why It Still Matters

Paul's Areopagus speech is the model for engaging sophisticated secular thought with the Gospel. He did not begin with condemnation of Greek philosophy. He did not ignore it. He read it carefully enough to find the places where it was reaching toward something it could not quite grasp and used those reaching points as bridges. The altar to the unknown God was the Athenians' own admission that their system was incomplete. Paul filled the gap. That approach — finding what is true and good in a culture and showing how the Gospel completes rather than merely contradicts it — is the most durable model of cultural engagement the Church has. It requires knowing the culture well enough to read it generously without surrendering the distinctives of the Gospel. Paul knew Greek philosophy well enough to quote it accurately. He never let it replace the resurrection.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 17

The Response in Athens

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 17
What Is Happening Here

When they hear about the resurrection of the dead some sneer. Others say they want to hear Paul again on this subject. Paul leaves the meeting. Some people become followers and believe — among them Dionysius a member of the Areopagus a woman named Damaris and a number of others.

What You Might Not Know

Some sneered — the resurrection was the sticking point for Greek philosophy. Greek thought generally held that the body was a prison for the soul — the goal of the philosophically enlightened person was to escape the body at death, not return to it. The idea of bodily resurrection was not just unusual to Greek ears. It was philosophically repugnant. The Epicureans had no place for any afterlife. The Stoics had no place for individual bodily resurrection. When Paul got to the resurrection the philosophical framework that had been nodding along suddenly hit a wall. The sneering was the sound of a worldview colliding with a claim it could not accommodate.

We want to hear you again on this subject — not everyone sneered. Some were genuinely curious and wanted to continue the conversation. The Areopagus speech did not produce mass conversion. It produced a divided response — some dismissive, some curious, some believing. Luke presents all three responses without apology. The most carefully constructed sermon in Acts produced the smallest recorded response. Athens was the intellectually hardest soil Paul had encountered. The Gospel's power is not dependent on the sophistication of the presentation or the receptivity of the audience.

Dionysius a member of the Areopagus — one of the council members who had been Paul's audience became a believer. A man of civic authority and intellectual standing in the most philosophically prestigious city in the ancient world was among those who believed. The early church historian Eusebius records that Dionysius later became the first bishop of Athens. His conversion was not typical — most of the Areopagus dismissed the resurrection. But one came.

A woman named Damaris — she is named alongside Dionysius as a specific individual who believed. We know nothing more about her than her name and her belief. She was present at the Areopagus — which was unusual for a woman in that context. Her inclusion by name suggests she was a person of some standing. Luke names her because she mattered. The small Athenian church began with a council member and a named woman and a number of others. It was a modest beginning.

Paul did not establish a church in Athens — or if he did Luke does not mention it. Athens receives less narrative attention than Thessalonica, Berea, Philippi, or Corinth which follows immediately. Some have suggested the Athens mission was a relative failure — too much philosophy not enough straightforward proclamation of Christ crucified. Paul's first letter to the Corinthians where he writes about coming in weakness and fear and trembling, determined to know nothing except Christ crucified, may reflect a course correction after Athens. Whether Athens was a failure or simply a harder field Luke records honestly — some sneered some were curious some believed. Paul left and moved on to Corinth.

Why It Still Matters

Some sneered but others said we want to hear you again and some believed. Three responses to the best-prepared sermon in Acts. The diversity of response is not a measure of the sermon's quality. It is a measure of the freedom God gives every person to respond to the truth as they choose. The Gospel does not compel belief. It invites it, argues for it, demonstrates it, and then leaves the response to each person. Paul gave the Athenians everything they needed to believe. Some sneered. One council member believed. A woman named Damaris believed. A number of others believed. That was enough. The church in Athens began with those few. The rest of the city made its own choice. The Gospel's job is not to force agreement. It is to make agreement possible and to leave the choice genuinely free.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 17

Acts Chapter 18

Paul in Corinth

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18
What Is Happening Here

Paul leaves Athens and goes to Corinth where he meets a Jewish couple named Aquila and Priscilla who have recently come from Italy because Claudius had ordered all Jews to leave Rome. Paul stays and works with them because they are all tentmakers. Every Sabbath he reasons in the synagogue trying to persuade Jews and Greeks. When Silas and Timothy arrive from Macedonia Paul devotes himself completely to preaching and testifying that Jesus is the Messiah. When the Jews oppose him and become abusive he shakes out his clothes in protest — your blood be on your own heads I am innocent of it from now on I will go to the Gentiles. He leaves the synagogue and goes next door to the house of Titius Justus a worshiper of God. Crispus the synagogue leader and his entire household believe in the Lord and many Corinthians who hear Paul believe and are baptized.

What You Might Not Know

Corinth was one of the most strategically important cities in the ancient world. Situated on the narrow isthmus connecting mainland Greece to the Peloponnese it controlled land traffic north and south and had ports on two seas — the Aegean to the east at Cenchreae and the Adriatic to the west at Lechaion. Ships often had their cargo unloaded and dragged overland to avoid the dangerous voyage around the southern cape. Corinth was wealthy cosmopolitan and morally notorious. Its population was diverse — Roman settlers Greek inhabitants a large Jewish community and transient merchants and sailors from across the Mediterranean. It was exactly the kind of city where a church would have maximum reach if the Gospel took root there.

Claudius had ordered all Jews to leave Rome — the Roman historian Suetonius mentions that Claudius expelled Jews from Rome because of continuous disturbances instigated by someone called Chrestus. Most historians believe Chrestus refers to Christ and that the disturbances were conflicts between Jewish communities and Jewish Christians in Rome. If so the expulsion scattered early Roman Christians including Aquila and Priscilla into the wider empire. The edict that was meant to suppress the Gospel instead distributed its carriers. Aquila and Priscilla carried the Gospel from Rome to Corinth where they became partners with Paul and later to Ephesus where they would teach Apollos.

He was a tentmaker as they were — Paul worked with his hands to support his ministry. In the Greek world manual labor was generally considered beneath the dignity of a teacher or philosopher. Sophists and traveling teachers expected payment for their instruction. Paul's refusal to accept payment from the Corinthians — which he discusses at length in 1 Corinthians 9 — was a deliberate choice to remove any suggestion that he was profiting from the Gospel. Working as a tentmaker alongside Aquila and Priscilla was both economically practical and theologically significant. The Gospel he preached was free. His labor made it so.

Your blood be on your own heads — this phrase came from Ezekiel 33 where the prophet described the watchman's responsibility. If the watchman warned the people and they refused to listen their blood was on their own heads. Paul had fulfilled his responsibility. He had preached the Gospel to the Jewish community in Corinth. Their rejection of it was their own choice and their own responsibility. Paul was not abandoning the Jewish people. He was releasing his own sense of obligation and turning to those who were willing to hear.

He went next door to the house of Titius Justus — the detail that Paul's new base of operations was immediately adjacent to the synagogue is almost darkly humorous. He did not go across town. He went next door. The synagogue that had rejected him would hear the Gospel continuing to be proclaimed from the building beside them every time they gathered. The separation was real but the proximity was a constant reminder of what they had rejected and what continued without them.

Crispus the synagogue leader believed — the leader of the very synagogue that had just opposed Paul believed along with his entire household. Crispus was not a peripheral figure. He was the man responsible for the synagogue that had expelled Paul. His conversion was a significant public event in the Corinthian Jewish community and would have had consequences for his position and relationships. Paul specifically mentions baptizing Crispus in 1 Corinthians 1 — one of only three people Paul says he baptized in Corinth himself.

Why It Still Matters

Paul worked with his hands to support his ministry. He did not make the Gospel a source of income in Corinth. That choice was costly — tentmaking was hard physical work on top of the already demanding work of preaching and teaching. But the freedom it gave him was priceless. He could preach without any financial entanglement with those he preached to. He could say things that needed saying without worrying about whether the people paying his salary would like it. The financial independence Paul maintained through manual labor is one of the factors that kept his preaching honest. When the Gospel is funded by those it serves there is always pressure toward telling people what they want to hear. Paul removed that pressure by picking up the needle and thread.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18

The Lord Encourages Paul in a Vision

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18
What Is Happening Here

One night the Lord speaks to Paul in a vision — do not be afraid keep on speaking and do not be silent. I am with you and no one is going to attack or harm you because I have many people in this city. So Paul stays in Corinth for a year and a half teaching them the word of God.

What You Might Not Know

Do not be afraid — the Lord's first words to Paul in the vision address fear. Paul was afraid in Corinth. This is not the only time in Acts that Paul needed direct encouragement to continue. After Athens where the response had been modest after the beatings at Philippi after the repeated pattern of opposition and expulsion the accumulated weight of the mission was evidently producing something that required direct divine address. The Paul of Acts is not a superhuman figure immune to discouragement. He was a man who got tired and afraid and needed to be told to keep going.

I have many people in this city — the Lord's statement is remarkable in its tense. Not I will have many people. I have many people. The believers who would come to faith in Corinth are already described as belonging to God before they have heard the Gospel. This is the same theological perspective Luke expressed in Acts 13 — all who were appointed for eternal life believed. The harvest Paul was about to reap had been prepared by God before Paul arrived. His job was to proclaim. God had already been at work in the city preparing the hearts that would respond.

Paul stayed in Corinth for a year and a half — this is the longest recorded stay in any city on the second missionary journey. The vision's promise of protection gave Paul the confidence to settle in and do the sustained teaching work that Corinth required. A cosmopolitan city of Corinth's complexity needed more than a few Sabbaths of synagogue preaching. It needed a community formed with depth — people taught carefully over time. The year and a half produced a church that Paul would write to extensively and struggle with repeatedly but never abandon.

Teaching them the word of God — the verb is imperfect tense in Greek indicating continuous ongoing action. Paul kept on teaching. Week after week month after month for eighteen months. The Corinthian church was not born in a revival and left to survive on enthusiasm. It was built by sustained careful teaching over a significant period. Whatever problems the Corinthian church later developed — and it developed many — they were not problems of a community that had not been taught. They were problems of a community that had been taught well but struggled to live up to what it knew.

Why It Still Matters

Do not be afraid — keep on speaking — do not be silent. The Lord's encouragement to Paul in Corinth is the encouragement the Church has needed in every generation when the accumulated weight of opposition and discouragement makes silence feel safer than speech. The Gospel does not advance by the courage of people who are never afraid. It advances by the faithfulness of people who are afraid and keep speaking anyway — because the Lord who told them to speak also promised to be with them. Paul's courage in Corinth was not the absence of fear. It was obedience in the presence of it backed by a promise he had received in a vision one night in a city that had not yet become what it would become.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18

Paul Before Gallio

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18
What Is Happening Here

While Gallio is proconsul of Achaia the Jews make a united attack on Paul and bring him before the place of judgment. They charge — this man is persuading people to worship God in ways contrary to the law. Just as Paul is about to speak Gallio interrupts. If the Jews were making a complaint about a misdemeanor or serious crime it would be reasonable to listen. But since this involves questions about words and names and their own law they should settle it themselves. He will not be a judge of such things. He drives them off. The crowd then turns on Sosthenes the synagogue leader and beats him in front of the tribunal. Gallio does not care at all.

What You Might Not Know

Gallio the proconsul of Achaia — Gallio was the brother of the philosopher Seneca and was known in ancient sources as a man of considerable charm and intelligence. An inscription discovered at Delphi in 1905 mentions Gallio as proconsul and can be dated to approximately 51-52 AD. This inscription is one of the most important chronological anchors in all of Pauline chronology. It allows scholars to date Paul's stay in Corinth with reasonable precision and to establish a timeline for much of his missionary activity. Luke's casual reference to Gallio as proconsul has proven to be historically accurate and historically useful.

This involves questions about words and names and your own law — Gallio's dismissal was shrewd. He recognized that the charge was a religious dispute dressed up as a legal complaint. The question of whether Jesus was the Messiah was not a matter of Roman law. It was a question of Jewish theology. Roman courts had no jurisdiction over internal Jewish religious debates. Gallio's ruling was legally sound and it had significant implications for the early Church. If Roman courts would not adjudicate Jewish religious disputes then the Christian movement had at least a measure of legal protection as long as it could be understood as a sect within Judaism.

Gallio's ruling became an important precedent — the proconsul of one of Rome's most significant provinces had publicly ruled that the Christian movement was an internal Jewish religious matter outside Roman legal jurisdiction. Word of this ruling would have spread through the legal and administrative network of the empire. The protection it afforded was not absolute — later emperors would take a different view — but for the period of the second and third missionary journeys Gallio's ruling gave the mission significant breathing room.

The crowd turned on Sosthenes the synagogue leader and beat him — this is one of the more puzzling moments in Acts. The same crowd that had attacked Paul turned on the synagogue leader when Gallio dismissed the case. Whether this was Greeks expressing anti-Jewish sentiment now that Gallio had publicly sided against the Jewish complainants or whether it was a faction within the Jewish community venting frustration at Sosthenes for bringing a case that backfired is unclear. Paul mentions a Sosthenes in 1 Corinthians 1 as a co-sender of the letter — if this is the same man he eventually became a believer. The man beaten in front of the tribunal may be the man who later co-wrote a letter to the Corinthian church.

Gallio did not care at all — the Greek phrase is ouden touton emelein — none of these things mattered to him. Gallio's indifference was not hostility to either side. He simply did not regard an internal Jewish theological dispute as something worth his attention. That indifference served the Gospel's interests in this instance. God used a Roman official's disinterest in religious questions to protect his missionaries from a legal attack.

Why It Still Matters

Gallio's ruling that the dispute over Jesus was an internal Jewish matter outside Roman jurisdiction gave the early Church legal space to operate across the empire. God used a pagan proconsul's procedural ruling to protect his mission. The tool of protection was not a spiritual gift or a miraculous intervention. It was a Roman official applying Roman legal procedure correctly. Acts consistently shows God using the structures and procedures of human authority — sometimes their justice and sometimes their indifference — to advance the Gospel. The mission does not require the authorities to be sympathetic. It requires only that they stay out of the way long enough for the word to take root. In Corinth Gallio's disinterest was exactly that.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18

Paul Returns to Antioch

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18
What Is Happening Here

Paul stays on in Corinth for some time then says goodbye to the brothers and sails for Syria with Priscilla and Aquila. Before sailing he has his hair cut at Cenchreae because of a vow he has taken. They arrive at Ephesus where Paul leaves Priscilla and Aquila. He goes into the synagogue and reasons with the Jews. When they ask him to stay longer he declines but promises to return if it is God's will. He sets sail from Ephesus lands at Caesarea goes up to Jerusalem greets the church and then goes down to Antioch. After spending some time there he sets out and travels through the region of Galatia and Phrygia strengthening all the disciples.

What You Might Not Know

He had his hair cut off because of a vow — the vow Paul had taken was almost certainly a Nazirite vow described in Numbers 6. During a Nazirite vow a person would let their hair grow as a sign of dedication to God. When the vow period ended the hair was cut and offered at the Temple in Jerusalem. Paul — the apostle who had argued most forcefully against requiring Gentiles to observe the law — was himself voluntarily observing a traditional Jewish practice. This is consistent with the Paul who circumcised Timothy and who later in Acts will participate in a Temple purification ceremony. Paul's freedom from the law as a requirement for salvation did not mean personal abandonment of Jewish practice as a matter of conscience and devotion.

I will come back if it is God's will — Paul's response to the Ephesian synagogue's invitation was the first hint that Ephesus was on his horizon. The city had been closed to him on the second journey — the Spirit had redirected him away from Asia in Chapter 16. Now at the end of the second journey Paul passes through Ephesus briefly and promises to return. That return will come in Chapter 19 and will produce the most sustained ministry of Paul's entire missionary career. Ephesus was not forgotten. It was being prepared.

He went up to Jerusalem greeted the church and then went down to Antioch — the brief mention of Jerusalem is significant. Paul maintained his connection to the mother church even as his ministry ranged across the Roman world. The greeting of the church in Jerusalem was not a perfunctory formality. It was a deliberate act of maintaining the relational and theological accountability that connected the Gentile mission to its Jewish roots. Paul's letters show a man who cared deeply about the unity of Jewish and Gentile believers. His visits to Jerusalem were part of how he sustained that unity in practice.

Strengthening all the disciples — the third missionary journey begins not with dramatic new evangelism but with pastoral consolidation. Paul traveled through the regions where he had planted churches on the first and second journeys — Galatia and Phrygia — strengthening what was already there. New mission and consolidation of existing mission are both necessary. Paul gave time to both. The disciples he had won at great cost deserved the follow-up care that would help them go deep rather than just wide.

Why It Still Matters

Paul said goodbye to the brothers in Corinth and it cost something. Eighteen months in one city meant relationships of genuine depth. The people who gathered at the harbor to send him off were not acquaintances. They were a community he had built from nothing, taught for a year and a half, and now had to leave. The pattern of forming deep bonds and then leaving them in God's hands repeated itself in every city Paul planted a church. His letters to Corinth show how much the continued welfare of the community weighed on him after he left. The cost of itinerant mission is real relationships with real people who you cannot stay with. Paul paid that cost repeatedly. The churches he planted paid it too.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18

Apollos Comes to Ephesus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18
What Is Happening Here

Meanwhile a Jew named Apollos from Alexandria comes to Ephesus. He is a learned man with a thorough knowledge of the Scriptures. He has been instructed in the way of the Lord and speaks with great passion teaching about Jesus accurately — though he only knows the baptism of John. He begins to speak boldly in the synagogue. When Priscilla and Aquila hear him they invite him to their home and explain the way of God to him more completely.

What You Might Not Know

Alexandria — the city of Apollos's origin was the second largest city in the Roman Empire and the intellectual capital of the Jewish diaspora. Alexandria's Jewish community was enormous and had produced the Septuagint — the Greek translation of the Hebrew Scriptures that Paul quoted throughout his ministry. Alexandrian Jewish scholarship was sophisticated and deeply engaged with Greek philosophy. Apollos came from a tradition of learned Jewish engagement with the wider intellectual world. His thorough knowledge of the Scriptures combined with his passion for teaching made him an exceptionally gifted communicator — as the Corinthian church's later fondness for him over Paul suggests.

He taught about Jesus accurately though he only knew the baptism of John — Apollos's situation is one of the most theologically interesting in Acts. He knew about Jesus. He taught about Jesus accurately. He was passionate and Scripturally grounded. But his knowledge stopped at John's baptism — he knew the preparation but apparently not the full story of the Spirit's coming at Pentecost. He was like someone who had read the Gospels carefully but had not yet encountered Acts. What he taught was true as far as it went. It did not go far enough.

Priscilla and Aquila — the couple Paul had left in Ephesus were now doing what faithful disciples do when they encounter a gifted teacher who needs completion. They did not correct Apollos publicly in the synagogue. They did not report him to anyone as teaching inadequately. They invited him to their home. The correction was private, relational, and generous. The tent-making couple who had learned the full Gospel from Paul now passed it on to the brilliant Alexandrian who would carry it to Corinth and beyond.

Priscilla is named first — throughout Acts and in Paul's letters Priscilla is consistently named before her husband Aquila when the couple appears together. This is unusual in ancient texts where the husband was typically named first. Most scholars take the consistent naming of Priscilla first as an indication that she was the more prominent figure in the ministry of the couple — possibly the primary teacher. The woman who explained the way of God more completely to one of the most gifted preachers in the early Church is named first in every reference to the couple. Luke is not careless with these details.

Why It Still Matters

Priscilla and Aquila invited Apollos to their home and explained the way of God to him more completely. The most gifted preacher in Ephesus needed someone to take him aside and fill in what was missing. He was bold and learned and accurate as far as he went. But he needed more. And he received it not from an apostle or a council but from a tentmaking couple in their home. The way the Church has always grown in depth is through people who know more being willing to share it privately and generously with people who know less — regardless of the gap in public profile between them. Apollos was famous. Priscilla and Aquila were craftspeople. That difference in status did not prevent the transmission of truth. It was simply irrelevant to what needed to happen.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18

Apollos Goes to Achaia

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18
What Is Happening Here

When Apollos wants to go to Achaia the brothers and sisters encourage him and write to the disciples there to welcome him. When he arrives he is a great help to those who have believed by grace. He vigorously refutes his Jewish opponents in public debate proving from the Scriptures that Jesus is the Messiah.

What You Might Not Know

The brothers wrote to the disciples in Achaia to welcome him — the letter of commendation was a standard practice in the ancient world and in the early Church. A stranger arriving in a new city needed someone to vouch for them. The Ephesian church's letter to the Achaian churches — primarily Corinth — opened the doors that Apollos's reputation alone could not have opened. He arrived in Corinth not as an unknown visitor but as someone the Ephesian community had vouched for. The network of trust that connected the early churches made it possible for gifted teachers to move between communities and be received.

He was a great help to those who had believed by grace — Apollos's ministry in Corinth was supplementary to Paul's. Paul had planted. Apollos watered. In 1 Corinthians 3 Paul explicitly uses this imagery — I planted Apollos watered but God made it grow. The different gifts of different teachers serving the same community was not a problem to be resolved. It was the normal pattern of how the Church grows. Apollos brought something Paul had not brought — a different style, a different approach, perhaps a more philosophical mode of engagement suited to a city that admired Athenian-style intellectual prowess.

He vigorously refuted his Jewish opponents proving from the Scriptures that Jesus is the Messiah — this is exactly what Paul had been doing in the Corinthian synagogue. Apollos took up the same method and the same argument. The continuity of the message across different personalities and styles is one of the marks of the early Church's theological integrity. The Gospel was not Paul's personal interpretation. It was the common proclamation of everyone who had been taught the way of God completely. When Apollos preached in Corinth he was preaching the same Jesus Paul had preached — with his own voice and his own gifts but the same content.

Why It Still Matters

He was a great help to those who had believed by grace. The people who had come to faith under Paul's ministry were strengthened and helped by Apollos's different gift. Different teachers serving the same community with different gifts is not a threat to unity. It is a contribution to maturity. The problem arises when people attach their identity to a teacher rather than to the Christ the teacher points toward. The Corinthians will do exactly this — some claiming to follow Paul others Apollos others Peter. Paul will address it directly in 1 Corinthians. But the diversity of gifted teachers serving one community was itself good. The mistake was in making the diversity into a division. Apollos was a gift to Corinth. The Corinthians' factionalism around his name was not Apollos's fault. It was their own.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 18

Acts Chapter 19

Disciples in Ephesus Receive the Holy Spirit

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19
What Is Happening Here

While Apollos is at Corinth Paul takes the inland route and arrives in Ephesus where he finds some disciples. He asks if they received the Holy Spirit when they believed. They have not even heard that there is a Holy Spirit. Paul asks what baptism they received — John's baptism they reply. Paul explains that John's baptism was a baptism of repentance and that John told the people to believe in the one coming after him — that is Jesus. When they hear this they are baptized in the name of the Lord Jesus. Paul places his hands on them the Holy Spirit comes on them and they speak in tongues and prophesy. There are about twelve men in all.

What You Might Not Know

Did you receive the Holy Spirit when you believed — Paul's opening question to these disciples is unusual. He did not normally begin a conversation with believers by asking about their Spirit reception. Something about this group prompted the question — perhaps an absence of the quality of life or understanding that Paul associated with Spirit-filled believers. The question reveals that for Paul the reception of the Holy Spirit was not an assumed automatic result of some prior religious experience. It was something real and recognizable whose presence or absence could be detected.

We haven't even heard that there is a Holy Spirit — this statement has puzzled readers because the Hebrew Scriptures are full of references to the Spirit of God. These disciples were not saying they had never heard the phrase. They were saying they had not heard that the Holy Spirit had been given — that the promised outpouring of the Spirit prophesied by Joel and announced by Jesus had actually occurred. They were disciples of John's movement who had not yet learned about Pentecost. Their situation was similar to Apollos before Priscilla and Aquila explained the way of God more completely — genuine faith reaching toward God but not yet knowing the fullness of what God had done.

John's baptism was a baptism of repentance — Paul distinguishes carefully between what John's baptism was and what it was not. It was genuine — a real call to repentance and a real preparation for the coming Messiah. But it was preparatory not complete. John himself had said so. The one coming after him would baptize with the Holy Spirit. These twelve had received what John offered. They had not yet received what Jesus promised. Paul's instruction completed what was missing and they were baptized in Jesus's name and received the Spirit.

They spoke in tongues and prophesied — the reception of the Spirit by the Ephesian disciples produced the same visible manifestation as Pentecost and Cornelius's household. Luke records four occasions in Acts where the Spirit's coming is accompanied by tongues — Pentecost, Samaria implicitly, Cornelius's household, and here. Each occasion marks a significant stage in the Gospel's advance. The Ephesian disciples were not just individuals being completed in faith. They were the nucleus of the Ephesian church — the community that would become one of the most important in the early Christian world.

About twelve men — the number echoes the twelve apostles and the twelve tribes of Israel. Whether Luke intends a deliberate symbolic connection or is simply noting the actual number is unclear. What is clear is that this small group of completed disciples became the foundation of Paul's three-year Ephesian ministry — the longest and most sustained of his entire missionary career.

Why It Still Matters

These twelve disciples had genuine faith and genuine baptism and were still missing something essential. Paul did not dismiss their prior experience as worthless or tell them to start over entirely. He built on what was real and completed what was lacking. That approach — honoring what is genuine while honestly addressing what is incomplete — is a model for pastoral care across theological differences. Some people come to the Church with real seeking, real prayer, real moral seriousness, and still need to hear the full story of what Jesus did and what the Spirit offers. Meeting them where they are and completing what is missing is the shape of faithful discipleship ministry.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19

Paul Teaches in Ephesus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19
What Is Happening Here

Paul goes into the synagogue and speaks boldly for three months arguing persuasively about the kingdom of God. But some become stubborn refusing to believe and publicly speaking evil about the Way. Paul leaves them takes the disciples with him and has daily discussions in the lecture hall of Tyrannus. This goes on for two years so that all the Jews and Greeks living in the province of Asia hear the word of the Lord.

What You Might Not Know

Three months in the synagogue — Paul's Ephesian synagogue ministry was three times as long as his Thessalonian synagogue ministry of three weeks and comparable to his extended time in Corinth. Ephesus was a strategic city that deserved and received sustained investment. The kingdom of God was the content of his preaching — the same theme Jesus had taught after the resurrection according to Acts 1. Paul's synagogue preaching was not primarily about individual salvation. It was about the arrival of God's reign through the Messiah and what that meant for Israel and the world.

The lecture hall of Tyrannus — when the synagogue doors closed Paul moved to a secular lecture hall. Tyrannus was either the owner of the hall or a teacher who used it. According to one early manuscript tradition Paul used the hall during the midday hours from eleven in the morning to four in the afternoon — the hottest part of the day when Tyrannus himself would not have been using it and when most people took their rest. Paul and his audience were giving up their rest period for daily teaching. The commitment required from both teacher and learners was significant.

Two years of daily discussions — the Greek word dialegomai means to reason through, to discuss, or to dialogue. Paul was not delivering monologues. He was engaging in sustained daily dialogue about the kingdom of God and the Gospel. Two years of this produced something remarkable — all the Jews and Greeks in the entire province of Asia heard the word of the Lord. Paul did not travel to every city in Asia. The Ephesian church sent people out. The daily teaching of two years produced a community equipped and motivated to spread the Gospel throughout the surrounding region. Colossae, Laodicea, Hierapolis and other cities in the province likely received the Gospel during this period through people who had sat in the hall of Tyrannus.

The province of Asia heard the word of the Lord — this is one of the most remarkable statements in Acts. The entire province reached from one city through two years of sustained teaching and the community it produced. Paul's strategy of investing deeply in strategic cities and equipping the community to multiply outward was the mechanism. The province of Asia would become one of the most thoroughly Christianized regions in the ancient world. The seven churches addressed in Revelation — Ephesus Smyrna Pergamum Thyatira Sardis Philadelphia Laodicea — were all in this province. Their existence traces back to the two years in the hall of Tyrannus.

Why It Still Matters

Paul reasoned daily for two years. The Ephesian ministry was not a series of evangelistic events. It was sustained daily engagement with a growing community of learners who then carried what they had learned into the surrounding region. The multiplication of the Gospel from Ephesus across the province of Asia happened not through Paul traveling everywhere but through Paul investing deeply in one place and equipping people who traveled everywhere. That model — deep investment in one community producing wide multiplication through that community — is still the most effective pattern of Gospel advance. A church that goes a mile wide and an inch deep produces little lasting fruit. A church that goes a mile deep in a strategic place tends to produce fruit that reaches places the original planter never went.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19

Extraordinary Miracles

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19
What Is Happening Here

God does extraordinary miracles through Paul so that even handkerchiefs and aprons that had touched him are taken to the sick. Their illnesses are cured and evil spirits leave them. Some Jewish exorcists try to invoke the name of the Lord Jesus over demon-possessed people saying — in the name of Jesus whom Paul preaches I command you to come out. Seven sons of Sceva a Jewish chief priest are doing this. One day the evil spirit answers them — Jesus I know and Paul I know about but who are you. The man with the evil spirit jumps on them overpowers them all and they run out of the house naked and bleeding. When this becomes known throughout Ephesus they are all seized with fear and the name of the Lord Jesus is held in high honor. Many believers come and openly confess their evil deeds. A number who had practiced sorcery bring their scrolls together and burn them publicly. The value of the scrolls is calculated at fifty thousand silver coins. In this way the word of the Lord spreads widely and grows in power.

What You Might Not Know

Ephesus was one of the major centers of magical practice in the ancient world. The city was famous for what were called Ephesian letters — magical formulas and incantations believed to have protective and healing powers. The combination of the Temple of Artemis, a large population of religious practitioners, and the city's cosmopolitan character made it a hub for every kind of occult practice. Paul was doing ministry in a city thoroughly saturated with competing supernatural claims. The extraordinary miracles through Paul were God's response to a specific context — a city that evaluated spiritual authority by miraculous power needed to see that the authority behind Paul's name was in a different category from the magical practices surrounding him.

Handkerchiefs and aprons that had touched him — these were sweat cloths and work aprons from Paul's tentmaking. People who could not reach Paul directly were taking objects that had been in contact with him and using them for healing. This echoes the woman who touched the hem of Jesus's garment in the Gospels. Whether the cloths themselves had power or whether God honored the faith expressed in the practice Luke does not explain. He simply notes that God worked through them. This is one of the stranger passages in Acts and Luke records it without theological commentary. The extraordinary nature of the Ephesian miracles was proportional to the extraordinary spiritual opposition the mission faced in that city.

Jesus I know and Paul I know about but who are you — the evil spirit's response to the seven sons of Sceva is one of the most memorable lines in Acts. The spirit recognized genuine authority — Jesus. It recognized someone operating under that authority — Paul. It recognized nothing in the sons of Sceva that could back up the name they were invoking. The name of Jesus was not a magical formula that worked for anyone who pronounced it correctly. It was a relationship — a reality that backed the one who spoke it because they actually belonged to Jesus. The sons of Sceva were using the name without the relationship and the spirit knew the difference.

Fifty thousand silver coins — the value of the burned scrolls was enormous. A silver coin — a drachma — was roughly a day's wages for a laborer. Fifty thousand drachmas represented about 137 years of daily wages. The people who burned these scrolls were burning a significant portion of their economic assets. The sorcery scrolls were not just books. They were tools of income for practitioners. Their burning was a costly public repudiation of the entire framework of magical power that Ephesus had been built around. The bonfire was a statement — we are done with this. The cost of the statement was counted and accepted.

The word of the Lord spread widely and grew in power — Luke's summary follows the bonfire. The voluntary destruction of the magical infrastructure did not weaken the community. It strengthened it. The clearing out of the competing spiritual practices created space for the word of God to take deeper root and spread further. Spiritual housecleaning and Gospel advance are connected in Acts. Communities that hold onto practices incompatible with the Gospel tend to experience stunted growth. The Ephesian believers who burned their scrolls experienced something different.

Why It Still Matters

They brought their scrolls together and burned them publicly. The burning was voluntary, costly, and communal. No one commanded it. The fear of the Lord that came through the sons of Sceva incident produced in the Ephesian believers a spontaneous desire to rid themselves of everything incompatible with genuine faith. They counted the cost — fifty thousand coins worth — and burned the scrolls anyway. The public nature of the burning was important. It was a community-wide declaration of changed allegiance. Private repentance is real. Public repudiation of what was incompatible with the Gospel added something to the private decision. Sometimes the community needs to see the fire to know that something genuine has changed.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19

Paul Plans to Travel to Rome

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19
What Is Happening Here

After all this Paul decides to go to Jerusalem passing through Macedonia and Achaia. After being there he says he must also visit Rome. He sends Timothy and Erastus to Macedonia while he stays in the province of Asia a little longer.

What You Might Not Know

I must visit Rome also — the word must is dei in Greek — the same word used for divine necessity throughout Acts and the Gospels. It is not Paul expressing a personal preference. He is describing a divine compulsion — the same kind of necessity that drove Jesus toward Jerusalem. Rome was not just the capital of the empire. It was the symbol of the ends of the earth. Jesus had said in Acts 1:8 that the apostles would be his witnesses to the ends of the earth. Rome was the center from which everything radiated and to which everything was connected. Paul's must go to Rome was a recognition that the commission of Acts 1:8 required it.

Passing through Macedonia and Achaia — Paul's route to Jerusalem via Macedonia and Achaia was not the most direct path. He was planning to revisit the churches he had planted on the second journey — Philippi Thessalonica Berea Corinth — and to collect the offering for Jerusalem that he had been organizing among the Gentile churches. The offering was theologically important to Paul as a demonstration of the unity between Jewish and Gentile believers. Romans 15 and 2 Corinthians 8-9 show how much energy Paul invested in this collection. It was not charity. It was a statement about the body of Christ.

He sent Timothy and Erastus to Macedonia — Paul worked through a network of co-workers who could represent him in multiple locations simultaneously. Timothy had been with him since the second journey. Erastus appears here and in Romans 16 where Paul mentions Erastus as the city's director of public works — a significant civic position in Corinth. The early Church included people from diverse social backgrounds — tentmakers and city officials, slaves and synagogue rulers, businesswomen and Roman centurions. Paul's team reflected that diversity.

Why It Still Matters

I must visit Rome also. The trajectory of Acts from Jerusalem to Rome was not Paul's strategy. It was the Spirit's design. Paul's sense of divine compulsion toward Rome was the human experience of a plan that had been in place since Acts 1:8. The word must appears at critical turning points throughout Acts — Jesus must suffer, the Messiah must rise, Paul must stand before Caesar. The divine necessity driving the narrative is not fate in the pagan sense but the purposeful movement of a God who knows where his story is going and calls people into it. Paul's compulsion toward Rome was his participation in a story larger than himself.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19

The Riot in Ephesus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19
What Is Happening Here

A great disturbance breaks out over the Way. Demetrius a silversmith who makes silver shrines of Artemis and provides work for many craftsmen calls them together. He warns that Paul has convinced large numbers of people in Ephesus and practically the whole province of Asia that man-made gods are no gods at all. There is danger that their trade will lose its reputation and that the temple of the great goddess Artemis will be discredited and the goddess herself robbed of her greatness. When the craftsmen hear this they are furious and begin shouting — great is Artemis of the Ephesians. The whole city is in an uproar. The people seize Gaius and Aristarchus Paul's companions and rush into the theater. Paul wants to appear before the crowd but the disciples will not let him. Even provincial officials who are friends of Paul beg him not to venture into the theater.

What You Might Not Know

The Temple of Artemis at Ephesus was one of the Seven Wonders of the Ancient World. It was four times the size of the Parthenon in Athens and had stood for centuries as the defining feature of Ephesian identity and economy. Pilgrims came from across the Mediterranean world to worship there. The sale of silver shrines and religious objects was a major industry. Artemis was not just a religious figure in Ephesus. She was the economic engine of the city. The Gospel's success in turning people away from idol worship was therefore not just a theological challenge to the city's religion. It was an economic threat to a significant portion of the city's workforce.

Demetrius named the threat honestly — Paul says that man-made gods are no gods at all. This was exactly what Paul had been saying — in the synagogue in the hall of Tyrannus in the streets and through the community he had been teaching for two years. The Gospel had been so effective in the province of Asia that the silversmith trade was suffering measurably. The riot was economic grievance dressed in religious language — exactly the same pattern as the slave girl's owners in Philippi. The theology was real but the primary driver was money.

The theater at Ephesus — the great theater of Ephesus held approximately 24,000 people. It was the largest gathering space in the city and the natural venue for public assemblies. The riot moved there instinctively — a crowd looking for a platform for its anger. The theater has been excavated and confirmed by archaeology. Its capacity gives some sense of the scale of the disturbance Luke is describing. This was not a street fight. It was a city-scale crisis.

Paul wanted to appear before the crowd — Paul's instinct was to go into the theater and address the mob directly. He had done this kind of thing before — speaking to hostile crowds in Jerusalem and before courts and councils. His companions and his provincial friends would not let him. For once Paul was held back from the dangerous thing he wanted to do. The restraint came from people who cared about him and calculated the risk more soberly than he did. Courage is a virtue. Recklessness is not. The line between them is sometimes drawn by the people who love you.

Why It Still Matters

Demetrius said Paul has convinced large numbers of people that man-made gods are no gods at all. He said this as an accusation. It was an accurate description of the Gospel's effect in Ephesus. The Gospel had been so thoroughly persuasive that it had disrupted the economic infrastructure of idol worship in one of the most idol-saturated cities in the ancient world. The measure of the Gospel's impact was visible in the silversmith's declining income. When the Gospel is genuinely transforming a community it tends to show up in the economics. What people stop buying and what they start valuing differently reflects what they actually believe. The Ephesian silversmiths noticed the change in their customers before the theologians had finished debating the implications.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19

The City Clerk Restores Order

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19
What Is Happening Here

The crowd is in total confusion — some shouting one thing some another. Most people do not even know why they are there. The Jews push Alexander to the front and some shout instructions to him. He motions for silence to make a defense. But when the crowd realizes he is a Jew they all shout in unison for about two hours — great is Artemis of the Ephesians. Finally the city clerk quiets the crowd. He tells them all the world knows Ephesus is the guardian of the great Artemis and her image that fell from heaven. These facts cannot be denied so they need to calm down. The men they have brought here have neither robbed temples nor spoken against the goddess. If Demetrius and his fellow craftsmen have a grievance the courts are open and there are proconsuls — they can press charges. If there is anything further it must be settled in a proper legal assembly. As it is they are in danger of being charged with rioting because of what has happened today. There is no good reason for the commotion and they would not be able to explain it. He dismisses the crowd.

What You Might Not Know

Most of the people didn't even know why they were there — this detail is one of the most honest observations in Acts about the nature of mob behavior. The riot had taken on a life of its own. The people shouting great is Artemis of the Ephesians had not chosen to be outraged about theology or economics. They had been caught up in a crowd emotion that preceded any individual decision. The confusing noise of a crowd in which some were shouting one thing and some another captures the reality of mass hysteria. The riot was not a coherent protest. It was contagion — fear and anger spreading from person to person until a theater full of people were chanting for two hours without knowing exactly what they were chanting for.

Alexander — the Jews pushed Alexander to the front presumably to dissociate themselves from Paul and the Christians. They wanted the crowd to know that Jewish people as a community were not responsible for Paul's teaching. When the crowd realized Alexander was Jewish the plan backfired immediately. Anti-Jewish sentiment in a mob inflamed about a perceived threat to Greek religion was not going to pause to make distinctions between different kinds of Jews. The crowd did not care that Alexander was trying to distance himself from Paul. He was Jewish and that was enough for the mob.

The city clerk — this was one of the most important civic officials in Ephesus. The city clerk was responsible for official documents and communications between the city and Roman authorities. He was also responsible for maintaining public order and was accountable to Rome for what happened in the city. His intervention was motivated partly by genuine concern about the disorder and partly by the very specific Roman legal threat he named — the city could be charged with rioting and lose some of its civic privileges. Roman cities caught in civic disorder faced real consequences from the empire.

They have neither robbed temples nor spoken against our goddess — the city clerk's defense of Paul and his companions was another legal ruling in Paul's favor — echoing Gallio's ruling in Corinth. The second highest civic official in one of Asia's most important cities publicly stated that the Christians had done nothing legally actionable. They had not committed sacrilege. They had not made public speeches against Artemis. What they had done — teaching that man-made gods are no gods at all — was within the bounds of philosophical speech permitted in the Roman world. The clerk's ruling gave the Christian community in Ephesus significant protection.

There is no good reason for this commotion and we wouldn't be able to explain it — the clerk's final argument was practical and shrewd. If Roman investigators came to Ephesus to inquire about the riot what would the city say? That craftsmen were upset because people stopped buying their shrines? The city would look foolish and the legal consequences could be serious. The mob's anger had no legally articulable cause. That inability to explain the riot to Roman authority was the clerk's most effective argument for disbanding it.

Why It Still Matters

The city clerk dismissed the Ephesian riot by pointing out that the Christians had done nothing legally wrong and that the riot itself posed a legal threat to the city. God used a pagan civic official's concern for Roman legal standing to protect the Church — the same pattern as Gallio in Corinth. Neither official was sympathetic to the Gospel. Both were useful to it. The protection the early Church received from Roman law was not because Rome loved the Gospel. Rome was largely indifferent or hostile. But Rome's interest in legal order and civic stability sometimes aligned with the Church's need for protection. God used that alignment repeatedly. The instruments of God's protection for his people have never been limited to the sympathetic.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 19

Acts Chapter 20

Paul Travels Through Macedonia and Greece

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20
What Is Happening Here

After the uproar ends Paul sends for the disciples encourages them says goodbye and sets out for Macedonia. He travels through that area speaking many words of encouragement and finally arrives in Greece where he stays three months. Because some Jews hatch a plot against him just as he is about to sail for Syria he decides to go back through Macedonia. He is accompanied by Sopater from Berea, Aristarchus and Secundus from Thessalonica, Gaius from Derbe, Timothy, and Tychicus and Trophimus from Asia. These men go on ahead to Troas. Paul and Luke sail from Philippi after the Festival of Unleavened Bread and five days later join the others at Troas where they stay seven days.

What You Might Not Know

Seven traveling companions from different regions — the list of men accompanying Paul on this final journey toward Jerusalem is carefully preserved by Luke. Each man represented a different church — Berea, Thessalonica, Derbe, Asia. They were almost certainly the delegates carrying the collection for Jerusalem from their respective Gentile communities. Paul's letter to the Romans describes this collection as a sacred obligation of the Gentile churches toward the mother church in Jerusalem. The multi-ethnic team traveling together to Jerusalem with their offering was itself a living demonstration of the Gospel's power to create one community out of many peoples.

Because some Jews hatched a plot against him — this is the third time in Acts that a plot against Paul's life forces a change of travel plans. The opposition was persistent and Paul had learned to take it seriously. The plot against him on a ship sailing for Syria — probably a plan to throw him overboard — caused him to change his route entirely and go overland through Macedonia instead. The change added weeks to his journey but preserved his life for the ministry still ahead.

We sailed from Philippi — the we-narrative resumes here after a long absence. Luke had last used the first person plural in Acts 16 when the team left Philippi. Now he rejoins the party at the same city where he had apparently remained during the intervening years. Luke had stayed in Philippi and was now traveling with Paul again for the final journey to Jerusalem and eventually to Rome. The resumption of the we-narrative signals that Luke was an eyewitness to everything from this point forward.

Five days — on the second journey the same crossing from Philippi to Troas had taken two days according to Acts 16. The five days on this occasion suggest unfavorable winds. Travel in the ancient world was at the mercy of weather and wind in ways modern travelers rarely experience. The same journey could take two days or five depending entirely on conditions beyond human control. Paul was not always moving efficiently. He was moving as conditions permitted.

Why It Still Matters

Paul traveled through Macedonia speaking many words of encouragement. The Greek word for encouragement is paraklesis — the same word used for the Holy Spirit as Paraclete. Paul's ministry of encouragement to churches he had planted and churches he was now revisiting was not peripheral to his mission. It was central to it. Planting a church and leaving it is only half the work. Returning to strengthen encourage and build up what has been planted is the other half. Paul modeled both the pioneering and the pastoral dimensions of apostolic ministry throughout his journeys.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20

Eutychus Falls from a Window

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20
What Is Happening Here

On the first day of the week in Troas the community gathers to break bread. Paul speaks because he is leaving the next day and keeps talking until midnight. Many lamps burn in the upstairs room. A young man named Eutychus seated in a window sinks into deep sleep as Paul talks on and on. Sound asleep he falls to the ground from the third story and is picked up dead. Paul goes down throws himself on the young man and puts his arms around him. Do not be alarmed he says — he is alive. Paul goes back upstairs breaks bread and eats and talks until daylight then leaves. The people take the young man home alive and are greatly comforted.

What You Might Not Know

The first day of the week — this is one of the earliest references in Acts to Sunday as the regular gathering day for Christians. The resurrection had happened on the first day of the week. The earliest communities began meeting on that day to break bread and remember the Lord. Paul's visit to Troas fell on this day and the community gathered in the way they normally did. The break bread reference is almost certainly the Lord's Supper rather than an ordinary meal — the same language used in Acts 2 for the community's regular practice of fellowship and remembrance.

Many lamps in the upstairs room — this detail is not decorative. Numerous oil lamps burning in a small enclosed room would have significantly reduced the oxygen level and increased the heat. A young man sitting in a window — which was both the coolest and most ventilated spot in the room but also the most precarious — in a warm low-oxygen environment late at night after what was likely a full day of work was in a genuinely difficult situation for staying awake. Eutychus's falling asleep was understandable. The fall was fatal. Luke the physician notes the details with the precision of someone trained to observe.

Paul went down and threw himself on the young man — the action echoes Elijah throwing himself on the widow's son in 1 Kings 17 and Elisha throwing himself on the Shunammite's son in 2 Kings 4. Paul was not inventing a technique. He was doing what the prophets had done — physical identification with the person in need, prayer, and trust that God would act. Whether the young man had actually died or was in a death-like coma Luke's diagnosis as a physician is clear — he was picked up dead. Paul's response was the same regardless — he went down and wrapped himself around the body.

He is alive — Paul's announcement echoes the language of resurrection throughout the New Testament. The one who was dead is alive. The same word used for Jesus in the resurrection accounts. Eutychus's restoration was not the resurrection of Jesus. But it was a sign pointing toward the same power — the God who raises the dead was at work through his apostle in an upper room in Troas.

Paul went back upstairs broke bread and ate and talked until daylight — the restoration of Eutychus did not become the center of the evening. Paul went back upstairs and continued the meeting. He broke bread. He talked until dawn. The miracle was real and comforting but it was not the point of the gathering. The point was the breaking of bread and the teaching of the word. The miracle served the community. The community was not constituted by the miracle.

Why It Still Matters

The people took the young man home alive and were greatly comforted. The comfort was not primarily about the miracle. It was about the young man being alive — a specific person known to them being restored to them. The early Church's gatherings were not primarily events. They were communities of specific people who knew each other and whose loss would be felt and whose restoration was a cause of genuine joy. Eutychus was not a symbol or a theological point. He was someone's son, someone's neighbor, someone the community in Troas knew. They were greatly comforted not because God had performed a sign but because the person they had seen fall from the window was home alive. Community is made of specific people and the restoration of one specific person mattered to all of them.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20

The Journey to Miletus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20
What Is Happening Here

The team goes ahead to the ship and sails for Assos where they will take Paul aboard. He has arranged to meet them there going on foot. When he meets them at Assos they take him aboard and continue to Mitylene. The next day they sail and arrive off Chios. The day after that they cross to Samos and the following day arrive at Miletus. Paul has decided to sail past Ephesus to avoid spending time in the province of Asia because he is in a hurry to reach Jerusalem if possible by the day of Pentecost.

What You Might Not Know

Paul went on foot while the team sailed — the distance from Troas to Assos on foot was about twenty miles along a road that cut across a peninsula while the ship sailed around it. Paul chose to walk alone. Why? Luke does not say. Perhaps he needed time alone to pray and prepare his spirit for what lay ahead. He knew he was going to Jerusalem under the Spirit's compulsion. He knew prison and hardships were waiting. The solitary walk from Troas to Assos may have been Paul's Gethsemane moment — the time alone with God before the final movement toward Jerusalem and whatever awaited him there.

He decided to sail past Ephesus — Paul bypassed the city where he had spent three years because he could not afford the time a visit would require. The Ephesian community was large and deeply attached to Paul. A stop in Ephesus would have generated the kind of extended farewell that would have consumed days or weeks. Paul had a deadline — Pentecost in Jerusalem. The collection needed to arrive, his ministry in Jerusalem needed to happen, and the trajectory toward Rome was pulling him forward. Bypassing Ephesus was a painful strategic choice made by someone who loved the Ephesian community too much to give them the rushed visit they deserved.

Miletus was about thirty miles south of Ephesus — close enough for a messenger to reach the Ephesian elders and return with them in a reasonable time but far enough to accomplish what stopping in Ephesus would not allow. Paul found a way to see the elders he loved without derailing the journey. The compromise was characteristically Paul — finding a solution that honored both the relationship and the mission.

If possible by the day of Pentecost — Pentecost was fifty days after Passover. Paul was traveling in the spring and calculating whether he could reach Jerusalem in time for the festival. The significance of Pentecost for Paul was not purely sentimental. Pentecost brought large numbers of Jewish pilgrims to Jerusalem from across the diaspora — exactly the people Paul wanted to reach with the collection and with his testimony. The strategic timing reflects Paul's consistent pattern of using the Jewish festival calendar to maximize the reach of his ministry.

Why It Still Matters

Paul sailed past Ephesus to keep his appointment in Jerusalem. The city where he had spent more time than any other on his missionary journeys he passed by because the mission required it. That discipline — the willingness to pass by what you love for the sake of what you are called to do — is one of the costlier forms of faithfulness. Paul's love for the Ephesian church was genuine and deep. His call to Jerusalem was also genuine and deep. He chose the call. But he did not abandon the love — he found a way to honor both by calling the elders to Miletus. The creative solution that honors both the relationship and the mission is always worth looking for before simply accepting that one must be sacrificed for the other.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20

Paul's Farewell to the Ephesian Elders

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20
What Is Happening Here

Paul sends to Ephesus for the elders. When they arrive he tells them how he lived among them from the first day — serving the Lord with great humility and tears in the midst of severe testing. He never hesitated to preach anything helpful and taught publicly and house to house declaring repentance toward God and faith in the Lord Jesus to both Jews and Greeks. Now compelled by the Spirit he is going to Jerusalem. He does not know what will happen there. The Holy Spirit warns him in every city that prison and hardships are waiting. But he does not consider his life worth anything to him — his only aim is to finish the race and complete the task of telling others about the good news of God's grace. He knows they will never see his face again. He declares he is innocent of the blood of any of them — he never hesitated to proclaim the whole will of God. He charges them to keep watch over themselves and all the flock the Holy Spirit has placed in their care. After he leaves savage wolves will come in. Even from their own number men will rise up to twist the truth and draw disciples after them. He warns them for three years he never stopped warning each of them night and day with tears. He places them in God's care and the message of his grace. He never wanted anyone's silver or gold or clothing — his own hands provided for his needs and his companions. By working hard they must help the weak. And they must remember the words of the Lord Jesus — it is more blessed to give than to receive.

What You Might Not Know

This is the only speech in Acts addressed to a Christian audience rather than to Jews or pagans. Every other major speech in Acts was evangelistic or apologetic — addressed to people who needed to hear the Gospel or to authorities who needed to understand it. The Miletus speech is pastoral — addressed to leaders of an existing church who need to be equipped for what is coming. It is Paul's most personal and transparent speech in Acts. He speaks of tears, of humility, of the cost of the work, of his own integrity, and of what he expects will happen to him and to them. No other speech in Acts reveals Paul's inner life this fully.

I served the Lord with great humility and with tears in the midst of severe testing — Paul's self-description is not the self-presentation of someone who made the mission look easy. Three years in Ephesus had involved tears, humility, and severe testing. The plots of his Jewish opponents had been a constant pressure. The work had cost him emotionally and physically. Paul is giving the elders an honest account of what genuine ministry looks like from the inside — not triumphant and effortless but persistent and costly and sustained by grace rather than by natural strength.

I never hesitated to proclaim to you the whole will of God — this phrase is one of the most important in the speech. Paul had not edited the message to please his audience. He had not avoided the hard teachings because they were uncomfortable. He had not told the Ephesians what they wanted to hear instead of what they needed to hear. The whole will of God — not a curated selection of the encouraging parts. This standard — the whole counsel of God declared without hesitation — is what Paul holds up as the measure of faithful ministry. It is also his basis for claiming that he is innocent of the blood of anyone who rejected the message. He had done his part. What they did with it was their responsibility.

Savage wolves will come in among you and won't spare the flock — Paul's warning about false teachers was not vague. He expected both external threats — wolves coming in from outside — and internal corruption — men rising from within the community to twist the truth. His letters to Timothy and his letter to the Ephesian church in Revelation 2 suggest both happened. The Ephesian church's subsequent history included significant battles over false teaching. Paul had seen it coming. He told them plainly. The warning and the preparation he gave them were part of his accountability to them as their founder.

It is more blessed to give than to receive — this is the only saying of Jesus recorded in Acts that does not appear in any of the four Gospels. Paul quotes it as something the Lord Jesus himself said. It was apparently a saying of Jesus that circulated in the early Church through oral tradition. The fact that it was important enough for Paul to quote in his final address to the Ephesian elders suggests it was well-known. Its absence from the Gospels is a reminder that the written Gospels preserved a selection of Jesus's teaching rather than its entirety. John 21 says as much explicitly — the world itself could not contain all the books that would be written.

Why It Still Matters

My only aim is to finish the race and complete the task the Lord Jesus has given me. Paul's self-description of his life's purpose is one of the clearest statements of focused mission in the New Testament. Not to be safe. Not to be comfortable. Not even to survive. To finish. To complete the specific task — telling others about the good news of God's grace — that Jesus had given him. That single-mindedness did not make Paul careless about his life. It made him free from the fear of losing it. A man who has already decided that his life is not the most important thing he possesses cannot be effectively threatened by those who would take it. Paul's freedom from the fear of death was the freedom that made his ministry unstoppable.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20

A Tearful Goodbye

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 20
What Is Happening Here
