When I find it convenient — the Greek phrase kairo de metalabein literally means when I have a favorable moment or opportunity. Felix was not rejecting the Gospel. He was deferring it. The deferral felt safer than rejection and less costly than response. But indefinite deferral produces the same result as rejection while maintaining the psychological comfort of not having definitively refused. Felix sent for Paul frequently — which means he kept being confronted with the same message — and still did not respond. The conviction was real. The response was perpetually deferred.

Why It Still Matters

When I find it convenient I will send for you. Felix's response to genuine conviction is one of the saddest in Acts. He was afraid — which means the message reached him. He sent Paul away — which means he chose not to respond. He sent for Paul frequently afterward — which means the conviction kept returning. He never found the convenient time. That pattern — genuine conviction repeatedly deferred in search of a more convenient moment — is one of the most common ways people avoid the Gospel without explicitly rejecting it. The convenient time has a way of never arriving. Felix waited two years for it and then was replaced. Whether he ever found his convenient time Acts does not say. The silence is its own answer.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 24

Paul Remains in Prison

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 24
What Is Happening Here

When two years had passed Felix was succeeded by Porcius Festus. But because Felix wanted to do the Jews a favor he left Paul in prison.

What You Might Not Know

Two years — Paul spent two years in custody in Caesarea. Two years of his life and ministry held in suspension by a corrupt governor's combination of hoped-for bribery and political calculation. The man who had been compelled by the Spirit to finish the race and complete the task spent two years waiting for a governor who never found his convenient time and never received his bribe. What Paul did during those two years in terms of ministry to his visitors and possibly in terms of writing is not detailed in Acts. What is clear is that the two years were not wasted in God's economy even if they were wasted by Felix.

Porcius Festus — Felix's successor is introduced without elaboration here but will become important in Chapter 25. Festus was apparently a more conscientious administrator than Felix. Ancient sources suggest he tried to govern more justly though he faced the same intractable political pressures. His tenure was brief — he died in office around 62 AD. His arrival gave Paul's case new momentum after two years of Felix's deliberate inaction.

Felix wanted to do the Jews a favor — after two years of doing nothing with Paul's case Felix's final act regarding Paul was to leave him imprisoned as a political gift to the Jewish leadership he had failed to satisfy throughout his tenure. He was being recalled to Rome to answer charges of misgovernance. Leaving Paul in prison was a parting gesture toward the Jewish authorities whose goodwill he would need when they testified against him before the emperor. Paul's imprisonment was a chip in a political game between a corrupt governor and the religious establishment that had been trying to kill him.

Why It Still Matters

Felix left Paul in prison to do the Jews a favor. The man who had heard the Gospel and been afraid of it used Paul as a political bargaining chip on his way out of power. Paul was not a person to Felix — he was a useful object to be deployed in managing relationships with the Jewish leadership. That reduction of a person to a political instrument is what power without conscience does to the people it controls. Felix had heard about righteousness self-control and the judgment to come from the very man he was leaving in prison for political convenience. The judgment Felix deferred on the convenient occasion was still coming. He just would not be the one administering it.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 24

Acts Chapter 25

The Jews Press Their Case Against Paul

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25
What Is Happening Here

Three days after Festus arrives in the province he goes up from Caesarea to Jerusalem. The chief priests and Jewish leaders present their formal charges against Paul and beg Festus to transfer Paul to Jerusalem — they are actually planning an ambush to kill him along the way. Festus replies that Paul is being held at Caesarea and that he will be going there shortly. He tells them to send influential leaders to come down with him and present their charges if Paul has done anything wrong. After eight or ten days Festus goes down to Caesarea and the next day takes his seat on the tribunal and orders Paul brought in. The Jews who had come from Jerusalem stand around Paul and bring many serious charges — none of which they can prove.

What You Might Not Know

Three days after arriving Festus went to Jerusalem — the new governor's immediate priority was establishing his relationship with the Jewish religious leadership. Jerusalem was the center of Jewish religious and political life in the province and the high priest's cooperation was essential for maintaining order. The speed of Festus's visit suggests he understood the political landscape he had inherited from Felix. The Jewish leadership's immediate request regarding Paul showed Festus exactly what kind of political pressures he would be navigating.

They were actually planning an ambush — the assassination plot had not died with the forty men who had sworn their oath in Chapter 23. More than two years later the Jerusalem leadership was still planning to kill Paul on the road. The request to transfer Paul to Jerusalem for trial was a judicial cover story for another murder attempt. Luke notes the real intention plainly. The persistence of the plot over two years tells you something about the intensity of the opposition Paul's ministry had generated in Jerusalem's religious establishment.

Festus refused the transfer — his refusal was legally sound. Roman judicial procedure required that a case be heard where the defendant was held and that the accusers come to the defendant rather than the other way around. Festus was following proper Roman legal procedure when he told the Jerusalem leaders to come to Caesarea. Whether he knew about the ambush plot is not stated. His procedural correctness happened to protect Paul regardless of whether it was motivated by concern for Paul's safety or simply by adherence to Roman legal norms.

They couldn't prove any of them — the pattern that had held through every hearing since Paul's arrest continued. Multiple serious charges. No proof. The Jerusalem leadership had been trying for over two years to produce evidence against Paul that would satisfy a Roman court. They had not succeeded. The charges were real in the sense that they genuinely believed them. They were unsubstantiated in the sense that actual evidence for the specific legal violations alleged was absent.

Why It Still Matters

More than two years after Paul's arrest the same charges were being brought by the same people with the same inability to prove them. The persistence of the opposition and the consistent failure to substantiate the charges says something important. When people with significant resources and genuine motivation cannot prove a case over two years the most likely explanation is that the case cannot be proved because it is not true. Paul had not desecrated the Temple. He had not incited riots. He had not broken Roman law. The repeated inability to prove these charges was itself a form of vindication repeated in every court that heard them.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25

Paul Appeals to Caesar

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25
What Is Happening Here

Paul makes his defense — he has done nothing wrong against the law of the Jews the Temple or Caesar. Festus wanting to do the Jews a favor asks Paul if he is willing to go to Jerusalem and stand trial there on these charges. Paul answers — he is standing before Caesar's tribunal where he ought to be tried. He has done no wrong to the Jews as Festus himself knows very well. If he is a lawbreaker and has done anything worthy of death he is not trying to escape it. But if there is no truth to the charges no one has the right to hand him over to them. I appeal to Caesar. After conferring with his council Festus declares — you have appealed to Caesar. To Caesar you will go.

What You Might Not Know

Festus wanting to do the Jews a favor — this phrase echoes the same phrase used of Felix at the end of Chapter 24. Both governors made decisions about Paul based on political calculation rather than legal principle. The pattern reveals something systemic about Roman provincial governance — keeping the local religious establishment reasonably satisfied was a practical necessity for maintaining order, and Paul had become a pawn in that political management. The new governor was falling into the same pattern as his predecessor within days of taking office.

The provocatio ad Caesarem — Paul's appeal to Caesar was a formal legal right available to Roman citizens. It is not entirely clear whether the right of appeal to the emperor applied at this stage of proceedings — before a verdict had been rendered — or whether it was primarily a right of appeal against a verdict. Some scholars believe Paul was invoking a related right — the right to demand that his case be sent to Rome rather than tried in a potentially hostile local venue. Whatever the precise legal mechanism Festus accepted the appeal and treated it as binding. Once made the appeal could not be withdrawn and the case had to be sent to Rome.

No one has the right to hand me over to them — Paul recognized exactly what Festus's suggestion of a Jerusalem trial meant. It was not an offer of a fairer hearing. It was a capitulation to political pressure that would deliver Paul into the hands of people who had been trying to kill him for two years. The appeal to Caesar was Paul's way of removing himself from the local political game entirely. By appealing to the emperor he moved the case to a venue where Jerusalem's religious establishment had no influence and where Roman legal procedure would have to be followed without the distorting pressure of local politics.

To Caesar you will go — Festus's declaration was the fulfillment of the Lord's promise in Chapter 23. You must testify in Rome. The mechanism by which Paul would reach Rome was now set in motion — not by Paul's own planning but by Festus's politically motivated suggestion and Paul's legally astute response. The divine necessity and the human legal machinery worked together to produce the outcome the Lord had promised from a military barracks in Jerusalem two years earlier.

Why It Still Matters

If there is no truth to the charges no one has the right to hand me over to them. Paul's statement to Festus is one of the clearest articulations of legal principle in Acts. He was not asking for special treatment. He was asking for the equal application of the law that his status as a Roman citizen guaranteed. No one — not even a Roman governor trying to manage political relationships — had the right to hand an innocent man over to his accusers as a political favor. That principle — that political convenience does not override legal protection — is one the Church has had to invoke in its own defense in every generation since.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25

Festus Consults King Agrippa

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25
What Is Happening Here

A few days later King Agrippa and Bernice arrive at Caesarea to pay their respects to Festus. During their several day stay Festus explains Paul's case to Agrippa. He describes Felix leaving Paul as a prisoner and the Jerusalem leaders bringing charges and asking for condemnation. Festus had told them Roman custom does not hand over anyone before they have faced their accusers and had opportunity to defend themselves. When they came to Caesarea he sat on the tribunal the next day and ordered Paul brought in. The accusers charged Paul with none of the crimes Festus had expected — instead they had disputes about their own religion and about a dead man named Jesus whom Paul claimed was alive. Festus was at a loss as to how to investigate such matters and asked if Paul would go to Jerusalem for trial. But Paul appealed to be held for the Emperor's decision. Agrippa says he would like to hear Paul himself. Tomorrow you will hear him Festus replies.

What You Might Not Know

King Agrippa — this was Herod Agrippa II, son of the Herod Agrippa I who had executed James and been eaten by worms in Chapter 12. He had grown up in Rome and was well educated in both Jewish religious matters and Roman political culture. He had been given oversight of the Temple and the right to appoint the high priest by the Roman emperor. He was therefore the person whose understanding of Jewish religious disputes was most relevant to Festus's problem — how to write a coherent legal document explaining why he was sending a prisoner to Rome.

Bernice — she was Agrippa's sister, not his wife. She had been married twice and was currently living with her brother in circumstances that prompted gossip in both Jewish and Roman circles about an incestuous relationship. She would later become the mistress of the emperor Titus. Her presence at the hearing was simply as Agrippa's companion. Luke names her because she was present and because her identity was known to his readers. The moral complexity of the people before whom Paul made his defense is part of the honest texture of Acts.

A dead man named Jesus whom Paul claimed was alive — Festus's summary of the theological core of Paul's case is one of the most compressed and accurate descriptions of the Gospel's central claim in Acts. From a Roman governor's perspective the entire dispute reduced to this — Jewish religious leaders were upset about Paul's claim that a dead man was alive. Festus found this dispute incomprehensible as a matter for Roman courts. His incomprehension was genuine and understandable. The resurrection claim was either true or false. If false it was a theological error of no concern to Rome. If true it was the most significant event in human history. Festus had no framework for evaluating which it was.

I was at a loss as to how to investigate such matters — Festus's honesty about his inability to adjudicate Jewish theological disputes is one of the most candid moments in the final chapters of Acts. He was a Roman administrator confronted with a question that Roman administration had no tools to answer. The resurrection of Jesus was not a matter of Roman law, Roman philosophy, or Roman religious practice. Festus could verify facts about riots and temple desecration. He could not investigate whether a dead man had come back to life. His candor about his limitations was more honest than his predecessor's indefinite delays had been.

Why It Still Matters

A dead man named Jesus whom Paul claimed was alive. Festus stripped the Gospel down to its irreducible core in one clause. Every theological elaboration Paul had ever offered — justification by faith, the body of Christ, the mystery of the Gospel, the reconciliation of Jew and Gentile — rested on this single claim. If Jesus was dead the entire structure collapsed. If Jesus was alive everything Paul had been preaching was true. Festus could not decide which it was. That inability to remain neutral about the resurrection — you either believe it or you don't — is the feature of the Gospel that forces a response from everyone who encounters it seriously. Festus tried to remain neutral. The appeal to Caesar prevented him from maintaining that neutrality indefinitely.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25

Paul Is Brought Before Agrippa

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25
What Is Happening Here

The next day Agrippa and Bernice come with great ceremony and enter the audience hall with high-ranking military officers and prominent men of the city. At Festus's command Paul is brought in. Festus addresses the assembly — King Agrippa and all who are present look at this man. The whole Jewish community has petitioned about him both in Jerusalem and in Caesarea shouting that he ought not to live any longer. Festus found that he had done nothing deserving death but because he appealed to the Emperor he decided to send him. But Festus has nothing definite to write to His Majesty about Paul. He has brought Paul before them and especially before Agrippa so that after this hearing he may have something to write. It makes no sense to send a prisoner without spelling out the charges against him.

What You Might Not Know

Great ceremony — the audience hall filled with military officers and prominent citizens was the most formal and impressive setting Paul had faced since his arrest. The pomp of the occasion was Roman imperial display at its most elaborate in the provincial context. Luke's description of the ceremony is not admiring — it is setting up the contrast between the trappings of earthly power and what Paul is about to say. The man in chains before this glittering assembly will speak in the next chapter with more authority than anyone wearing a uniform or a crown in the room.

Look at this man — Festus's introduction of Paul to the assembly was an act of theatrical display as much as legal procedure. He was presenting a curiosity — a prisoner who had caused enormous controversy for two years and yet could not be charged with anything definite. The whole Jewish community had been petitioning for his death. Festus himself had found nothing worthy of death. The contradiction that Festus could not resolve he was now presenting to the most qualified person in the province to help him understand it.

I have nothing definite to write to His Majesty — Festus's practical problem was a Roman bureaucratic one. When a prisoner was sent to Rome the governor had to send a written statement of charges — a litterae dimissoriae or elogium. Without a clear legal charge Festus could not write the required document. He had a prisoner going to Caesar but no coherent case to send with him. The hearing before Agrippa was Festus's attempt to extract from Agrippa's knowledge of Jewish law some articulable charge that could be written into a Roman legal document. The hearing was as much about Festus's paperwork problem as about Paul's legal situation.

It makes no sense to send a prisoner without spelling out the charges — Festus's statement is an inadvertent summary of Paul's entire legal situation for the past two years. Every court had found nothing definite. Every charge had been unsubstantiated. The prisoner was going to Caesar without a coherent case against him. From one angle this was a legal absurdity. From another angle it was exactly the fulfillment of what Jesus had promised in Luke 21 — his followers would be brought before kings and governors as witnesses. Paul was not being sent to Rome as a criminal. He was being sent as a witness. The legal machinery that was supposed to condemn him was delivering him to the capital of the world.

Why It Still Matters

Festus found that Paul had done nothing deserving death but sent him to Caesar anyway because Paul had appealed. The Roman legal system was simultaneously protecting Paul and failing him. It protected him from assassination and from a rigged local trial. It failed him by keeping him imprisoned for two years without resolution. That mixed record — the legal system doing both good and harm to the same person at the same time — is the honest picture of how human institutions work. They are neither purely protective nor purely oppressive. Paul used what was protective and endured what was harmful and trusted the one who had said you must testify in Rome to bring it all to the purpose he had declared.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 25

Acts Chapter 26

Paul Addresses King Agrippa

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26
What Is Happening Here

Agrippa gives Paul permission to speak. Paul motions with his hand and begins his defense. He considers himself fortunate to stand before Agrippa who is well-versed in Jewish customs and controversies. The Jewish people have known Paul since childhood — in his own country and in Jerusalem. They can testify if they are willing that he lived as a Pharisee in the strictest sect of the religion. He is on trial because of his hope in what God promised the ancestors — the promise the twelve tribes are hoping to see fulfilled as they earnestly serve God day and night. It is because of this hope that the Jews are accusing him. Why should anyone think it impossible that God raises the dead?

What You Might Not Know

Paul considers himself fortunate — the opening is not sycophantic flattery like Tertullus's opening before Felix. Paul's statement that he considers himself fortunate to stand before Agrippa is theologically grounded. Agrippa was the most qualified person before whom Paul had yet appeared to discuss the Jewish hope and its fulfillment in Jesus. A Roman governor like Festus had no framework for evaluating Paul's claims. Agrippa did. He knew the Scriptures, knew the messianic expectations, knew the disputes within Judaism. Paul was genuinely glad to have an audience capable of following the argument he was about to make.

The promise our twelve tribes are hoping to see fulfilled — Paul frames the entire dispute as being about the hope of Israel not about a new religion. The resurrection hope was not invented by Paul or by the early Church. It was the ancient hope of the twelve tribes expressed in the prophets and earnestly pursued by faithful Jews through generations of prayer and service. Paul was not departing from Jewish hope. He was proclaiming that Jewish hope had been fulfilled. The charges against him for following Jesus were therefore a prosecution of someone for believing what Israel had always hoped for.

Why should any of you think it impossible that God raises the dead — Paul addresses this question directly to Agrippa and the assembled dignitaries. The question is not rhetorical decoration. It is the central issue. If God is the creator of life the claim that he can restore it is not philosophically impossible — it would only be surprising if he had not. The impossibility of resurrection is an assumption that needs examining rather than a conclusion that needs defending. Paul is asking his audience to interrogate their assumptions before dismissing the claim.

Why It Still Matters

Paul's opening to Agrippa connects his personal situation to the ancient hope of all Israel. He is not defending a personal eccentricity or a foreign religion. He is standing in the line of every prophet and faithful Israelite who had ever prayed for God to fulfill his promises. The charges against him were charges against the hope itself. Agrippa — who knew that hope from the inside — was the right person to hear that framing. The Gospel is not a departure from the story of God and Israel. It is the story's fulfillment. Paul states that plainly to the one person in the room most likely to understand what it means.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26

Paul Describes His Former Life

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26
What Is Happening Here

Paul was convinced he had to do everything possible to oppose the name of Jesus of Nazareth and that is exactly what he did in Jerusalem. On the authority of the chief priests he put many of God's people in prison. When they were put to death he cast his vote against them. Many times he went from synagogue to synagogue to have them punished and tried to force them to blaspheme. He was so obsessed with persecuting them that he even hunted them down in foreign cities.

What You Might Not Know

When they were put to death I cast my vote against them — this detail appears only in the Agrippa account and not in the earlier tellings of Paul's conversion. The Greek phrase katenenka psephon means I cast my vote or I gave my pebble — referring to the practice of casting a pebble into a vessel to register a vote. This implies Paul may have been a member of the Sanhedrin or at minimum had official voting authority in judicial proceedings. If so it means Paul was not just a bystander who approved — he was an active participant in capital decisions against believers. The weight of his guilt was even greater than the earlier accounts had implied.

I tried to force them to blaspheme — this detail is also unique to this account. Paul was not satisfied with imprisoning believers. He tried to make them renounce their faith — to publicly deny Jesus. The attempt to force blasphemy was an attempt to destroy not just the person but their testimony. Every believer who was forced to blaspheme became one less witness to the resurrection. Paul's persecution was strategically aimed at the theological foundation of the movement — the testimony of those who had encountered Jesus.

I was so obsessed that I even hunted them down in foreign cities — the Greek word perissōs means excessively or beyond measure. Paul's persecution was not measured or proportionate. It was obsessive. He pursued believers across international boundaries. The man who would later travel the entire Roman world to plant churches had first traveled it to destroy them. The scope of his former activity and the scope of his later ministry were mirror images of the same intensity directed in completely opposite directions.

Why It Still Matters

Paul's confession of his former life before Agrippa is the most detailed and the most damning he gives in any of his three accounts. He was not softening his history for a sympathetic audience. He was giving the full weight of what he had been to the most qualified audience he had. The depth of his former opposition to Jesus makes the reality of his conversion more rather than less credible. A man does not abandon a position he held with that kind of violent obsession unless something genuinely extraordinary happened to him. Paul's willingness to confess the full extent of his former persecution is itself a piece of evidence for the authenticity of what changed him.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26

The Road to Damascus

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26
What Is Happening Here

On one of his journeys to Damascus with authority and commission of the chief priests about noon Paul saw a light from heaven brighter than the sun blazing around him and his companions. They all fell to the ground and Paul heard a voice saying in his own language — Saul Saul why do you persecute me? It is hard for you to kick against the goads. Paul asked who it was. I am Jesus whom you are persecuting the Lord replied. Now get up and stand on your feet. I have appeared to you to appoint you as a servant and witness of what you have seen and will see. I will rescue you from your own people and from the Gentiles. I am sending you to them to open their eyes and turn them from darkness to light and from the power of Satan to God so that they may receive forgiveness of sins and a place among those who are made holy by faith in me.

What You Might Not Know

Brighter than the sun — this detail appears only in the Agrippa account. In Chapter 22 Paul described a bright light from heaven. Here he specifies it was brighter than the midday sun — and the encounter happened about noon when the Syrian sun would have been at its peak intensity. The light that knocked Paul and his companions to the ground outshone the brightest natural light available. Luke is being precise about the physical phenomenon — this was not a dream or a vision in the ordinary sense. It was an overwhelming external event witnessed by multiple people in full daylight.

It is hard for you to kick against the goads — this phrase appears only in the Agrippa account and not in the earlier tellings. A goad was a sharp pointed stick used to prod oxen. An ox that kicked against the goad only wounded itself further. The image suggests that Paul had been resisting something — perhaps his own doubts about what he was doing, perhaps the testimony of believers he had heard before imprisoning them, perhaps the memory of Stephen's death and the forgiveness in Stephen's face. Jesus was suggesting that the persecution had not been as uncomplicated as Paul's certainty implied. Something had been prodding him and he had been kicking against it.

I have appeared to you to appoint you as a servant and as a witness — the commission Jesus gives Paul in this account is the most detailed of the three versions. Paul is appointed as both a servant and a witness — doulos and martys. The servant language indicates subordination and ongoing obedience. The witness language indicates testimony — someone who reports what they have personally seen and heard. Both are required. Service without witness produces activity without proclamation. Witness without service produces words without integrity.

To open their eyes and turn them from darkness to light and from the power of Satan to God — this description of Paul's mission to the Gentiles is the most comprehensive statement of what the Gospel does in Acts. It is not just intellectual enlightenment. It is a transfer of allegiance and a change of power. The Gentiles are under the power of Satan and in darkness. The Gospel brings them into the light and transfers them to God's power. The language is cosmic and personal simultaneously — it describes both a change in the spiritual reality governing their lives and a change in their individual standing before God.

Why It Still Matters

It is hard for you to kick against the goads. Jesus's words to Paul on the Damascus road suggest that conversion is sometimes preceded by a sustained resistance to something that has been pressing on a person for some time. Paul had been in contact with believers he had imprisoned. He had heard their testimony. He had watched Stephen die with forgiveness on his lips. Something had been working on him that his fierce certainty had been suppressing. The Damascus road encounter was the moment the resistance finally gave way. That pattern — prolonged resistance preceding sudden surrender — is not unique to Paul. The goads God uses take different forms but the resistance and eventual yielding are recognizable across many conversion stories.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26

Paul Obeys the Heavenly Vision

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26
What Is Happening Here

Paul was not disobedient to the vision from heaven. First to those in Damascus then to those in Jerusalem and in all Judea and then to the Gentiles he preached that they should turn away from their sins and turn to God and prove their change of heart by the way they lived. That is why some Jews seized him in the Temple courts and tried to kill him. But God has helped him to this very day so he stands and testifies to small and great alike. He is saying nothing beyond what the prophets and Moses said would happen — that the Messiah would suffer and as the first to rise from the dead would bring the message of light to his own people and to the Gentiles.

What You Might Not Know

I was not disobedient to the vision — Paul's summary of his entire missionary career is framed as obedience to a single heavenly vision. Everything from Damascus to Jerusalem to Judea to the Gentile world was the outworking of what Jesus had commissioned him to do on the road. The three missionary journeys the churches across the Roman world the letters the suffering — all of it was one act of obedience extended across decades. Paul did not have a series of independent ministry decisions. He had one commission that he spent his life executing.

Prove their change of heart by the way they lived — Paul's summary of his preaching to Gentiles included ethical expectation alongside theological proclamation. He was not preaching a Gospel that left behavior untouched. Repentance was demonstrated in life change. The Greek word axios means worthily or in a manner consistent with — living in a way that matched the reality of the changed allegiance. This is Paul's characteristically integrated understanding of the Gospel — it changes what you believe and how you live and the two are inseparable.

God has helped me to this very day — Paul's summary of his protection through years of persecution is attributed entirely to God. He does not catalog his own resilience or strategic intelligence. He has survived riots stonings beatings shipwrecks conspiracies and two years of unjust imprisonment because God has helped him. The passive voice of divine protection runs through this summary. Paul was not a self-made survivor. He was a sustained servant.

Nothing beyond what the prophets and Moses said would happen — Paul's final claim to Agrippa is that his entire message was scriptural. The suffering Messiah the resurrection the light to the Gentiles — all of it was in the Hebrew Scriptures that Agrippa knew. Paul was not inventing a new theology. He was reading the existing theology of Israel and showing how it had been fulfilled. Agrippa had the texts. He had heard the testimony. He had the tools to evaluate the claim. What he did with that knowledge was his responsibility.

Why It Still Matters

Paul stands before the most powerful audience of his ministry career and says — I am saying nothing beyond what Moses and the prophets said would happen. His defense before kings and governors was not a new message dressed up for a prestigious audience. It was the same message he had preached in synagogues and marketplaces and prisons — the suffering and resurrection of the Messiah and the resulting light to the nations. The Gospel does not need to be upgraded for important audiences. It needs to be stated clearly. Paul's consistency across every audience from Lystra peasants to Roman kings is one of the marks of a message he genuinely believed rather than a performance he adjusted for each crowd.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26

Festus Calls Paul Crazy

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26
What Is Happening Here

Festus interrupts Paul's defense and shouts — you are out of your mind Paul your great learning is driving you insane. Paul replies — I am not insane Most Excellent Festus. What he is saying is true and makes perfect sense. The king is familiar with these things and Paul can speak freely to him. He is convinced that none of this has escaped Agrippa's notice because it was not done in a corner. King Agrippa do you believe the prophets — I know you do. Then Agrippa said to Paul — do you think that in such a short time you can persuade me to be a Christian. Paul replied — short time or long I pray to God that not only Agrippa but all who are listening today may become what I am except for these chains.

What You Might Not Know

Your great learning is driving you insane — Festus's interruption was the reaction of a man who had been following Paul's argument as far as Roman education could take him and had hit the wall of the resurrection. Everything before the resurrection Paul had said was coherent and verifiable. The resurrection was the point at which Festus's framework could not accommodate what he was hearing. His diagnosis — too much learning producing madness — was the educated Roman's way of saying this conclusion does not fit my categories. Festus was not stupid. He was operating from a worldview that had no place for bodily resurrection and his response to encountering the claim was to pathologize the person making it.

It was not done in a corner — Paul's response to Festus is one of his most important statements in the final chapters of Acts. The events surrounding Jesus — his ministry his death his reported resurrection his appearances to multiple witnesses — had happened publicly in Jerusalem during a major festival when the city was full of pilgrims from across the empire. This was not a private mystical experience or a rumor from a distant province. It was public events with multiple witnesses in the most watched city in the Jewish world. The Gospel's claims were historically grounded and publicly verifiable in principle even if Festus had not personally investigated them.

King Agrippa do you believe the prophets — Paul turns from Festus who cannot follow the argument to Agrippa who can. The question is a direct challenge. Agrippa knew the prophetic texts. He knew what they said about the Messiah. If he believed the prophets then everything Paul had been saying followed. Paul is pressing Agrippa toward a conclusion that Agrippa's own knowledge of Scripture should support. The question was not rhetorical. It required an answer.

Do you think that in such a short time you can persuade me to be a Christian — Agrippa's response has been translated and interpreted many ways. Some read it as dismissive — you really think you can convert me that quickly. Others read it as genuine engagement — are you actually trying to make a Christian of me. The Greek is ambiguous. What is clear is that Agrippa did not answer Paul's question about the prophets. He deflected it with a question about Paul's intentions. The deflection was a way of avoiding the direct answer that Paul's question required.

Short time or long I pray that all who are listening today may become what I am except for these chains — Paul's response to Agrippa is one of the most gracious and bold statements in Acts. He does not argue. He does not press. He states his heart — he would genuinely like everyone in that room to come to the same faith he has. The one qualification — except for these chains — acknowledges his imprisonment without bitterness and with a touch of dry wit. He wants them to have his faith. He does not want them to have his legal situation. The chains were incidental. The faith was the point.

Why It Still Matters

Short time or long I pray that all who are listening today may become what I am except for these chains. Paul's final statement to the most powerful assembly he ever addressed was a prayer for their conversion. Not an argument. Not a condemnation. A prayer. The man in chains before the king and the governor and the military officers and the prominent citizens of Caesarea expressed his genuine desire that every person in that room would come to the same faith he held. The chains on his wrists did not qualify the prayer. They were the incidental circumstance of a free man — free in the way that mattered most — standing before people who were less free than they appeared.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26

The Verdict

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26
What Is Happening Here

The king rises and with him the governor and Bernice and those sitting with them. As they leave the room they say to one another — this man is not doing anything that deserves death or imprisonment. Agrippa said to Festus — this man could have been set free if he had not appealed to Caesar.

What You Might Not Know

This man is not doing anything that deserves death or imprisonment — this is the fourth declaration of Paul's innocence by a Roman or Roman-affiliated authority in Acts. Claudius Lysias the commander had found no basis for charges. Felix had found nothing deserving death. Festus had found nothing deserving death. Now Agrippa and the assembled dignitaries reach the same conclusion. The cumulative weight of these declarations is part of Luke's argument throughout the final section of Acts — the Roman legal system, whatever its faults, consistently found Paul innocent of the charges brought against him. The Gospel was not a criminal enterprise. Its leading apostle was not a lawbreaker.

This man could have been set free if he had not appealed to Caesar — Agrippa's observation is accurate and ironic simultaneously. Paul's appeal to Caesar had been legally sound and strategically necessary given the real threat of being handed over to Jerusalem. But it had also locked him into a legal process that would keep him imprisoned until Rome resolved the case. The appeal that protected Paul from assassination also extended his imprisonment. The instrument of his protection was also the instrument of his continued confinement. Agrippa saw the legal irony clearly. He did not see — or did not say — that the appeal was also the mechanism by which the Lord's word to Paul would be fulfilled. Paul must testify in Rome. The appeal was the how.

The hearing before Agrippa produced no legal resolution — Paul was already going to Caesar and the hearing could not change that. What it produced was the closest thing to an official verdict Paul received anywhere in Acts — a consensus among the most qualified legal and political minds available that he had done nothing worthy of death or imprisonment. That consensus could not free him. But it was recorded. Luke preserved it. The innocence of the Gospel's apostle was on record in the proceedings of the Roman provincial government of Judea.

Why It Still Matters

This man could have been set free. The observation is true and it stands as a quiet indictment of every point in the process where a different decision could have produced a different outcome. Claudius Lysias could have investigated before chaining. Felix could have ruled rather than delayed. Festus could have released rather than offered Jerusalem. At each point someone with authority to do justice chose something other than justice for reasons of procedure or politics or personal gain. Paul went to Rome not because the system worked but because it consistently failed — and because the Lord had said he must testify there anyway. The justice Paul was denied by the system God used as the vehicle for a larger purpose.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 26

Acts Chapter 27

Paul Sets Sail for Italy

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

When it is decided that they will sail for Italy Paul and some other prisoners are handed over to a centurion named Julius of the Imperial Regiment. They board a ship from Adramyttium about to sail for ports along the coast of Asia. Aristarchus a Macedonian from Thessalonica is with them. The next day they land at Sidon where Julius in kindness to Paul allows him to visit friends who provide for his needs. They put out to sea again and pass to the sheltered side of Cyprus because the winds are against them. After sailing across the open sea off Cilicia and Pamphylia they land at Myra in Lycia where the centurion finds an Alexandrian ship sailing for Italy and puts them on board.

What You Might Not Know

The we-narrative continues throughout Chapter 27 — Luke was present for the entire voyage and shipwreck. The level of nautical detail in this chapter is extraordinary and has been verified by historians and sailors who have studied ancient Mediterranean seamanship. The specific winds the ports the depths the distances the techniques used in the storm — all correspond accurately to what ancient sailors would have done in those conditions. Luke was not embellishing a story. He was recording what he personally experienced with the precision of someone who paid close attention to everything happening around him.

Julius of the Imperial Regiment — the centurion responsible for Paul and the other prisoners showed Paul consistent respect and kindness throughout the voyage. He allowed Paul to visit friends in Sidon. He later listened to Paul's advice during the storm and ultimately saved Paul's life by preventing the soldiers from killing the prisoners. Julius was not a believer as far as Acts indicates but he was a man whose basic decency repeatedly worked in Paul's favor. God used a Roman soldier's personal respect for a prisoner to protect the apostle through a dangerous journey.

Aristarchus a Macedonian from Thessalonica — he had been seized by the Ephesian mob in Chapter 19 and had been traveling with Paul through Macedonia and Greece. He was present at the farewell in Troas. He was on the ship to Rome. Paul's letter to the Colossians written during his imprisonment mentions Aristarchus as a fellow prisoner. He had been with Paul through some of the most dangerous moments of the third journey and stayed with him all the way to Rome. His faithfulness across years of dangerous travel is part of the quiet testimony of Acts.

An Alexandrian ship sailing for Italy — the grain ships that traveled between Alexandria in Egypt and Rome were among the largest vessels in the ancient Mediterranean. Egypt supplied a significant portion of Rome's grain and the Alexandrian grain fleet was a major commercial enterprise. These were large ships capable of carrying hundreds of passengers and significant cargo. Paul's transfer to an Alexandrian grain ship at Myra was a transfer to a vessel large enough to make the open sea crossing to Italy — and also large enough to carry the 276 people whose number Luke carefully records later in the chapter.

Why It Still Matters

Julius in kindness to Paul allowed him to go to his friends. The journey to Rome began with an act of human kindness from the man responsible for Paul as a prisoner. That kindness — allowing a prisoner to receive care from his community — set the tone for a relationship that would eventually save Paul's life. Small acts of basic decency have consequences that cannot be calculated at the time they are extended. Julius had no idea when he let Paul visit friends in Sidon that he was building a relationship of trust that would later lead him to protect Paul from his own soldiers. Kindness compounds in ways the one who extends it cannot foresee.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

Dangerous Sailing

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

They make slow headway for many days and have difficulty arriving off Cnidus. When the wind will not let them hold their course they sail to the sheltered side of Crete opposite Salmone. Moving along the coast with difficulty they come to Fair Havens near the town of Lasea. Much time has been lost and sailing has become dangerous because it is past the Day of Fasting. Paul warns them that the voyage will be disastrous and bring great loss to ship cargo and lives. But the centurion follows the advice of the pilot and the ship's owner rather than Paul. Since Fair Havens is unsuitable for wintering the majority decide to sail on hoping to reach Phoenix and winter there.

What You Might Not Know

Past the Day of Fasting — the Day of Fasting was Yom Kippur the Jewish Day of Atonement which fell in late September or early October. Ancient Mediterranean sailors generally considered open sea sailing dangerous after mid-September and closed for the season after early November. The fact that Paul and his companions were still at sea past Yom Kippur meant they were in the dangerous window of autumn sailing. The conditions that produced the subsequent storm were the conditions ancient Mediterranean experience had taught sailors to avoid. Paul's warning was not prophetic insight alone — it was the observation of a man who had made multiple sea voyages and understood the seasonal risks.

The centurion followed the advice of the pilot and the ship's owner — the decision to press on was made by majority vote among the relevant stakeholders. The pilot and the ship's owner had economic interests in reaching a better harbor. The centurion had military interests in completing the journey. Paul was a prisoner with no financial stake and no official expertise in navigation. The decision to ignore his warning was procedurally reasonable even if it proved catastrophically wrong. The people with formal authority and relevant expertise overruled the prisoner with prophetic insight. That is how human decision-making usually works — and it is often wrong in exactly this way.

Fair Havens was unsuitable for wintering — the harbor was real and has been identified on the southern coast of Crete. Ancient sources confirm it was not well-protected enough for extended winter anchorage. The majority's preference for Phoenix was not irrational — Phoenix was a better harbor. The problem was the gamble of getting there given the season and the winds. The distance from Fair Havens to Phoenix was only about forty miles but those forty miles would prove fatal to the ship.

Why It Still Matters

Paul warned them and they did not listen. The warning was accurate. The storm came. The ship was lost. The warning being ignored did not make Paul bitter or vindictive — when he stood before them in the storm he did not dwell on being right. He told them what God had promised about their survival and focused on what needed to happen next. Being right when others ignored your warning is a test of character. The temptation to say I told you so is real. Paul passed the test. He had been right about the danger and he moved immediately to being useful in the crisis without dwelling on the failure of those who had not listened to him.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

The Storm Hits

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

When a gentle south wind begins to blow they see their opportunity and weigh anchor sailing along the shore of Crete. Before long a wind of hurricane force called the Northeaster sweeps down from the island. The ship cannot head into the wind so they give way to it and are driven along. Passing to the sheltered side of the island of Cauda they barely manage to secure the lifeboat and hoist it aboard. They pass ropes under the ship to hold it together. Fearing the sandbars of Syrtis they lower the sea anchor and let the ship be driven. The next day they begin throwing cargo overboard. On the third day they throw the ship's tackle overboard with their own hands. When neither sun nor stars appear for many days and the storm keeps raging they finally give up all hope of being saved.

What You Might Not Know

The Northeaster — the Greek word is Eurakylon a compound of the Greek word for east and the Latin word for north. Modern meteorologists have confirmed that this type of storm — a violent northeast wind that sweeps down from the mountains of Crete — is consistent with the weather patterns of that region in autumn. The storm Luke describes is not a legendary embellishment. It is a recognizable meteorological phenomenon that ancient Mediterranean sailors feared and named. Its sudden eruption after a gentle south wind was exactly the kind of weather trap experienced sailors knew to avoid in that season.

They passed ropes under the ship — the technique Luke describes is called frapping or undergirding. Large wooden ships in heavy seas could flex and work apart at the joints. Running ropes under the hull and tightening them above deck was a recognized emergency technique for holding the hull together under stress. The fact that Luke records this specific technical detail is another marker of eyewitness accuracy. A person fabricating or embellishing the story would not know to include this detail or describe it accurately.

The sandbars of Syrtis — the Syrtis was the name for the shallow waters and sandbars off the North African coast near modern Libya — notorious in antiquity as a ship trap. Being driven by the northeast wind southwestward toward Syrtis was exactly what an ancient sailor in this storm would have feared most. The sea anchor they lowered was intended to slow the ship's drift and give them more control over their direction. The technical knowledge embedded in Luke's narrative is consistent throughout the chapter.

We finally gave up all hope of being saved — the Greek word for gave up is periaireo meaning to take away completely or strip away. All hope had been stripped from them. This was not discouragement or pessimism. It was the rational conclusion of experienced sailors who had done everything seamanship could do and had run out of options. The ship was likely waterlogged, its cargo and tackle were gone, navigation was impossible without sun or stars, and they had been driven helplessly for days. Human competence had reached its limit. The moment was prepared for what Paul was about to say.

Why It Still Matters

They gave up all hope of being saved. The storm had stripped away every human resource — the cargo the tackle the ability to navigate the collective competence of experienced sailors. When human ability reaches its absolute limit and hope is completely gone something else becomes possible. Paul's announcement of God's promise came at the exact moment when no human alternative remained. The word that cuts through despair requires that despair to be complete before it can be fully heard. God did not speak through Paul while the sailors still thought they could save themselves. He spoke when they had nothing left.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

Paul Encourages the Crew

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

After they have gone a long time without food Paul stands up before them. He acknowledges they should have taken his advice not to sail from Crete — that would have spared them this damage and loss. But now he urges them to keep up their courage because not one of them will be lost — only the ship will be destroyed. Last night an angel of the God to whom Paul belongs and whom he serves stood beside him and said — do not be afraid Paul you must stand trial before Caesar and God has graciously given you the lives of all who sail with you. So keep up your courage men — Paul has faith in God that it will happen just as he was told. They will run aground on some island.

What You Might Not Know

Paul stood up — the physical act of standing up in a ship being battered by a hurricane after days without food among people who had given up all hope required something. Paul was not a young man. He had been beaten flogged imprisoned and shipwrecked before. His body carried the accumulated damage of years of physical hardship. His standing up before that desperate crew was an act of physical courage that preceded and embodied the spiritual message he was about to deliver.

I told you so — Paul's brief acknowledgment that his earlier warning had been correct is the one concession to human nature before he moves entirely to encouragement. He does not dwell on it. He states it and immediately pivots — but now I urge you to keep up your courage. The acknowledgment was honest rather than bitter. He was establishing his credibility as a source of reliable information before delivering the promise. If he had been right about the danger they were more likely to believe him about the survival. The brief reference to his previous warning was pastoral setup not vindictive self-justification.

The God to whom I belong and whom I serve — Paul identifies God in relational terms before any other terms. Not the God of the Jews. Not the creator of the universe. The God to whom I belong. The possessive runs the other direction from what one might expect — not the God I believe in but the God who has me. Paul's relationship to God was one of belonging before it was one of believing. That ordering — belonging first then serving — is characteristic of Paul's theology. He was not primarily a religious practitioner. He was a person owned by a God who had met him on a road outside Damascus.

God has graciously given you the lives of all who sail with you — the Greek word kecharistaí comes from the same root as grace — charis. God has graced Paul with the lives of the 276 people on the ship. Their survival was a gift given to Paul — not because they deserved it but because Paul was on the ship and God was protecting his apostle's mission to Rome. The 276 people would survive the shipwreck not because of their own merit but because they happened to be on the same ship as God's appointed witness to Caesar. They were beneficiaries of a grace directed primarily at someone else.

Why It Still Matters

Keep up your courage — I have faith in God that it will happen just as he told me. Paul's encouragement to the crew was grounded in a specific promise he had received rather than in a general optimism about outcomes. He was not telling them things would work out because things usually do. He was telling them a specific angel had delivered a specific promise from a specific God who had a specific purpose for this voyage. The courage he was calling them to was not courage based on favorable odds. It was courage based on a word from God. That is the only kind of courage that holds when the odds are genuinely terrible.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

Approaching Land

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

On the fourteenth night still being driven across the Adriatic Sea about midnight the sailors sense they are approaching land. They take soundings and find the water is a hundred and twenty feet deep. A short time later soundings show ninety feet. Fearing they will be dashed against the rocks they drop four anchors from the stern and pray for daylight. Some sailors try to escape from the ship by lowering the lifeboat pretending to lower anchors from the bow. Paul tells the centurion and soldiers — unless these men stay with the ship you cannot be saved. The soldiers cut the ropes holding the lifeboat and let it drift away.

What You Might Not Know

The fourteenth night — Luke tracks the days of the voyage with precision. The fourteen days from leaving Fair Havens to approaching land is consistent with what modern calculations of wind speed and drift suggest would have brought a ship from Crete to Malta in a northeast storm. The bay where the ship eventually ran aground has been identified as St. Paul's Bay on the northeast coast of Malta. The depth soundings Luke records — 120 feet then 90 feet — correspond to the actual seabed topography of the approach to that bay. The navigational details have been verified by modern maritime historians as accurate.

They sensed they were approaching land — the Greek word huponooumenous can mean to suspect or to perceive dimly. Ancient sailors developed a sensitivity to subtle changes in the sound and feel of the water and the smell of the air that could signal proximity to land even in darkness. The sailors' perception was likely based on the sound of breaking surf or changes in the wave patterns rather than anything they could see. The experienced crew was reading their environment with skills developed over years of seafaring.

Unless these men stay with the ship you cannot be saved — Paul's intervention to stop the sailors from escaping in the lifeboat was a practical judgment that contradicted his earlier message of guaranteed survival. His message had been that all would be saved. But all would be saved if they stayed with the ship — not if the experienced sailors abandoned it leaving 276 passengers and soldiers to navigate a damaged ship onto a rocky shore without skilled seamanship. The divine promise and the human responsibility worked together. God guaranteed the outcome. He did not guarantee it regardless of what the humans did.

Why It Still Matters

Unless these men stay with the ship you cannot be saved. The divine promise of survival did not make human action irrelevant. Paul had been given God's guarantee that all 276 would survive. He also intervened decisively to prevent the action that would have made the guarantee impossible to fulfill. The sovereignty of God and the necessity of human action are held together in one sentence. The promise was certain. The means by which it would be fulfilled required people to do the right thing. God's purposes are not achieved despite human choices but through them — which means the choices still matter even when the outcome is guaranteed.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

Paul Urges Everyone to Eat

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

Just before dawn Paul urges them all to eat. For fourteen days they have been in constant suspense going without food — not eating anything. Now he urges them to eat because they need it to survive. Not one of them will lose a single hair from his head. After saying this he takes some bread gives thanks to God in front of them all breaks it and begins to eat. They are all encouraged and eat some food themselves. There are 276 people on board altogether. When they have eaten as much as they want they lighten the ship by throwing the grain into the sea.

What You Might Not Know

You haven't eaten anything — fourteen days without food during a violent storm was physiologically serious. The combination of seasickness stress cold and exhaustion would have depleted everyone on board. Paul's instruction to eat was not just practical comfort. It was medically necessary preparation for what was coming — the physical demands of abandoning a grounded ship and swimming or floating to shore. People who had not eaten for fourteen days would have diminished strength for exactly the kind of physical exertion the next few hours would require. Paul was thinking practically about survival even while announcing divine promises about it.

He took some bread gave thanks to God in front of them all broke it and began to eat — the language is unmistakably eucharistic. The same four actions — taking giving thanks breaking eating — appear in every account of the Last Supper and in the feeding of the five thousand. Luke uses this language deliberately. Paul is doing on a storm-damaged ship in the Adriatic in front of 276 frightened people what Jesus had done in the upper room. The meal before the shipwreck echoes the meal before the crucifixion. The breaking of bread in the face of death was an act of faith that what lay ahead was not the end.

Not one of you will lose a single hair from his head — this proverbial expression for complete safety appears in Luke's Gospel on the lips of Jesus — not a hair of your head will perish. Paul is using Jesus's own language of assurance. The promise was specific and it was fulfilled — all 276 people reached shore safely. The hair idiom was not a vague comfort. It was a precise claim that was verifiable after the fact.

Two hundred and seventy-six of us on board — Luke counts them. The precision of the number is characteristic of his eyewitness account throughout Chapter 27. He knew how many people were on the ship because he was one of them and someone counted or the number was known from the ship's manifest. The specific number also serves Luke's later point — all 276 survived. The exactness of the count makes the exactness of the survival more striking.

Why It Still Matters

Paul gave thanks to God in front of them all. The prayer before the meal was public and deliberate. There was no privacy about who Paul was thanking or why. In front of 276 frightened people including soldiers prisoners sailors and whatever passengers were aboard Paul took bread and gave thanks to the God whose angel had stood beside him the previous night. The meal was both a practical preparation for the ordeal ahead and a public act of worship in the middle of a crisis. The two were inseparable for Paul. Eating and giving thanks were the same act — the body being sustained and the God who sustains acknowledged in the same gesture.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

The Shipwreck

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27
What Is Happening Here

When daylight comes they do not recognize the land but see a bay with a sandy beach where they decide to run the ship aground if possible. They cut loose the anchors and leave them in the sea untying the ropes holding the rudders. They hoist the foresail to the wind and make for the beach. But the ship strikes a sandbar and runs aground. The bow sticks fast and will not move and the stern is broken to pieces by the pounding surf. The soldiers plan to kill the prisoners to prevent them from swimming away and escaping. But the centurion wanting to spare Paul's life keeps them from carrying out their plan. He orders those who can swim to jump overboard first and get to land. The rest are to get there on planks or pieces of the ship. In this way everyone reaches land safely.

What You Might Not Know

The soldiers planned to kill the prisoners — Roman military law held soldiers personally responsible for prisoners who escaped. If a prisoner escaped the soldier guarding him could face the same punishment the prisoner would have received. With a ship breaking apart and prisoners potentially swimming to freedom the soldiers' plan to execute them was procedurally rational under Roman military law. It was also something the centurion overrode. Julius had protected Paul throughout the voyage. At the moment of maximum danger when Paul's life was most immediately threatened by the soldiers responsible for guarding him Julius intervened again. His consistent protection of Paul across the entire voyage culminated in this final act of keeping his own soldiers from killing the man he had come to respect.

In this way everyone reached land safely — the Greek word diasothénai means to be thoroughly saved or to come through safely to the other side. Luke uses the strong form of the verb — not just saved but completely and thoroughly saved. Every single one of the 276 people on the ship reached shore. The divine promise delivered by an angel to Paul in the middle of the storm was fulfilled with complete precision. Not 275. Not most. Everyone. The God who makes promises keeps them exactly.

The bay with a sandy beach — the location has been identified with considerable confidence as St. Paul's Bay on the northeast coast of Malta. The sandbar where the ship ran aground has been identified through archaeological investigation. The specific geography Luke describes — the bay the sandy beach the sandbar where the bow stuck and the stern broke apart — corresponds to the actual physical features of that location. The shipwreck of Acts 27 is one of the best-attested events in the entire book from the standpoint of archaeological and geographical verification.

Why It Still Matters

Everyone reached land safely. The promise given in the middle of the storm was kept in the chaos of the shipwreck. Planks and swimming and pieces of broken ship — the means were improvised and desperate. The outcome was exact. That is how God's promises tend to be fulfilled in Acts — not in the way anyone would have planned or predicted but with the precision of an outcome that was determined before the means were visible. The 276 people who waded ashore on Malta that morning did not know they had been the beneficiaries of a promise made to one man because God needed that man to testify in Rome. They just knew they were alive. That was enough.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 27

Acts Chapter 28

Shipwrecked on Malta

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28
What Is Happening Here

Once safely on shore they learn the island is called Malta. The local people show extraordinary kindness — they build a fire and welcome everyone because it is raining and cold. Paul gathers brushwood and as he puts it on the fire a viper driven out by the heat fastens itself on his hand. The islanders say to each other — this man must be a murderer for though he escaped from the sea the goddess Justice has not allowed him to live. But Paul shakes the snake off into the fire and suffers no ill effects. The people expect him to swell up or suddenly fall dead but after waiting a long time and seeing nothing unusual happen they change their minds and say he is a god.

What You Might Not Know

Extraordinary kindness — the Greek word philanthropy literally means love of humanity. The Maltese islanders showed Paul and the 276 survivors the kind of spontaneous generous hospitality that transcends cultural and religious boundaries. They did not know who these people were or why they had shipwrecked. They built a fire in the rain for strangers. Luke records this act of basic human kindness with the same care he records the miracles and speeches. The barbarian hospitality of Malta was itself a kind of testimony — the image of God in people who did not know the Gospel expressing itself in practical love for those in need.

A viper fastened on his hand — the bite of a viper in the ancient Mediterranean was generally understood to be fatal. The islanders' interpretation — that Paul must be a murderer being punished by divine justice — reflected the common ancient assumption that suffering was retributive. The goddess Justice also called Dike was believed to pursue wrongdoers who escaped other forms of punishment. The snake attack was interpreted as the arrival of delayed cosmic justice for someone whose crimes had caught up with him.

Paul shook the snake off into the fire — the lack of drama in Luke's description is striking. Paul did not pray over the bite or make a speech. He shook the snake off and went back to gathering wood. The miracle was almost incidental in his behavior if not in the observers' reaction. Jesus had promised his disciples in Luke 10 that they would trample on snakes and scorpions and nothing will harm you. The promise was being fulfilled on a Maltese beach in the rain without Paul apparently giving it a second thought.

They changed their minds and said he was a god — the Maltese crowd swung from murderer to god in a matter of minutes. The same crowd psychology that had turned on Paul in Jerusalem and Ephesus was now divinizing him on Malta. Paul's response to this kind of crowd reaction had been consistent throughout Acts — he refused divine honors in Lystra and tore his clothes in protest. Luke does not record Paul correcting the Maltese interpretation explicitly though the healing ministry that follows suggests he directed their attention toward the God he served rather than toward himself.

Why It Still Matters

The local people showed extraordinary kindness. The final journey of Acts opens with an act of human hospitality so notable that Luke uses the word philanthropy to describe it. The people of Malta did not know Paul was an apostle. They did not know he was going to Rome to testify before Caesar. They knew it was cold and raining and that people had just survived a shipwreck. They built a fire. That response — seeing need and meeting it without requiring the need to be theologically significant — is the shape of the love that Acts describes from beginning to end. Sometimes the most important thing you can do for someone is build them a fire in the rain.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28

Paul Heals the Sick on Malta

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28
What Is Happening Here

There is an estate nearby belonging to Publius the chief official of the island. He welcomes the survivors to his home and shows generous hospitality for three days. His father is sick in bed with fever and dysentery. Paul goes in to see him and after praying places his hands on him and heals him. When this happens the rest of the sick on the island come and are cured. The people honor them in many ways and when they are ready to sail give them everything they need for the journey.

What You Might Not Know

Publius the chief official — the Greek title protos meaning first man is the exact title found in inscriptions discovered on Malta referring to the senior Roman official on the island. This specific title used only here in Acts and confirmed by Maltese inscriptions is another small but significant marker of Luke's historical accuracy. He used the correct local title for the correct local official. These kinds of details are not invented by people writing fiction or embellishing stories — they come from people who were present and paid attention.

Fever and dysentery — Luke the physician specifies the diagnosis. The combination of fever and dysentery suggests a bacterial infection consistent with what ancient medical writers described as Malta fever — now known as brucellosis, a disease contracted from unpasteurized goat's milk that was endemic on Malta. The specific medical description is the kind of detail a physician observer would note. Luke was not just a travel companion. He was watching the medical situations around him with professional attention.

After praying placed his hands on him and healed him — Paul's method on Malta was the same method used throughout Acts — prayer and the laying on of hands. He did not claim his own power. He prayed first. The acknowledgment of dependence on God preceded the act of healing. The sequence — prayer then action — is consistent across every healing narrative in Acts. The healers in Acts are consistently people who know they are not the source of what they are doing.

They gave us everything we needed for the journey — the generosity of the Maltese community toward the survivors extended to practical provision for the continuation of the journey. The people who had shown up cold and empty-handed from a shipwreck left three months later with everything necessary for the voyage to Rome. The island that had received them as strangers sent them off as honored guests. The three months on Malta were not an interruption of the journey to Rome. They were the provision for it.

Why It Still Matters

Paul healed the father of the man who had shown him hospitality. Publius had welcomed the survivors generously without knowing who they were or what they could do. The healing of his father was not a payment for the hospitality — it was a gift given to someone who had given first without expecting anything. That pattern — generosity received and generosity returned, hospitality honored with healing, kindness meeting kindness — is the texture of the community that forms around the Gospel wherever it goes. Malta had received strangers. The strangers left behind a healed island. Neither side had calculated the exchange. Both sides gave what they had.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28

The Voyage to Rome

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28
What Is Happening Here

After three months they put out to sea in an Alexandrian ship with the figurehead of the twin gods Castor and Pollux that had wintered at the island. They put in at Syracuse and stay three days. From there they sail to Rhegium and the next day a south wind comes up and the following day they reach Puteoli where they find brothers and sisters who invite them to spend a week with them. And so they come to Rome. The brothers and sisters there have heard they are coming and travel as far as the Forum of Appius and the Three Taverns to meet them. At the sight of these people Paul thanks God and is encouraged. When they get to Rome Paul is allowed to live by himself with a soldier to guard him.

What You Might Not Know

The figurehead of the twin gods Castor and Pollux — the Dioscuri as they were called were the patron gods of sailors and navigation in the Greco-Roman world. A ship bearing their image was considered especially favored for safe passage. The detail is historically accurate — Alexandrian grain ships did carry divine figureheads. Luke records this without comment or theological elaboration. The ship that carried Paul to Rome was decorated with pagan deities. The Gospel has always traveled in vessels that carry the marks of the world it is moving through. The figurehead did not determine the cargo.

Brothers and sisters in Puteoli — there was already a Christian community in the major port city near Naples before Paul arrived. The Gospel had reached Italy independently of Paul. The week Paul spent with believers in Puteoli before the final leg to Rome was a week of community and preparation — the prisoner receiving care from the community he was about to join in Rome. Someone had taken the Gospel to Puteoli before Paul ever sailed from Caesarea. The advance of the Gospel in Acts was not solely through Paul's work.

They traveled as far as the Forum of Appius and the Three Taverns to meet us — the Roman believers who came out to meet Paul traveled significant distances along the Appian Way — the main road into Rome from the south. The Forum of Appius was about forty-three miles from Rome. The Three Taverns was about thirty-three miles. Some came further than others but the point is that the Roman church came out to welcome Paul before he reached the city. They were not waiting passively. They mobilized and walked out to meet him.

Paul thanked God and was encouraged — the Greek word tharsen means to take courage or be heartened. The same word the Lord had spoken to Paul in the Jerusalem barracks — take courage — is now fulfilled by the sight of believers coming to meet him on the Appian Way. Paul had traveled thousands of miles survived beatings imprisonments a shipwreck and two years of unjust detention to reach this moment. When he saw the Roman believers coming down the road to meet him he thanked God and was encouraged. The journey was almost over. The community he was about to join was real and present and coming to meet him.

Why It Still Matters

At the sight of these people Paul thanked God and was encouraged. The encouragement Paul received came from the sight of people — not from a vision or a promise or a theological insight. From people walking down a road to meet him. The community of faith made visible in the physical presence of people who had heard he was coming and came out to welcome him was what encouraged the apostle at the end of his long journey. Theology sustains. Community enacts what theology describes. The Roman believers walking down the Appian Way were the embodiment of the Gospel's power to create community across distance and culture and they were exactly what Paul needed to see at that moment.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28

Paul Meets the Jewish Leaders in Rome

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28
What Is Happening Here

Three days after arriving Paul calls together the local Jewish leaders. He tells them he has done nothing against his people or the customs of his ancestors and was arrested in Jerusalem and handed over to the Romans. They examined him and wanted to release him because he was not guilty of any crime deserving death. The Jews objected so he was compelled to appeal to Caesar — not because he had any charge to bring against his own people. That is why he has asked to see them. It is because of the hope of Israel that he is bound with this chain. They reply that they have received no letters from Judea about him and none of their people have reported anything bad. But they want to hear his views because they know that people everywhere are talking against this sect.

What You Might Not Know

Three days after arriving Paul called together the Jewish leaders — Paul's first action in Rome was the same as his first action in every city he had ever visited. He went to the Jewish community. The Gospel to the Jew first. After years of beatings and imprisonments and two years of unjust detention in Caesarea and a shipwreck Paul arrived in Rome and immediately sought out the synagogue community. The pattern that had been established in Damascus continued unchanged in the capital of the empire. Some things about Paul never changed regardless of what had happened to him.

We haven't received any letters from Judea concerning you — the Jerusalem religious establishment had not sent word to the Roman Jewish community about Paul. Whether this was because they expected the case to be resolved before Paul ever reached Rome or because they had decided to let the Roman legal process handle it or because communication was slow is not stated. The absence of prior contact meant the Roman Jews came to the meeting with open minds rather than with inherited prejudice. Paul had a genuine opportunity to present his case without the weight of Jerusalem's opposition preceding him.

People everywhere are talking against this sect — the Roman Jewish community's description of the Way as a sect being spoken against everywhere was accurate.
