Paul's mission had generated controversy in every city he had visited. The reputation of the movement had traveled faster than Paul himself. Rome already knew about the Way even if Roman Jews had not formed a definitive view of it. They came to Paul's meeting curious rather than hostile — which was more than Paul had received in most cities.

It is because of the hope of Israel that I am bound with this chain — Paul's summary of why he is imprisoned is the same framing he had used before Agrippa. Not because of a crime. Because of the hope of Israel. The chain on his wrist was the consequence of proclaiming that Israel's ancient hope had been fulfilled in Jesus. He was not a criminal. He was a man imprisoned for believing what his people had always hoped for. That framing — the Gospel as the fulfillment of Jewish hope rather than its abandonment — was Paul's consistent message from his first sermon in Pisidian Antioch to this final meeting with Roman Jews.

Why It Still Matters

I didn't do this because I had any charge to bring against my own people. Paul's care for the Jewish people never wavered even after years of opposition from Jewish communities across the Roman world. He appealed to Caesar not out of hostility to his own people but to protect himself from assassination. He arrived in Rome and immediately sought out the Jewish community. His letter to the Romans — written before his arrival — expresses the depth of his anguish over Israel's response to the Gospel and his conviction that God had not abandoned his people. The man the Jerusalem establishment had tried to kill for years called the Roman Jews brothers when he met them. The love was not performance. It was consistent with everything Acts shows about who Paul was.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28

Paul Preaches in Rome

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28
What Is Happening Here

They arrange to meet Paul on a certain day and come in even larger numbers to the place where he is staying. From morning until evening he explains about the kingdom of God and tries to persuade them about Jesus from the Law of Moses and the Prophets. Some are convinced by what he said but others will not believe. They disagree among themselves and begin to leave after Paul makes this final statement — the Holy Spirit spoke the truth to their ancestors through Isaiah the prophet. Go to this people and say you will be ever hearing but never understanding you will be ever seeing but never perceiving. For this people's heart has grown hard — they barely hear with their ears and they have closed their eyes. Otherwise they might see with their eyes hear with their ears understand with their hearts and turn to me and I would heal them. So Paul wants them to know that God's salvation has been sent to the Gentiles and they will listen.

What You Might Not Know

From morning until evening — Paul's final recorded teaching session in Acts lasted an entire day. He explained the kingdom of God and argued from the Law and the Prophets about Jesus from sunrise to sunset. This was not a brief presentation or a summary. It was a full day of sustained theological engagement with the Roman Jewish community. The same intensity Paul had brought to every previous encounter with Jewish communities — the three Sabbaths in Thessalonica the three months in the Ephesian synagogue — he brought to his final recorded teaching day in Rome.

Some were convinced but others would not believe — the divided response that had followed Paul's preaching in every city followed it in Rome. Some believed. Some did not. The Gospel always divides. It does not produce uniform acceptance or uniform rejection. The division in the Roman Jewish community was the same division that had appeared in Pisidian Antioch Corinth Athens Thessalonica and every other place Paul had preached. Rome was not different. The Gospel's effect on the Roman Jews was consistent with its effect everywhere.

The Isaiah quotation — Isaiah 6 was the passage describing Isaiah's commissioning vision where God told him to go to a people who would hear without understanding and see without perceiving. Jesus had quoted the same passage in the Synoptic Gospels to explain why he spoke in parables. Paul now quotes it as his final word to the Roman Jewish community that has divided over his message. The pattern of Israel's resistance to the prophets — which Stephen had traced in Chapter 7 — was being named one final time. The Spirit had spoken through Isaiah about exactly this. The resistance was not a surprise to God. It had been foreseen and described centuries before it happened.

God's salvation has been sent to the Gentiles and they will listen — Paul's final declaration to the Roman Jews echoes his declaration in Pisidian Antioch when the synagogue there rejected the message. We are turning to the Gentiles. The pattern that had begun in Chapter 13 was completed in Chapter 28. The Gospel offered to Israel first, partially rejected, turned toward the Gentiles who received it — this pattern ran through Acts from the beginning. Paul's final words to the Roman Jews were the final instance of the pattern that had shaped his entire ministry.

Why It Still Matters

Some were convinced by what he said but others would not believe. The final result of Paul's preaching in Rome was the same as the first result of Peter's preaching in Jerusalem — a divided response, some believing and some not. The book that began with three thousand believing on Pentecost ends with a room of Roman Jews divided over what they heard from a prisoner under house arrest. Acts does not end with a triumph. It ends with the same honest picture it has maintained throughout — the Gospel proclaimed faithfully, received by some, rejected by others, and continuing regardless. The division was not a failure. It was the reality of what genuine proclamation always produces when genuine freedom is honored.

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28

Paul in Rome — Two Years

◀ 📖 Return to Acts Chapter 28
What Is Happening Here

For two whole years Paul stayed there in his own rented house and welcomed all who came to see him. He proclaimed the kingdom of God and taught about the Lord Jesus the Messiah with complete boldness and without any hindrance.

What You Might Not Know

Two whole years — Acts ends without telling us what happened at the end of the two years. Did Paul's case come to trial? Was he released? Was he executed? Early church tradition holds that Paul was released after the two years, continued his ministry, and was eventually executed under Nero around 64-67 AD. Some scholars believe Paul wrote the Pastoral Letters — 1 and 2 Timothy and Titus — during a period of freedom between the two Roman imprisonments. The book of Acts does not resolve Paul's legal situation because Luke's purpose was not to write a biography with a complete ending. He was writing a theological history of the Gospel's advance from Jerusalem to Rome and that advance was complete.

His own rented house — Paul was under house arrest rather than imprisoned. He could receive visitors could teach and could presumably correspond. The letters from this imprisonment — possibly Ephesians Philippians Colossians and Philemon — show a man fully engaged in theological reflection and pastoral care for the communities he could not visit. The chains on his wrist did not prevent the word from going out. If anything the enforced residence in one place concentrated Paul's teaching ministry in a way that his constant travel had not.

Welcomed all who came to see him — Paul's open house in Rome was accessible to everyone. Jews Greeks Romans soldiers officials curious visitors community members — all who came were welcomed. The Gospel had arrived at the center of the Roman world and it was available to whoever showed up at Paul's door. The prisoner whose arrival in Rome had been prepared by years of danger was now the host of an open invitation to hear the kingdom of God proclaimed in the capital of the empire.

With complete boldness and without any hindrance — the two final words of Acts in Greek are akolutos — unhindered or without restriction. The word that ends Acts is the word that describes the Gospel's condition after everything the book has narrated — the opposition the beatings the imprisonments the riots the shipwrecks. After all of it the Gospel arrived in Rome and was proclaimed without hindrance. The powers of the world had tried to hinder it. They had failed. The last word of Acts is the word for their failure and the Gospel's freedom.

Why It Still Matters

He proclaimed the kingdom of God and taught about the Lord Jesus the Messiah with complete boldness and without any hindrance. Acts ends not with Paul's death or release or legal resolution but with the Gospel being proclaimed freely in Rome. The book that began with Jesus commanding his disciples to be witnesses to the ends of the earth ends with his apostle in the capital of the world teaching everyone who will come to his door. Jerusalem to Rome. The Twelve to Paul. The Jewish Messiah to all the nations. The promise of Acts 1:8 kept in Acts 28:31. The word of God cannot be chained. The last word of the book is the proof of it. Unhindered. The Gospel arrived. The Gospel was proclaimed. The Gospel continued. It still does.
